THE RED PAGE.
Healthy Australian History.
GB. BARTON did well when he showed
in his part of the State History of New
• South Wales that convictism was but
an episode in the making of our nation ;
and that it was not exceptional. America suffered
more from the same cause in her early years.
Larger numbers of the scum of Britain, sturdy
beggars, masterless men, wastrels, rogues, and
malefactors, were sent over, to the Plantations of
America than ever reached Australian shores. It
may come to another to show that in Australia, even
as America, the true begilinings of national 1 ife were,
apart from, though co-incident with, the absorption
of that vomit. Free pioneers laid the foundation
of all that endures, and healthy growths of their
planting make the people and the institutions
which are. Healthy Australian history can only
be written front*such a standpoint as this.
When did our commerce first begin ? When
our agricultural and pastoral settlement, our
manufactures, our arts, our religious, educational,
political systems? By whom were they estab-
lished and by whom continued? It is answers to
such, questions as these which make up history,
and if these answers can be rendered graphic and
impressive, then is such history written as will be
easily learned and never forgotten.
The elements even in Australia are not always
unpicturesque, or sordid;-or tame. We have had
enough, perhaps, of Captain Cook, and Phillip is
overdone for ever in Sydney Palace Gardens.
Unhappy Flinders made his own record; the
little life of Lass went out in obscurity. And,
apart from the narrative of exploratory enter-
prise, the record of the first decade of .settlement
might well, in a history of the making of Aus-
tralia, be summarised in one page. But when did
men begin to fight for natural human rights, and
to settle in natural human hope? From those
dates the author of healthy history must make his
beginnings, and their development must be
his theme. They are not hard to find even
in the days when Sydney was “ gaol and barracks
mostly.” Within a decade of Phillip’s arrival
our first struggles begin, whose issues are of
more importance than all of bad or good that came
out of convictism. A little band of free settlers
fighting for what we consider rudimentary rights ;
a banditti convict-guard playing Pretorian busi-
ness over again to a gutter tune. One English
sailor Governor wishing himself in any pain of
death or hell rather than New South Wales ;
another content to draw his pay and let things
drift. Then Bligh, the fighting man, roaring out
to this miscreant and that, “You tremendous
blackguard ! ”, trying hard to do right and set the
free life of the colony on its infant feet, but finally
snared by the conspirators, and absolutely got rid
of, to Australians great and enduring loss. We
have scores of tales of that date, through which
the cat hisses and the fetters clank, but even in
“history” have we any fair account of the true
nation-makers of that time ?
Another foundation-stone was laid a few years
before the “Rebellion.” It was of Australia’s
commerce, the actual beginning of the business
which makes all the busy life of all our ports. It
was the battle of Robert Campbell, merchant.
He loaded up the Lady Barlow with Australian
produce, seal-skins, whale oil, and “tall spars
suitable for frigates’ masts,” sent her across to
London river, and awaited his account sales.
Instead, came a tale of the effective objections
raised by old John Gampany, and the sale of the
cargo in a foreign port. It was not deemed well
to encourage Australian commerce “lest the
people build navies, and become pirates.” (Now
it is not deemed well to allow an Australian navy
lest it lead to independence.) Yet Robert Camp
bell won in the end, and where is the story of
that fight and that winning ? It should be in
all our school readers.
In the story of John Macarthur and his sheep
(with King George at last cut out) there is also
much to make healthy history. Came it ever to
another man, poor and unaided, to oradle the sires
and the dams of the stock of a continent ? This
did John Macarthur, with much more , not quite so
worthy. And who, at that date, gave us the
orange and the vine, strangely neglecting the
olive?
Of Macquarie there is too much. Macquarie
put back the date of a free Australia full five-and-
twenty years. The histories we have tell us more
about the Rum Hospital than that work of retarda-
tion and degradation. Old Dr. Lang got Mac-
quarie’s measure in the “ Cimex Macquariensis ”
story. Gordon M‘Qrae hit the .humor of the tune
in his unpublished Comic History of Australia.
“ Ladies,” says the usher, to the mob of emanci-
pist females hustling at the door of the presence
chamber, • ‘ let me suggest that you go in, in the
order of your convictions'. ”
With Sir Thomas Brisbane the burly person-
ality of Dr. Lang begins to move influentially, de-
termined from the outset to justify his Australian
boast, “ Quorum pars inagna fui.” And, indeed,
a history of Australia which belittles or neglects,
him must be inaccurate or inadequate. Follows
that noble gentlewoman, Caroline Chisholm, who
should loom large in Australian history, as does
Florence Nightingale in that of England
ing in the good cause whose history is lost in the
sloughs. Sir Richard Bourke, facing the east
from the Sydney Domain gates to-day as he faced
all the inland west ‘in his time, resolute, just,
merciful. Sir George Gipps, never to be despised
however cordially detested. And then, to be
marked with a white stone for ever, the fight for
cleanly national life, when the war-ships on the
harbor trained their .guns on the anti-transporta-
tionists assembled in Mort’s store, and the
last of the convict ships at length shook out her
sails and bore her accursed 'Human freightage
away to the far west.
Quickly then came the fight for the Constitu-
tion. A capable historian could make that story
glow and burn. And what have we lost through
the untold tqle of the invasion of the diggings ?
It never has been told. Poor old Withers’ history
of Ballarat is a tame chronicle ; and Bendigo,
save in the Melbourne press of the time, has no
record. Yet think of Bendigo in the days when
...young Robert Cecil got off the coach,and mounted
the dogcart beside young Warden Pan ton, when
those men of all nations, and all armed, faced the
young warden, resolute but reasonable, at the
Camp, and demanded redress of wrongs which
none but men “blind as a fool’s heart” would
have imposed. Hill End and Lambing Flat would
furnish chapters succeeding for that story, which
still, from Mt. Morgan to Broken Hill and Broken
Hill to Kalgoorlie, goes on.
The settlement of the coast, the too rash occupa-
tion of the West, are not barren of noble interest
nor untinged of romance. The shearers’ wars, to
result eventually in the marvellous amelioration
of all the conditions of the life of the bush-workers,
are healthy, if considered in healthy mood ; the
bush-worker himself, the swagman, is a character-
istic and passing figure on the world’s face.
Federal accomplishments might well close a his-
tory of Australia in the present, and, though
Federal literature is so far turgid or dry, there has
been a healthy undercurrent of feeling amongst all
the masses of the people, and flashes of well-ex-
pressed noble emotion which, when things get in
proper focus, will illuminate the whole.
Also, there is in the present, destined, if our
race is true to its traditions, and its boasts, to
characterise this or the next decade—the artesian
water.
I have enumerated here but a few facts, and
accomplishments, mountain heads along the track
we have travelled, which ought to be fixed and
kept prominently in view. The mist, and the
mire below does not matter so much, is better, a
good deal of it, out of sight and forgotten. There
are others, doubtless, which will recur to many
minds, notably exploration with its endurance
and heroism, all details of which may be found, in'
Ernest Favene’s book. And the long battle with
the Blue Mountains, which Kendall has rescued
from oblivion. Also there are personalities,
heroic and worthy, which, in a history started on
a right foundation and with honest patriotic in-
tent, would drop into their natural places and
shine. But that this may be, it will be essential to
begin on things that have made, not marred our
history ; to seek out the true founders, the real
nation-makers, however humble or obscure, and
trace the thread of their effort down through all
the tangled web of adverse circumstances, until,
at length, they unite and form themselves the
web, which it is for us and those who follow us, to
weave true and roll out before the world’s face,
honest-wrought and clean, with the unmistake-
able colour of liberty, unbesmirched of humbug,
cant or fraud.
F. M.
The Literary Woman—and Man.
Creve Roe writes :
From the women-writers who have been discuss-
ing, in your columns, the Literary Woman and her
aspect towards the Literary Man, I gather that the
Literary Woman who wishes to make a passionate
thumb-mark on the milestones of life should, be
either not married at all, and evoke her
cryptogamic caricatures of men out of her own
virgin mind, or married to a disreputable husband,
who will be a living domestic model for the
villains in her stories. They also say, though
they differ slightly in detail, that the Literary
Man is rarely a fit mate for the Literary Woman.
Instances to the contrary will, of course, occVir to
any reader, but they are exceptions to the rule.
Both seem to have in their minds the picture of
an ideal husband. He must be clever in some
large, manly way, in which there is money to be
made, and fame amongst other men. He must
regard his wife with her genius as a gift of
Heaven to him, and be proud to be permitted to
kiss her ink-stained fingers when she has finished
the last verse of her poem, or the latest chapter of
her story. And if she should condescend to read
her work to him—as Moliere used to read his
comedies to his cook—dm must have sufficient
intelligence to express subdued, but not frenzied,
appreciation. He must be decent and presentable
on State occasions, and know enough of the
convenances of society not to fall over a chair when
he enters a drawing-room, or to mistake the
waiter for his Eitcellency the Governor. He
must drink a little, in a gentlemanly way, but
never drink so much a 3 would cause him
to drag his wife out of a quiet alcove
in whiah she might be talking earnestly
upon questions of the heart with some
brother writer. He must let her have her own
way, and if—through some grotesque accident —
they should happen to have children, he must look
after them and not allow them to worry her. The
children of her brain are more to her than the
children of her marriage, and her husband should
be aware of the fact. And when some cold-
blooded reptiles of editors return her manuscripts
he should console her, and say that it was all low
jealousy on their part, and take her out to dinner
and be proud of her generally.
The Literary Man is a horse of another colour.
I have known personally, or have been corres-
ponding intimately with, every Australian
Literary Man worth his salt during the last 20
years. Not one of them has had a Literary
Woman as a wife. And yet a Literary Man —I
object to the phrase, but let it go—has also his
ideals. He would like to marry a woman who
would be to him not only-a good comrade, but a
good critic as well. He would show her what he
had written, and if she said it was poor he would
smile with politeness and put his hat upon his head
and go out to read it to another woman with some
brains. It is the Other Woman that is the
salvation of the Literary Man. Just imagine :
He leaves his o\yn house, in which everything is
upside-down, and there is comfort nowhere, to
enter a cool drawing-room full of sweet quiet and
flowers. A lady is there with white unruffled
lace around her neck, and a robe of laburnum silk,
showing beneath its lower folds an under-robe of
the color of red wine. And there is a piano. And
she plays to him, plays the airs he loves. And
she tells him that she thinks the last poem he
wrote was too beautiful to be printed. And he
pretends to believe her. And she pretends to
believe that he believes her. And they are the
best comrades in the world. That is enough for
the Literary Man. His wife is a charming little
woman (wives of Literary men rarely run
to size) and understands him, and keeps
his creditors at bay when he has a
headache, and makes soup f®r him when he
is sick, and when necessity arises eats dripping
upon her bread instead of butter, and affects to
prefer the dripping, and says that the children
must be satisfied with bread and treacle for their
breakfast because their Father requires Beer, and
many similar things. But the Other Woman sits
in the melodious dusk and plays the piano.
Helen Jerome on the literary woman :
I differ with Mrs Lance Rawson as to the attraction
existing 1 between literary men and women. A literary
man rarely marries a literary woman. Look around us in
our own little centre ! Is it not the exception rather than
the rule to see two pen people of different sexes dipping
out of the same inkpot ? The fact is, the literary man
requires comfort, bodily comfort, from his helpmate. He
doesn’t want mental food in his home ; he wants a good
bracing dinner and a peaceful fireside, and no
“moods” from the wife of his bosom. Being the only
literary part of the concern, he has a monopoly of the
“ mood ” department. And she is a wise woman who
realises that. In the rare cas?s where literary men do
marry literary women there is a gradual dissatisfaction
between both parties—all owing to the “ mood question
again. Perpetual mental concentration does tend to
make one “ cranky,” especially a nervous woman, whose
soul is dragged two ways. It is always wiser for such
a woman to marry a healthy minded man, fairly common-
place, but always loving her, That is her only chance.
To come to the question which Frank R. plaintively
remarks “ has vanished from present day fiction ” —the
baby question ! The woman-poet writes much and many
“ baby-verses,” until her own baby arrives. Then she is
silent on that subject—only because she feels so intensely
the joy of motherhood, the poetry of babyhood, that ex-
pression becomes impossible. This applies to woman-
writers on all heart-questions. The world may banish
that widely established idea that she sneaks in her own
heart-aches between the lines. She doesn’t! It is the
one subject on which she is silent; perhaps not so much
from choice as from necessity.
Re “Refractory Rhymes,” E. Wilson Dobbs :
During the early ’6o’s many English papers—including
the staid Athenceum—fr&ve themselves over to the —shall
I say—frivolity of publishing such like tours deforce. In
the pages of your regretted “ Bookfellow,” and in “The
Red Page ” which also cultivates faculty for
“ regular and rhymed rhythms ” (to use Francis Adams’
alliterative phrase), similar references have been
’found from time to time—as in the'par. 11/10/’O2.
The only other word put forward, as rhyming with
“chimney,” that I know of, occurs in “The Mfelcome
Guest,” for Nov. 9th, 1861 :
“ Sir, I hope it’s no crime
To send you the rhyme,
Though you say there none is for chimney :
To prove it’s not true,
As stated by you.
Know this, sir, I’ve found it in Rhymney.”
Unfortunately, however, this reference to some mines in
Wales, although seemingly rhyming, does not really, as
the Welsh folk pronounce it Rhumney.
Similarly the far-fetched rhymes (so-called) for
“ month,” such as the Indian exotic “ Grunth” (the sacred
book of the Sikhs), and the word “ Lonth ” (meaning lip),
when scientifically translettered are quite different in
sound-value from “ month.” For the “th ” of the Indian
words is dental, whereas the Erlglish “th ” is lingual,
and there are further phonetic divergencies. Other well-
known inventions were to make an ordinal out of a car-
dinal numeral, and talk about “ raising n to the (x plus 1)
th,” or “ raising binomials to the(n plus 1) th,” attributed
to Drs. Donaldson and Whewell. Prof. Skeat also brought
into service Thackeray's Phil Fogarty of “the fighting
onety-oneth,” and there are the lisping versions, “ d unth,”
“ onth,” and “ wunth ” (for “ dunce ” and “ once ”) also
suggested. Another way is by elision, like Christina
Rossetti, did in her “ Sing Song ” :
“ How many weeks in a month?
Four as the swift moon runn’th ; ”
to which could be added “ shunn’th,” “ sptinn’th,"
“ stunn’th,” and so on, even to “ gerund’th,” and finally
“ none’th." Naturally there are a great number of
English words rhymeless—albeit, Walker has a dictionary
supplying so-called rhymes for a number of these, and
“allowable rhymes” for many more. But as he, for
instance, makes “avenge” rhyme with “revenge,”
he stultifies all his supposed rules. The following
are a few other simple English words for which no
satisfactory rhymes can be found (proper names and
foreign words barred) : bilge, coif, oulm, cusp, fugue,
gulf, have, oblige, orange, scarce, silver, and widow. As
to “orange”—a hill near Abergavemy, “The Blorenge,”
and the name of “ Gorringe.” in Sussex, have been sub-
mitted, but are inadmissible, so are “door-hinge” and
“more range.” The word “porringer”—likewise con-
sidered rhymeless—has been also used in some Jacobean
lines, in this connection :
“ The second James a daughter had,
Too fine to lick a porringer ;
He sought for her a noble lad
And gave the Prince of Orange, her."
And there are, of course, others.
Le Roux, a French critic thus discusses Zola :
Zola is a representative of Latin culture, and for that
very reason he is not a representative of French culture.
■Neither is he a Frenchman in race or spirit. He is one
of those Mediterraneans by descent whose homes are in
the seaports—Naples, Marseilles, Barcelona —merchants
and artists, for many generations in touch with various
cultures. In 1870, when the very children of France
served their country, Zola, then a young man, felt no
desire to battle for her.
Men are no more the puppets either of heredity, as Zola
would have us believe ; or of Providence, as Bossuet tries
to show. Man is neither angel nor beast.
Zola is not immoral. He is gross, but the grossness of
Zola is never enticing. He is no more immoral than filth
is immoral in itself.
Zola does not know women. He has the attitude of an
anchorite who fears them, and revenges himself upon
them. Zola does not recognise that love is a means not
an end. The type of woman portrayed in “Nana” he
knew only at second-hand, from the words of other men,
but this work and “La Debacle ” are the most popular in
America. It is unfair to place these books in the hands
of young Americans of both sexes, as has been done, and
let them form a false judgment of France.
Zola is'not an observer of life, but he is a great poet in
the sense that the prophets #f ancient Israel were great
poets, seeing evil and crying out against it mightily.
He sees phantoms, not realities.
Dr. Douglas Hyde, George Moore, and W. B.
Yeats are protesting in England at the desecra-
tion of what is popularly but incorrectly called
the Hill of Tara. Laborers dig and shovel among
the mounds without any supervision, at t ie insti-
gation of a sect which believes the English to be
descended from the Ten Lost Tribe* (the alleged
Lost Tribes) and who expect to find the Ark of the
Covenant under Ireland’s historic hill.
“The Misdemeanors of Nancy” (Angus and
Robertson, Melbourne and Sydney) is a daintily-
printed series of short stories by Eleanor Hoyt,
one of th.e best-known of American writers of
genre of to-day. With a graceful and dainty
style, Eleanor Hoyt contrives to invest a vetry
slight theme with much charm and interest.
Effective binding and well-printed plates, from
drawings by Penrhyn Staid a ws, help the volume
considerably. A. & R.’ are the publishers for
Australia.
S.T.S.: Whatever is “Matchbox” up to making
“ Lazarus and Dives ” rhyme with honored
wives” (B. 4/10/’02)? These classical mantraps
were prettily set forth, years ago, in the Dublin
University magazine:
Back to youth I seem to glide, as
I recall those peaceful scenes,
When we quoted Thucydides,
Or recited Demosthenes.
Sobbing slow, like summer tides
Flow thy verses, Euripides !
Lavington Grlyde, a scholar of the old times (and
one of the best Treasurers S.A. ever had), used to
tell of a lecturer who went astray and was cor-
rected thus:
When next you lecture, if you please,
Don’t call the man Aristides.
The name, you know, should rhyme with fiaes,
So please to call it Aristides.
There was a country Literary Society which was
delighted with this satire, and asked for a copy.
But later on they were discovered to be repeating
it with fides as a monosyllable (to rhyme with
“slides”), and Aris tides to match.

Love Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Cup of Bliss.
The reddest rose, the bluest violet,
Take them and bray them in a golden jar;
Drip in the clearest dewdr.ops, nor forget
Some faintest scent from where old shadows are,
Nor the night-laughter where the creek is loud,
Nor that far voice when all the silence grieves ;
Stir these with motion of the softest cloud,
Of winds that run along the sunny leaves,
Then, last, add glances of the moonlit stream,
Pink tremblings from the edges of the dawn,
A dash of rapture only youth dare dream,
And the dear pang it leaves when it is gone.
Pour now, and drink. Is it the cup of bliss ?
Thou canst not, then, remember Love’s first kiss.
N.s.w.
MAGGIE SULLIVAN.
Meeting.
What sorrow is in life, to know that you
Steal by these dim ways when the dusk has waned
Into a night reluctant and profaned;
A creature of night, whom earlier I knew,
So fair that all your life’s way seemed astrew
With lilies, and your Heaven almost attained.
By what fierce mood of God was it ordained,
Poor heart, that you were thus to sin and rue ?
Ah me ! the hint and challenge of your eyes;
Vague unremembered kisses that consume
Your soul in nights calamitous; and the gloom
Of mirth which stirs the sullen pain that lies
Within your heart, so lorn and dispossessed.
Nay, do not weep—yet weep, for that were Dost.
THOROLD DALRYMPLE.
A Love Song.
I love her eyes,
Her glorious haunting eyes,
That like twin stars from dusky western skies
Gaze wistfully into the breaking dawn
Of womanhood and all the years unknown.
Alas, those eyes!
I love her hands,
Her deft and busy hands,
Whose dainty fingers weave immortal bands
Of willing thraldom whereso’er they twine,
With witching touch around this heart of mine.
Those gentle hands!
I love her lips,
Her fragrant dewy lips,
Red buds, whence Love his honeyed virtue sips,
That tremble, pout and sigh, yet dare not frame
The simple “Yes” for very maiden shame.—
Ah me ! those lips !
But Love is vain,
Regrets are doubly vain,
The reachless stars of morning mock my pain
The hands elude me. Her sweet lips will never
To mine respond. Yet must I love for ever,
Though Love is vain ?
A.
The Honeymoon.
Did it last but a month? it was ours for that
while;
To be lost with a frown, to be gained with a smile.
Such a bower of roses, such gardens of laughter,
And such moonlight and starlight, with love to
come after.
White walls shutting out all the world and its
prying,
And outside the waste and the desert winds
sighing.
If by dawning the lean wolves crept close to the
gate,
Did we hear them or heed them —forerunners of
Fate?
Not we ! Just one strong arm drew closer ; that’s
all!
We were safe in the sweep of that magical wall;
And if the night breezes blew cold o’er our bliss,
Why, we changed their intent to the warmth of a
kiss.
Oh, soft arms ! A month you were tender and
clinging—
A month of the rose and the nightingale’s singing.
And now one hand is to the bar of the
gates
In the desert outside. How I wonder what waits!
Q.
M. BURKINSHAW.
Lust and Love.
To-night Lust died of satiety !
A week ago the very same ;
I cannot count his every death,
But only know the hollow pain
Accompanying the aftermath.
And now his dread black corse drags on
Beside my shuddering soul alway ;
And I am doomed, I know full well,
To suffer all its quick decay—
A pain unknown outside of Hell,
And shall he live again ? Ah, no !
Out of my life a great star burns,
And shoots a ray athwart the gate,
That man may pass the day he learns
A Love, white and immaculate.
W.A.
L. L. S.

The Case of
Mrs. B. EAGAR.
(Foe the Redfem Times.)
It is impossible to read the following
statement of the condition of things de-
scribed by Mrs. Bertha Eagar, of No. 145
Walker-street, Redfern, without being
struck by certain phases and facts in her re-
cital. Carrying her memory back eleven
years, Mrs. Eagar said :
“ It is that length of time since I was re-
leased from a complaint of such great ob-
stinacy that there was every reason to think
otru° U nevei ! be the same woman again.
Why I say this is because my sufferings ex-
tended over a period of six months or
more, and half that time I was under the
care of a doctor.”
What complaint was he treating you
tor?” asked our reporter.
“My ailments were as numerous as they
were difficult to cope with, but I am of
opinion that they were occasioned by a dis-
eased state of my liver. Be that as it may,
I know that indigestion was one of the prin-
cipal features of my illness, and I became so
seriously affected by it that before long I
was a completely helpleis invalid. When I
iad been taking medicines prescribed by my
physician til! I knew it was useless to drink
any more I did not know what to do next
:or I was so feeble that I could not walk
across the flom-. It seemed a cruel thing to
throw so much money away, and waste so
much time for nothing, but when people are
id, you know, they will spend their last
penny, it necessary, in buying things which
they expect to do them good. And that is
the very reason why I consider it my duty
to divulge to all mankind the fact that it
was only through using Clements Tonic at
ihe eleventh hour that my awful sufferings
were checked. Through reading a kindly-
disposeu person’s testimonial I started tak-
ing it, and ever since those words appeared
before me I have regarded them with as
much respect as though they had consti-
tuted a message from an angel above. When
, c °me to consider how much agony I en-
dured I am devoutly thankful that the right
”emedy came at last.”

The Bulletin
Registered at the General Post Office, Sydney, for Transmission as a Newspaper.

SATURDAY, OCTOBER 25, 1902.
Prior 6d.
WAITING! J. CALVIN’S TWO POUNDS OF FLESH.
“At a special Cabinet meeting consideration was given to the cases of Selina Sangal and August Tisler, sentenced to death last month for the Dandenong murder. It was decided that
the law should be allowed to take its course in both cases, and Tisler was hanged on Monday. The date of the execution could, of course, not be definitely fixed in the case of Mrs. Sangal,
who has not yet been told that the Executive has confirmed her sentence, and it is unlikely that the news will be broken to her until after her accouchement. - ’—IVews Ttem.


The Bulletin
The National Australian Newspaper.
11 Australia for the Australians.”

SATURDAY, OCTOBER 25, 1902.
A Federal Land Tax.
When Premier Barton has got all his
luggage ashore, including his new title, and
his scheme for doubling the naval contribu-
tion and getting less value for the large
contribution than we formerly did for the
small one, and his arrangement for giving a
preference to the British manufacturer and
getting nothing in return, it might be a fair
thing to direct his attention to a merely
Australian matter. Australia may be a poor
trashy place compared to a country where
there are dukes and coronations and the like,
but still it is the only place we have, and we
must needs make the best of it. Though
the first Federal session is only just finished
the next one is very close at hand, and there
are many urgent matters to be attended to—
matters that may look very small in the
atmosphere of dukes and coronations, but
which loom up rather largely here.
* * * *
To mention one among many. A Federal
tax on land and mortgages on the graduated
principle, with a weighty absentee-tax added,
is a matter of the first importance. Such a
tax is a feature—though often a much-
neglected feature —in every democratic pro-
gramme, but there is little hope of securing
it from the Tory oligarchies which run the
dominant Upper Houses in Tasmania and
Yictoria, or from the Boodle party in
Queensland, or from the great borrowing
party in Westralia. The N.S.W. Labor
party has said nothing for a long, long time
about an absentee tax, or about graduating
the land-tax in order to break up big
estates, though isolated members mention
these matters now and then. And the
awful Jenkins, of South Australia, pro-
poses to suspend the land-tax law for
a long series of years as far as con-
cerns the Ninety Million Acres which he
proposes to job away to a private railway
syndicate ; whereby he foolishly ignores the
fact that the Federal Parliament is no party
to his agreement, and that a power which is
greater than Jenkins has to be reckoned
with in the matter. Taking all these and
many other things into account, the demo-
cratic party should give up any serious
attempt at land-tax agitation in the State
Legislatures, where corruption, or the dead
weight of the plural property vote, or the
opposition of immovable Upper Houses,
nullifies their efforts, and should follow the
line of least resistance. In the Federal
Parliament bricks have no vote, and mortar
is disfranchised, and decomposed vegetable
matter doesn’t run the show.
* • * *
That the Federal Parliament has power to
levy a land and mortgage tax, or any other
form of direct or indirect taxation, there is
no doubt whatever. The Constitution gives
it power to impose any kind of taxation.
There may or there may not have been an
intention on the part of the framers of the
Constitution, or on the part of some of them,
to leave the land-tax as a purely State
impost, but Australia has nothing to do
with any unexpressed intentions on the part
of anybody. The Federal land and mort-
gage tax is not proposed by way of duplicat-
ing existing taxation. It should be so
adjusted as to yield to the State with the
most efficient land-tax at least as much as
the present tax does. When it is imposed
it should be expressly specified that the
amount collected shall be returned to the
States, or, which would be much better,
the States should be relieved of an equal
amount of their present expenditure. Then
it will be for the States to abolish their
existing land-taxes, and, under the circum-
stances, they can be fully trusted to do so.
• * *
The Bulletin’s idea of what a Federal
land-tax should be like is somewhat as
follows ;
Every holder of land of the total unimproved value of
£5OO or less to go absolutely free. From £5OO to £lO,OOO,
the rate to be Id. in the £ ; from £lO,OOO to £20,000, the
rate to be ljd. in the £ ; and an additional Jd. in the £
for every extra £lO,OOO of unimproved value up to 6d. in
the £. Every absentee person or corporation to pay
50 per cent, extra, any corporation (including the
Salvation Army) which has its head office outside
Australia or more than half its capital held outside
Australia, to be deemed an absentee. And when the
land is subject to mortgage, the burden to be divided
between the nominal owner and the mortgagee, in pro-
portion to their interests.
In a previous issue of this paper it was sug-
gested that two years continuous absence
before the date of assessment, or fragmen-
tary absences totalling two years out of the
four immediately preceding assessment,
should constitute absenteeism. Further
consideration suggests that one year’s con-
tinuous absence, or casual absences totalling
one year out of the previous two, should
qualify a landholder or mortgagee for extra
taxation.
• • * *
All over Australia there is good and suffi-
cient reason why the land-tax should be
made a Federal impost. In Queensland
there is a stupendous deficit, and unless a
land-tax is imposed it is difficult to see
how the steady downward slide towards
ruin is to be checked. Also Queensland
is the most deeply and hopelessly pawned
State in the Commonwealth, and has got
the absentee in a worse form than almost
any other. If Queensland could go to ruin
without injuring the rest of the Common-
wealth, then it might be pleaded that
Queensland’s ruin is its own affair, but no
one State can go down to bad disaster and
not do grievous damage to the rest. There-
fore, all Australia has a right to supply
Queensland with extra revenue by means of
the land-tax which its Philp bureaucracy
hasn’t the sense to supply itself ; to help to
stop Queensland’s deficit; to help to crush
the absentee who is draining Queensland’s
life-blood : to break up Queensland’s big
land monopolies ; and, generally, to put a
large and helpless brother on his feet.
• * * •
In New South Wales the Labor party
seems to have abandoned all serious idea of
imposing an absentee-tax, or of graduating
the land-tax so as to break up the huge and
disastrous monopolies of the A.A. Co. and
other companies built on the same principle
as that incubus. The N.S.W. land-tax is
utterly and shamefully undemocratic. It
strikes the rich and the poor almost alike.
It levies no special penalty on the rack-
renting “ squire,” or other elevated loafer ;
or the worse rack-renter who spends his
wealth in England, and thus constitutes
himself a perpetual drain on the State
as well as a loafer; or on the great
land-steal corporations which lock up
the best part of the State against closer
settlement, while the agriculturist is driven
away towards the sunset to battle with an
impossible desert, and to raise hope-
less crops on the hobs of Gehenna.
In the richer and better-watered part of
N.S. W. the large estates are growing instead
of diminishing, and the absentee is an in-
creasing curse and an increasing loss. If the
N.S.W. Labor party won’t look to this evil,
it is time that the Australian. Labor party
did.
* • * ♦
In Yictoria, all the valuable city lands and
the bulk of the best country lands pay no
tax whatever. There is an alleged land-tax
which, provided an estate covers more than
640 acres, and is worth more than £2500,
levies ljd. in the £ on the excess above
£2500. As city lands are not held in 640-
acre blocks, Melbourne, Ballarat, Bendigo,
Geelong, and other towns go free. There is
no absentee-tax, and no serious effort to
break up the large estates which block
settlement. Yictoria is desperately in want of
revenue, but the Bent Government and the
48 Rich Landlords in the Upper House would
sooner see the State bankrupt than levy a
tax on their own gorgeous land monopoly.
Therefore the Federal Parliament should
come to the rescue of a State, which, by
reason of the awful Upper House that
Britain inflicted upon it, cannot come to its
own rescue ; should supply it with much-
needed revenue ; should break up the in-
famous Clarke and Chirnside monopolies
which hinder its progress; and teach the
House of Landlords that it isn’t the 48
deities it has always imagined itself to be.
f £ $ A
Tasmania has a land-tax of in the £.
which, by reason of the many exemptions
and the absurdly low valuations, produces
comparatively little. Tasmania doesn’t tax the
great and somnolent Y.D.L. Co.’s monopoly
to anything like the extent required to make
it take its huge burden off the island and
cease blocking settlement in the north-west.
Such a tax as is outlined above would supply
Tasmania with the revenue it needs to prevent
it sliding down into financial Tophet, and at
the same time it would relieve the small
farmer of some of the taxation he now pays.
It would put an end to the draining of the
State’s wealth by the Y.D.L. Co.’s octopus.
It would throw open for settlement a whole
lot of locked-up land, and thereby bring a
rush of tax-paying population, for in this
driest of all dry Commonwealths every
cultivable inch of well-watered Tasmania
would be occupied if only the obstructions to
settlement were removed. The Lewis
Government, the Upper House, the city
newspapers and the V.D.L. Co. would doubt-
less raise a clamor against the Federal land-
tax, which clamor might sound like public
opinion, but the mass of the people would
almost certainly be with the Commonwealth
Government in the matter.
♦ * * #
As regards South Australia, the proposed
measure would hasten the break-up of that
vast absentee corporation, the South Aus-
tralian Company. Also, it would be the
rising up of a new Pharaoh who knows not
Jenkins. It would end the transcontinental
land-grant railway swindle, for the proposed
90,000,000 acres steal would come under the
heaviest scale of taxation as the biggest kind
of absentee-owned estate, and the great
Jenkins job, whereby an area twice as large
as Victoria is to be given away to a gang of
corrupt company-promoters, would die a
natural death. In Westralia the Federal
land-tax policy would do equally good ser-
vice in breaking up the Hampton Plains
syndicate and other unwholesome jobs and
diseases. In fact, there is no part of Aus-
tralia where it wouldn't do good, though it
would probably do most good to Philp and
Jenkins.
* <* • •
The Federal land-tax idea would, of
course, be denounced as a gross encroach-
ment on “ State Rights,” and the party of
monopoly would cover its opposition with
the ragged cloak of a bogus patriotism—that
patriotism which is the last refuge of
scoundrels. But while the Constitution
gives the Commonwealth absolute power to
levy any and every kind of taxation, there
could be no encroachment on State Rights.
It was never specified in the bond that any
kind of taxation should be exclusively State
taxation. All over Australia the progressive
party realises that a graduated land-tax and
an absentee-tax are essential to the country’s
prosperity—that the big landlord is a curse,
and that the crowd of absentees who draw
immense sums out of this country to spend
abroad are a burden which a poor community
is utterly unable to stand. The absentee
who draws £30,000 a year out of Australia
is equal to a debt of £1,000,000, without the
country having had the use and benefit of
the million. He is a gratuitous injury and a
dead loss. Realising these things, it is for
the progressive party to seek a remedy in
the only Australian Legislature where the
people are absolutely supreme—where there
is no nominee element or brick-and-mortar
conspiracy, and where loam has no vote
and cattle don’t rule. To seek for
reform in the State Legislatures is only a
useless going out of the straight road in
search of obstacles.
The Sectarian Issue in Politics.
“ Wab is an obscene word, never used except by
those who wish for it,” said a famous European
diplomatist, most truly. In heading a leading
article “ Sectarian War”; in quoting with sweet
approval the countenance which G. H. Reid
has seen fit to give to the rascally effort of the
“Protestant Defence League” in N.S.W. to
make religious bigotry the issue of politics; in
giving support to the absurd statements of that
absurd league about Romish organisation and
domination —Sydney Daily Telegraph shows
that it wishes sectarian war in its State. The
game is an old one for the cause which that paper
champions with so much vigor and so little scruple.
The foreign-trade party in Australia has before this
rallied its battalions in the dirty ditch of sectarian-
ism, and fooled the electors into voting for “ the
good old English cause,” which centres around the
-bergs and the -heins and so on of Flinders-
lane, Melbourne, and York-st., Sydney, by con-
juring upaKiama ghost, or the wraith of Rome
Rule. One of the greatest difficulties of the
Australian National cause has ever been to dis-
sociate itself from the idea of R.C. domination, of
which its enemies accused it so artfully; to show
that Protection didn’t mean Popery; and that
independence wasn’t necessarily indfpmdmce.
The importers’ gang and their wretched toolsfhave
always, when in difficulty, made it part or their
tactics to allege that the only alternative to their
domination is priestly domination. Before sniffing
at the trap which the bigotry of zealots, the selfish
wickedness of politicians and of certain newspapers,
have prepared for them now, the N.S.W. people
(and the Australian people too —the revival of sec-
tarianism is not confined to one State) should refresh
their minds with a few wholesome truths —estab-
lished as truths by past experience. When the
sectarian cry is successfully raised, when religion
s made the issue of statesmanship, other ques-
tions sink into insignificance, and religious opinion,
becomes the one test of fitness for public life.
Whichever faction wins, holding office as it does
on a religious cry, it must pander to the feelings of
the sectaries supporting it, must look to the clergy
for guidance or direction, must, in short, rule, to
a large extent, on hieratic principles. A Roman
Catholic faction triumphing would naturally, in-
evitably, be directed in its actions by the R.C.
priesthood. A Protestant party getting into
power,would just as certainly and logically shape
its actions by the wishes of its clerical leaders.
Wfipn the ecclesiastic comes into politics, the
other elements of the community are in
the position of the Arab who, in kind-
ness, allowed his camel to put its head inside
his tent for warmth one cold night ; soon the
tent was full of camel and there was no room for
Arab. Accustomed to be the spiritual shep-
herd of his flock, the priest or parson, given the
political sceptre, wishes to wield it with as
full power as he does his pastoral staff.
• • *
There is a very prevalent misconception that,
in matters of religion, the only section from which
political domination is to be feared is the Roman
Catholic. This idea—how mistaken it is will soon
be shown —had a quite natural genesis. Pro-
testantism has generally been more liberal than -
Roman Catholicism, because it has been protest-
ing. It is constituted authority which has the
chance to be tyrannical, not revolt; and in the ,
history of Europe the constituted authority has
generally been the R.C. church, and the party of
revolt and protest the Protestant sect. But there
is the amplest of evidence that there is nothing in
Prote-tant sectarianism less overbearing, cruel
and debasing than in Roman Catholic sectarian-
ism. When Protestantism has become triumphant,
and has remained under the control of its clergy,
it has been guilty of tyranny as gross, of outrages
as repulsive as can ever be charged against a
Roman Catholic Inquisition. Wherever aChristian
church, it may be broadly stated, has secured con-
trol of the secular affairs of a nation, the result has
been cruelty, intolerance, retrogression. History
reeks with examples. Let two of the most con-
spicuous be cited, yielding precedence to the Rev.
Dill Macky’s church, the Presbyterian.
* * • *
During the 17th century the Government of
Scotland was completely in the hands of t he Presby-
terian clergy. “ The clergy, ” says Buckle, “ were
supreme.” They exercised their supremacy by
stopping all intellectual culture, discouraging all
independent inquiry. Whoever disobeyed the
clergy was excommunicated, lost his property
and was publicly condemned to eternal
damnation. The control of the family of the
private person was assumed by the clergyman.
Spies were appointed to ransack every domestic
hearth. The servants of any suspected religiously-
disaffected person were cited before the Inquisi-
tion of the Protestant clergy to give evidence as
to his habits. To differ from a parson in opinion,
to neglect to salute him, was a crime. His name
was sacred and not to be taken in vain. The
clergy claimed that, by their curses, they could
strike men dead. No parson might be sued with
impunity in the civil courts. All people were
forced to attend at church. Innocent amusements
were denounced as sinful; even filial love was
often held to be a sin. Diseases were not caused
by dirt, but by the anger of God, and it was
blasphemy to seek to ward them off. Cheerfulness
was a crime ; music and dancing heinous wicked-
ness. Those who disobeyed the clergy were
punished with fines, imprisonment, and branding
with hot irons. Some of the “crimes” punished
by the clergy were : to allow shelter to a Catholic,
to shave the beard, and to bathe the body. That
was the state of a country, otherwise civilised,
which fell under the dominion of the Protestant
clergy—the liberal, freedom-of-conscience, tolerant
Protestant clergy.
* * * •
To match it with a Roman Catholic example is
not hard. To take one from the same era, In
the 17th century Spain was putting the coping-
stone on to the infamies of the. Netherlands
persecution and the Spanish Inquisition by the
expulsion of the Moriscoes, whose orthodox
Catholicism was more than doubted. Before
then, desperate efforts had been made to kill out
by persecution these people of Moorish race. One
of the chief efforts of'the Spanish R.C. clergy
(who absolutely ruled the country at the time)
against the cleanly Moriscoes was to order the
destruction of all public and private baths, “as
bathing was a heathenish custom.” Like the
Presbyterian clergy in Scotland, the R.C. clergy
in Spain saw a deadly foe in cleanliness. But
such half measures having failed, the Moriscoes
were finally expelled in a body, after the
Archbishop of Toledo had suggested as a better
alternative that they should all be massacred.
In all, 1,000,000 of these people were driven out
of Spain by the R. C. clergy in the space of a few
months. Of these perhaps the majority perished.
Of one body of 140,000 refugees, 100,000 died.
The unfortunate Moriscoes, on their way across to
Africa in the Spanish transports, were subjected
to all the cruelties which a thoroughly pious and "
priest-ridden race could inflict ; their women
were ravished, the men and children butchered in
droves. The result of this murderous expulsion
was the swift decline of Spain. By the end of
the 17th century the people, who had been pro-
mised prosperity by the clergy so soon as they
had cleared out all heretics and infidels, were
suffering “poverty and wretchedness surpassing
all description.” The whole race was absolutely
starving, and it was not until the French Bourbons
came to the throne that the power of the clergy
was curbed and some amelioration obtained.
* * * •
Ireland’s history in itself gives terrible warning
against clerical domination of any kind. It was
a Pope who first sold Ireland into bondage, and it
was the insolent rapacity of the Roman Catholic
Eowers in England which gave the stimulus tothose
orrible wars of ravage and rapine which were
waged against a brave but disunited people. At a
later stage, the narrow Protestant sectaries of the
Cromwellian period gave the unhappy country a
taste of the other type of clerical tyranny, drench-
ing her fields in blood, murdering men, women
and children and depopulating whole provinces to
sell the inhabitants as slaves in the West Indies.
But why multiply historical instances ? To the
most casual student of the past it must be evident
that whenever and wherever a priesthood or a
parsonry—no matter what its dogma—has secured
control of the State the result has been tyranny,
cruelty, a negation of all that is noble and just in
human nature, an affirmation of all that is san-
guinary and superstitious.
* • * *
It is indeed a logical necessity that it should be
so. Religious teachers, believing firmly that the
dogmas to which they hold are necessary to
eternal salvation—necessary to secure the noblest
happiness and avoid the blackest misery for a
period of time beside which the currency of a
thousand earthly lives is not even as the shadow
of a shadow of a speck in Space, are almost in-
evitably pushed into persecution. A logic true


snough in its steps, absurd only in its grotesque
premises, almost compels them to force by any
means everybody into “ the true fold.” What is
the hanging or burning of a few hundreds by the
side of securing eternal happiness for many thou-
sands? That is the mental attitude of the
theologian, and that he transfers into action if he
is allowed to establish a hierarchy and to seize
the sword of the civil power. And it matters not
one whit whether lie is Protestant or Catholic.
The one hangs you in gloomy silence, the other
roasts you to excellent music. Religious persecu-
tion may seem to be a far remote thing in
these days. But it is only the power
to persecute, not the desire, that slum-
bers. Give the clergy their way, inflame the
minds of the people to religious hatreds,
make the preposition in a creed the political issue,
and soon superstition, bigotry, hatred, persecu-
tion will raise their heads. From hating one
another heartily for God’s sake at the ballot-box,
the people will go on to breaking one another’s
heads for the same reason ; and after that the fires
will be lighted. '
* • • *
The Bulletin, at this crisis in the affairs of the
young Australian Commonwealth, urges the
people to stamp to death the venomous serpent of
sectarianism, to refuse to be led by Bishop or
Bethel-thumper into religious war. It may seem
a small matter to drop “for just this once” the
true issues of politics, and vote on questions, to a
certainty on which the human mind can never
grope. But it is not. Let sectarianism once
establish its noisome rule, and there are possibili-
ties of disaster matching the conditions of Scot-
land and Spain in the 17th century. The sectary
is never content with one hell. Give him his way
and he will make one of the earth. Let us keep
priest and parson in their places.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Coercion, Sedition, and Sundries.
Another violent attack of coercion has fallen
upon Ireland, and the news that an Irish M.P.,
or some other prominent person, has been sent to
gaol for inciting the people to disaffection against
the rack-renter and the absentee English land-
lord, happens along every now and then as a
matter of course. Just now the crime of not
loving the> absentee English landlord is a very
dreadful one in Ireland. This is really the only
present grievance, for the island is singularly free
from crime, as the term is commonly understood.
In proportion to population, England and Scot-
land have, of late, shown a vastly worse record.
It is not the prevalence of murder, assault, theft,
or violence or dishonesty in any form that causes
the British Tory Government to load Ireland
with all manner of extraordinary laws
of repression, to suppress public meetings,
td prosecute newspapers, and generally to rage like
the heathen mentioned in the Scripture. Yet the
insurrectionary Irishmen who are sent to gaol
every day under the special repressive laws of
the British Tory Government only want to do
what Maoriland and South Australia have been
doing for years. They want to get rid of the big
estates and the great absentee landlords ; and the
absentee taxes and graduated land-taxes of King-
ston and Seddon were acts of bitter hostility
directed against the same class that the Irish
Land League objects to. They want to build
up their own industries by a Protective tariff,
though there is no reason to suppose that they
want to direct against England quite so stu-
pendous a hostile tariff as Seddon has done.
And, just like all these States, they want some
measure of local self-government, though the idea
of maintaining a separate army, as these States do,
isn’t specifically mentioned. Strange as it may
seem, Seddon, the recent guest of royalty, has
done, in the very worst form, everything that the
recently-gaoled Irish M’sP. wanted to do, and
more. Even Parnell never attempted against
England quite so much as the loyal groveller
Seddon has actually accomplished. It is one of
the strange kinks of the English mind that what
is loyalty in other places is treason in Ireland ;
and the thing that, done in other parts of the
Empire, makes a man the King’s guest at the
Hotel Cecil, if done in Iceland is liable to make
him the King’s guest for three months at the
Hotel Skilly. The injuries that England did to
Ireland are what England can never forgive. For
instance, this old historical ghost rises from
that standard English work, Green’s “History
of the English People ” :
Every Catholic Irishman, and there were five Irish
Catholics to every Irish Protestant, was treated as a
stranger and a foreigner in his own country. The House
of Lords, the House of Commons, the magistracy, all cor-
porate offices in towns, all ranks in the army, the bench,
the bar, the whole administration of government or
justice were closed against Catholics. The very right of
voting for their representatives in Parliament was denied
them. Few Catholic landowners had been left. .. .
England did her best to annihilate Irish com-
merce and ruin Irish agriculture. Statutes passed
by the jealousy of English landowners forbade the
export of Irish cattle or sheep to English ports. The ex-
port of wool was forbidden, lest it might interfere with
the profits of English woolgrowers. Poverty was thus
added to the curse of misgovernment, and poverty
deepened with the rapid growth of the native population
till famine turned the country into a hell. . . . Soldiers
and yeomanry marched over the country torturing and
scourging the “ croppies ” (as the Irish peasantry were
called, in derision, from their short-cut hair), robbing,
ravishing and murdering. These outrages were sanc-
tioned by the (Protestant.) landowners, who formed the
Irish Parliament, in a Bill of Indemnity.
The race that has done things like that never quite
forgives the race it did them to. All the mean,
narrow animosity of DiLL-MACKY-ism is aroused,
and Dill-Mack Y-ism is a horrible and brutal
virtue when it gets in full blast.

An All-Night Sitting.
N.S.W. Assembly recently raged in financial
debate for 26 hours consecutively, and as a large
proportion of the speakers were hostile to the
Ministry, it might be expected that they would
contrive, in that time, to at least mention what
was wrong with the finances, and how they might
be put right. A patient study of these 26 hours
of squall and condemnation shows that the
Opposition did nothing of the kind. There were
complaints about borrowing in general, about
borrowing for wholly unproductive purposes,
about deficits, and misuse of trust funds, but the
most obvious ways of attempting a remedy never
got a mention. The following, in The Bulletin’s
view, are the principal things that the House
managed, with marvellous ingenuity, to leave
unsaid all those 26 hours:
(1) So long as there is no restriction as to the kind of
works a Treasurer may charge to loans—so long as there
is no law defining what is fairly loan expenditure and
what isn’t —there' will be Chaos.
(2) If it is enacted that all works not directly produc-
tive of income shall be charged to revenue, and a payment
of, say, £2,000,000 a year to a Public Works fund (to be
in charge of trustees) is made a first lien on revenue, and
if the trust funds are put out of the Treasurer’s reach so
that he can’t fall back on them, then the Treasurer will
probably find some way of making ends meet. Even a
N.S.W. Treasurer can do a good deal when he is driven
to it. , , .
(3) If the Treasurer is so placed that he has to make in-
come cover expenditure after providing for the above-
mentioned first lien, or else run short of money to pay
the Assembly’s salary, then the Assembly will probably
also develop an unexpected knowledge of how to make
ends meet (which is proposition No. 2 over again). _
(4) Necessity is the mother of invention (which is also
proposition No. 2 over again). As every party in the
Assembly so carefully avoids the necessity, the inference
is that it doesn’t want the invention.
(5) All the talk in the world about the Government
having borrowed too much is mere hypocrisy, so long as
not one member or party is game to bring in a Bill de-
claring that borrowing shall be rigidly limited to so much
per annum.
(6) All the conversation in the world about the misuse
of trust funds is dust and ashes, while no member or
party is prepared to bring in a Bill providing that the
trust-funds shall be vested in trustees, who shall not be
allowed to lend the Treasurer a copper, but shall invest
the money in buying up old Government securities in the
English market.
(7) All the complaints about deficits are a hollow joke
so long as no party has the courage to propose that all
the illicit means by which the Treasurer now supplements
the revenue shall be cut off, that he shall not have
access to either loan or trust-funds for temporary ad-
vances, and that, when money runs short, the payment
of wages, salaries and accounts must stop till more money
comes in—in other words, so long as no party is prepared
to take the responsibility of having no deficits.
(8) In addition to the above devices, a lot of good might
be effected if it were made law that the Premier must also
be Treasurer; and that in each 3 r ear, when the Auditor-
General certifies that there is no shortage after charging
the year with all sums fairly chargeable to it (including
the £2,000,000 for public works), the salary of the Pre-
mier-Treasurer shall be £lO,OOO, while in deficit years it
shall be £5OO. The difference is £9500, and £9500 is the
mother of invention just as much as necessity is. But,
as no party seems likely to suggest this either, it is
another missing evidence of the sincerity of the com-
plainants.
In fact, the only idea ever put forward is that the
crowd that is in should be put out, and the crowd
that is out should be put in, and, as the crowd
that is out always wants to be put in with full
liberty to do all the bad things for which the other
crowd is to be put out, the conclusion is that both
crowds are a nuisance.

The Irvine-Bent Programme.
The speech with which the Victorian Parliament
opened was a curious mixture of far-back Toryism
and professed Liberalism. 'The country is dead
certain that the Toryism will be acted up to;
still if it were equally certain that the Liberalism
would be acted up to, and that a Ministry could
be straight which is so badly Bent, it would
have every reason to be contented. The first
measure to be introduced is the new Factories
Act, which Minister Bent has already publicly
denounced, in Premier Irvine’s presence, as an
invention of the devil, or somewhere thereabouts.
It will doubtless be introduced, and it will
probably pass the Assembly, a sobered and
restrained Bent voting for it with the rest.
Then it will almost certainly be thrown out by
the House of 48 Rich Landlords, and, if it is,
the Bent Ministry can either pose as the crushed
and saddened victim of circumstances and let it go,
or it can fight. The finances are to be straightened,
mostly at the expense of the poor, by cutting
down wages, by charging income-tax on small
incomes hitherto exempt, and by charging probate
duty on small estates which have so far gone free.
As for a general land-tax, which will take in the
wealthy farming class and raise a little revenue
out of the enormously valuable ground-values in
the city of Melbourne, that seems as far off as
ever, and it will probably remain so till the
Australian Parliament makes the land-tax a
Federal impost, and a higher power smites Bent’s
allotments with a well-deserved ljd. or 2d. in the
£. But the clause in its programme on which the
Irvine Government’s reputation must stand or
fall is this :
A bill dealing with the reform of the Constitution will
be introduced at a very early date. That measure will
provide, amongst other things, for a reduction in the
number of Ministers, and of members of both branches of
the Legislature; for dispensing with the property quali-
fication for members of the Legislative Council, and ex-
tending the franchise for both chambers; for the settle-
ment of differences between the two Houses; and for an
early dissolution of Parliament so that a new Legislature
may be elected upon the altered basis.
If that Bill proves to be all that is described, and
if it doesn’t perish by the wayside, it will be the
greatest reform that Victoria has seen for at least
20 years. It will break the power of the narrowest
and most arrogant oligarchy in Australia, and
open the way for all manner of future reforms.
But the House of Landlords has already inti-
mated, by the mouth of one of its members, who
is also a member of the Irvine Government, that
while it will gladly pass the measures for laying
special taxation on the poor, there will be
violent opposition to Council reform, and the
Landlords will only move if they are assured that
the will of the country is behind the measure.
And the Landlords always refuse to become
aware that the will of the country is behind any
measure that they dislike. Until at least two
M’s.L.C. are killed in a riot the ignorance
of the other 46 about any public dissatisfaction
will continue. Certainly the Kyabram crowd
said nothing about constitutional reform;
and Bent isn’t a reformer; and the Argus
and Duncan Gillies and Sammy Lancaster
and F. Madden don’t look m the least like
people who want to do anything to the
House that blocks the land-tax. It is a misfor-
tune for the Irvine Government that its alleged
first principle should have been kicked by Minister
Bent, and its alleged second principle spoken off
with cold hostility by Solicitor-General Davies,
and that it should thus publicly hint to
the Upper House that it wants its sacred
principles trodden on. When these sacred,
principles are trodden on, if Irvine and
co. are the ordinary kind of reformers, they will
say regretfully that they can do no more. If they
are sufficiently extraordinary to tell the Council
that the whole machinery of Government is going
on strike till the 48 cave in and pass the Reform
Bill (a genuine Reform Bill), and if they keep
their word, The Bulletin will take back all the
unkind things it has said about them —even about
Bent. At a push, it will say that Bent doesn’t
weigh more than ten stone.
A Smell From the Colo River.
“ Counting-out ” in N.S.W. Assembly is becom-
ing scandalously prevalent. Within the last few
weeks there have been count-out attempts : to
shelve an awkward demand for a public works
return from the Owe’Sullivan (successful); to
dodge a decision on the Bushveldt Carbineers
question (unsuccessful); and to kill Mr. Arthur
Griffith’s Free Education Bill (successful). The
same game was played on Oct. 14, when the
Assembly counted-out on a silly “secession"
motion for adjournment by one of the comic poli-
ticians of the House. Possibly members wished
to dodge the responsibility for giving a straight
“aye” or “nay” on an issue which, however,
absurd, has “some votes in it” among the more
ignorant of the electors. But it is to be more
reasonably surmised that the count-out was due to
the fact that there appeared oil the business paper a
motion by Mr. J. C. L. Fitzpatrick expressing
disagreement “with the issue of rights or privileges
to private companies to utilise the natural forces
of the Grose, Colo, or other rivers of the State as
a means of providing motive power for the gene-
ration of electricity.” There have been, for
months past, rumors current as to the granting of
a big concession to a syndicate to generate elec-
tricity from the coastal rivers near Sydney, with
which to supply the city with motive and lighting
power. And the sinister circumstance in connec-
tion with these rumors is that no definite infor-
mation can be got as to their truth or not. If such
a concession has been granted, the members of the
N.S. W. Government should know of it; but they
profess ignorance as to so lofty a scheme, whilst
reluctantly owning up to a small firewood conces-
sion, or something of that sort, having been
granted to somebody or other. The public has a
right to know whether the only possible sources
of electric supply from water near to Sydney have
been secretly given over to a monopoly ; and it
won’t be less anxious.to know because an attempt
to ventilate the matter in Parliament has been
smothered by a count-out. Mr. Fitzpatrick
should take action on another night to bring the
matter again before Parliament.

The Kindly Landowner.
The landholders in the dry regions of Victoria are
getting their stock removed at merely nominal
railway rates to more favored localities, and they
go further and demand that it shall be removed
absolutely free. When the stock gets to the more
favored localities—the ones where there is grass—
the landlords there demand twice or three times
the usual rentals for grazing land. They shame-
lessly make a huge profit out of the distresses of
their fellow pastoralists, and no one seems sur-
prised at them doing it. Yet, if the Minister for
Railways doubled the railway rates for starving
stock, on the ground that it had to be shifted, and
thereforea fairopportunity presented itself to make
a little money, not for himself, after the fashion
of the private landlord, but for the community in
general, the public rage would be so great that
T. Bent would probably be lynched in Collins-
street and his body left on the tram-line. If he
simply retained the old rates, and took no advan-
tage of the pastoralists’ necessities at all, his billet
wouldn’t be worth a month’s purchase. Yet the
private landlord of the well-grassed regions, who
cruelly rack-rents his distressed rother from the
dry parts, isn’t assassinated. Hi i m’t even spoken
unkindly of. He deposits him.-elf on his usual
seat in the Upper House and legislates in the old
way. And he has strong opinions about the
necessity of the State making further railway
concessions, while he swallows up these conces-
sions by his exorbitant rents. And if the State
seized upon the unused or insufficiently used grass
lands, and paid just a fair rent for them, and let
them out at the same rent to the distressed mallee
pastoralists in order to save their flocks—well, the
howl of the landlord at not being allowed to de-
fraud his distressed fellow-countryman of his very
last copper would blow the front out of Parlia-
ment House, and the big stone pillars would go
careering down Bourke-street.

A Floating 1 Vote.
A wild rush is just now being made by the
Victorian Tories to sweep away one or two
decently democratic measures which stand in
the way of their having an absolutely free hand.
In 1898, the then fairly Tory electoral law of the
State was amended, so that electors could demand
special courts to be held any time up to a couple
of days before an election, and further, could, by
giving seven days’ notice and proving that they
resided in the district for one month, take out a
certificate entitling them to vote. The object of
this alteration was to enable workers who move
about a great deal, owing to the irregular nature
of their employment, to exercise the franchise.
The proof that such an amendment was highly
necessary is to be obtained in the great use that
has been made of voters’ certificates at every
election since the system came into operation. It
is not surprising, therefore, to find the Tories
doing their best to wreck the voters'-certificate
system, for the Tory always wants to go to the
polls untrammelled by the votes of the men he
employs. It is alleged, as a reason for abolishing
the certificates, that they encourage a floating
vote. Why a vote shouldn’t float instead of sink-
ing no Tory explains. And if there is a floating
vote in Victoria it is that manipulated and
exercised by the Tories themselves. Under the
present law a man, if he owns property in every
one of the 84 constituencies, may be on 84 different
rolls, and may cast his vote wherever it will be of
most use to his party. This scheme works out
handsomely for the Tories in cities and suburbs.
For instance, most of the property owners in the
Melbourne city own houses in several suburbs. If
there is no contest in the city they go to the
suburbs and vote. On the other hand, if there is
no contest in their suburb, they come in and
vote in Melbourne or Melbourne East.. This was
done with great effect at the last election ; indeed
the newspapers came out with lists showing how
certain Tory votes that were released by walks-
over in Brighton, Toorak and West Melbourne
should float round to other places and be east
there in the party interests. Now that the Tories
have raised the question of the amendment of the
electoral law, democrats ought to see to it that
property-owners are compelled to vote where they
reside, and not float.

An interesting row has started between the
Federal Government and the South Australian one,
as to “ the proper channel of communication,” in
matters affecting external affairs, between the
States and the British Govt. Chamberlain, set
going first by the Foreign Office, to which a com-
plaint had been made, asked the Federal Govt,
to consider and report on an alleged breach of the
Netherlands Convention at Adelaide. Deakin
wrote to Jenkins, asking for information, but got
no sort of answer whatever for two months, although
he asked repeatedly for at least the courtesy
of an acknowledgment. Finally, when Jenkins
did have the grace to reply at all, it was to say
that “ this Government cannot allow any of its
officers to submit their conduct to inquiry by any
other Government.” Mr. Deakin’s contention
opon this was.absolutely conclusive : “The fulfil-
ment of treaty obligations is obviously one of those
external affairs peculiarly Federal, which could
not be dealt with independently by each State
without producing an intolerable condition of con-
fusion prolific in international complications.”
Yet, at latest, Chamberlain had sent direct to
the S. A. Government for a report, whioh looks
very much like putting the Commonwealth into the
dust. Presumably, however, this has not settled
the matter for good. The Federal Government’s
duty, anyhow, is plain—to fight for the complete
knock-out of this foolish little claim that there are
still six tinpot “Foreign Ministers” in these
States, . all running on their own, and some of
them, it seems, discourteous enough to cause no
end of trouble.

BUNDABERG, October 7.
On search being made of the recruiter Coquette yester-
day, prior to her departure for the South Sea Islands, the
Polynesian officials came upon about 30 rifles, together
with a quantity of ammunition, fuses, and dynamite.
The islanders had concealed these things most carefully,
and only the most diligent and thorough searching
brought them to light.
It almost seems as if it were the wild nigger on the
islands who needed to be protected from his
Christian brother. Apparently, judging by the
careful search made, it is known to be the regular
thing for the Christian nigger to go back loaded.
This picture of him going ashore with 30 rifles
and a lot of dynamite is very different from the
one to which we are accustomed —the one in which
he returns armed only with the Scripture and a
spirit of resignation and a clean collar, and is met
on the beach by a howling demon, who leaps eight
feet in the air every time he howls, and who
promptly sheds the returned one’s blood, and
boils the cooking-pot with the Scripture and the
clean collar.

A miner, writing to us from Johannesburg, gives
some indication of the reason why the British (or
rather Cohen) administration in S’Africa has had
to impose a head-tax to force the Kaffirs to work
in the mines:
Under Boer rule the Kaffir laborer got 3s. per day and
he was satisfied. Now the capitalists have nobbled the
Government to cut the Kaffir down to Is. per day. A
number of Kaffirs at the Geldinous Deep G.M. Co., on
finding out how they were placed (out of the 30s. a month
which they were paid 2s. a month was taken £or a pass,
and they had to pay the expenses for their own enlist-
ment, so that for the first month they got about 155.)
wanted to be sent home again, but they were flogged
under the muzzles of guns into submission. Of course,
that is not slavery ! The military help the mine-owners
in everything and supply soldiers, who have been stran-
ded here, at the rate of ss. a day to take miners’ places.
Capital has played a gigantic game and has won a big stake.
Dr. Jameson has made another Raid, this time against
constitutional government in Cape Colony, and but for
the Australian Premiers in England, I believe the con-
stitution would have gone down. I feel sure the Labor
party here will poll heavily at election time if we get
anything like a liberal franchise, but the Government
will do all it can in the interests of capital to bilk the
democratic party. The country is just now over-run with
Jews. No one could get a pass to return to the Rand
without a Jewish name and a nose to endorse the name.
I know one man by the name of Gerald who applied
eight times for a pass but was refused ; he then took the
name of Rubinstein and got his pass without delay
♦
The materialising of spirits is an old game, and
the theory that other things can be material-
ised by hard thinking about them has also found
some credence. There is a legend about a French
artist who thought so hard about a unicorn he
was painting that the animal materialised in his
studio, broke nearly everything, kicked the paint-
ing backwards into the middle of the twelfth
century, so that it fell on the head of King Philip
Augustus, and put his horn through a door worth
£3 7s. 6d. In like manner, George Reid, by hard
thinking about his Dry Dog, seems to have
materialised that animal. Twice during his recent
Imported-Shoddy campaign in Victoria, a small,
disreputable-looking dog arose out of nowhere
in particular, and ascended the platform, and
sat in front of the great Wobbler, and hung
out its tongue to show that it was dry.
There is no evidence that it was a real dog ; it
was apparently only a temporarily materialised
idea. George tries hard to exorcise the animal
now by dropping his old figure of speech about the
Freetrade industry being like a small dog which
has to be thrown into the water and made to swim
for itself, but the animal has been so firmly
established that it will only wear away in the
slow process of years. It is the ghost of George
K.eid’s many protected industries —the ones he
didn’t throw into the water but kept dry on the
bank, and his silent demand for an explanation of
his own drouth is pathetic.

Some Victorian election figures: Average number
of electors per member, 2872, varying from 1542
for Clunes and Allendale, 1586 for Windermere,
and 1711 for Creswick, to 6472 for Essendon and
Flemington, and 5330 for Eastern Suburbs. Aver-
age number who voted, 1965 per member. For
the 70 seats for which there was a fight (including
11 double electorates), 169,058 votes were cast, of
which the Ministry scored 96,445; Opposition,
38,148; and Labor, 34,645. On these figures, in a
House of 95 members, the Government should
claim 54 supporters, the Opposition 22, and Labor
19; yet, allowing for the fact that in many
country electorates Opposition and Labor con-
stituents were limited to voting for one or
other of several Ministerial candidates, the
electorates are parcelled out in such an iniqui-
tous fashion that the parties number respectively
66, 15 and 14! By what process of reasoning the
3465 electors, who sent Anstey in for Bourke
Boroughs, are reckoned only half as good as the
382 who returned Wallace for Maldon, is known
only to the man chiefly responsible for the present
rascally system canny Tory Gillies. Last
Wednesday week 104,636 “ city ” voters (including
Ballarat, Bendigo and Geelong) returned 35 repre-
sentatives, just as many as were sent in by 64,402
country voters. In the metropolis alone Labor
secured 13 seats and the Opposition 4 against the
Ministry’s 12. Irvine says he represents the
oountry. So he does—but not in the sense he
means. He represents cows and turnips.

Melbourne Age country intelligence;
HIGH RENTALS FOR GRASS.
Maffra. Tuesday.—Rents in this district are increasing
as leases expire. Mr. W. B. Crisp’s farm, of 211 acres,
formerly let at 138. per acre, brought 21s. 3d. per acre at
auction. Another* formerly let at 65., brought 11s. 6d.
per acre. MessrSf'Glassford, of Mewburn Park, have let
2000 acres for ten months at the rate of 30s. per acre per
year for grazing only.
Leongatha, Tuesday.—Fifty trucks of starving stock
arrived at Leongatha from northern areas on Saturday.
Some of them were in a pitiable condition, and several
were dead in the trucks. Every week special trains are
arriving with both horses and cattle, the owners of which
are paying as much as 2s. 6d. and 3s. per head to local
graziers for pasture.
The Government railways carry the starving
stock for next to nothing, and the people make
good the loss. The Government has also come down
to giving the distressed pastoralist tick for the trifle
it does charge him. Then when the perishing ani-
mals reach grass country the above happens. The
landlord ghoul has no feeling for the distressed
country, or the distressed stock, or the ruined
owners—except to grind double tents out of
their distresses. He is firmly convinced, while
doubling his own charges, that the State
should “make concessions”—but as for making
concessions himself he would see his country
and his distressed countrymen in Hades sooner.
And the landlord’s prescriptive right to be an
extortioner is so deep-rooted that no one
thinks of hanging him on a lamp-post for
.such proceedings as these The Government is
