Melba’s third concert brought so huge
fen audience to Sydney Town Hall that
there wasn’t room for a programme to flutter
to the floor. Bird songs are as much in Madame’s
line as if she were winged, feathered and
beaked, and when she sang Bishop’s “Lo!
Here the gentle Lark,” her trilling seemed
to know no more bounds or bars than the
Soaring small bird with the great voice. After
four recalls she assuaged the musical thirst with a
dainty bit of sentimental simplicity, “Three
Green Bonnets,” and presently sang, “Home,
Sweet Home,” to her own accompaniment. One
is popularly supposed to dash aWay tears at this
number, but one never does. The Venetian
Chant which she gave to a harp accompani-
ment was received rapturously. Madame’s poor
support did its best, and the only hardship of
the evening was the National Anthem’s definite
announcement that there would be no more
Melba for three days. The diva’s appear-
ance was as fine as on previous occasions, but
she was differently festooned and garlanded—with
silver gauze, roses and diamonds. The final
Sydney concert is to-night (Thursday), and the
singer then goes on to Brisbane.
** * *
The pallid, whitewash-colored countenance
of Sherlock Holmes has so far looked inscrutably
on things at Sydney Royal in the presence of
respectable business. But Sherlock is a repul-
sively-cocky individual, and, on the whole, The
Bulletin’s sympathies are with Moriarty, even
apart from Atholwood’s fine rendering of the
character. Moriarty is forced by the author to
ridiculously mismanage his affairs in order that
Holmes may come out on top, whereas, if the
novelist left them to fight things out, the long,
brown, reptile person in the cellar would carry this
paper’s money every time. There is a very ancient
legend that Buddha or some other original person
was much worried in his mind as to whether a
deer or a tiger was the faster animal, so he created
the tiger, and then created the deer 100 yards
ahead, and watched the result. If the novelist
had similarly created his two characters and
turned them loose to settle things themselves, the
professor, in about six months, would probably
nave done it by throwing Sherlock’s head
through the author’s window. There is a patient
methodicalness about the long, lean, ancient King
of Crime, as he sits at a desk in his cellar, posting
the dead bodies in a big ledger, and adding up
the horrors and the loot, and sending telephone
instructions for new crimes, that contrasts
strongly with Sherlock’s habit of taking drugs by
the fireside and looking fixedly at space. And
when Sherlock, in the last act, becomes maudlin
in his love for the girl who was tortured to make
her give up “ the papers,” he contrasts badly with
Moriarty, who has just gone to gaol cursing and
disguised as a cabman, but has refused to propose
to any girl right up to the end. All through
Moriarty lays to his book and sticks to business.
Sherlock mixes business with girl and cocoaine,
and that ought to be his undoing.
* sfc * *
“ A Million of Money ” continues to be a
glittering mass of wealth at Sydney Lyceum.
The fact that the two villains go about their
wickedness in a probable and systematic manner
is largely responsible for this, for the improbable
villain has become a weariness and the impossible
ruffian is a burden. Miss Harrie Ireland, as the
bad lady with the magic eye, sandwiches her life
of sin with relapses into legal matrimony, which
invariably turn out badly. Baker’s position, when
beloved by two such beauteous apparitions as
Harrie Ireland and Frances Ross, is an enviable
one, and it is a pity he doesn’t better appreciate
his luck. It is a duel of dress between trie two,
and Frances wins by an extra yard of tail.

(25th), Tuesday and Thursday.
** * *
At Sydney Criterion “ Mrs. Goldstein”
gives place this week to “Little Christopher
Columbus” revived. The Goldstein production,
though improving towards the end, did not take
too well, being sketchy, purposeless and uncertain;
also there wasn’t much of it at any time. More-
over, the joke on which it rested was old and
tattered ; and Johnny Sheridan, as a mother-in-
law, had certain drawbacks.
** * *
At Sydney Tivoli, Sfr.ndow comes to the
close of his brief season with a new and wonderful
equestrian feat of strength. Of late, Sandow has
been trying to put some little graces and tricks
into his turn ; but the light manner does not sit
on him well. The Nawns, the Wiltons, Wallace
Brownlow, and the biograph pictures continue to
give a necessary flavor of frivolity and jocoseness
to the programme, over which Sandow’s muscle
casts a great shadow of seriousness.
* # * *
“ The Messenger Boy ” is, naturally, not
a better play than it was last week, but the com-
pany at the Palace Theatre makes a somewhat
more successful effort to put some life into its
mummy-like form and to do the librettist’s work
for him. It is probable that the ‘ ‘ Boy ” will see
the season of his co. out. Next month “Cyrano
de Bergerac ” will be staged by a co. led by Mr.
Henry Lee. Melbourne critics spoke very highly
of this production; and the Melbourne public
stayed away from it cordially; which two facts
seem to hint at success here.
* * * *
“Oh 1 Mr. Pennilove,” the farcical comedy
that cropped up at Melbourne Bijou last Satur-
day, is an early essay in dramatic authorship by
“Billy” Elton’s son. Nothing so substantially
food was expected from the silly title of G. W.
Ilton’s three-act farce. "Perhaps the result
seemed all the better because so little had been
expected, but after allowing for the effects of sur-
prise, one must admit that “ Oh ! Mr. Pennilove”
has genuine pretensions to newness, originality,
and go. The plot has no side-issues to
speak of; the interest in its characters is not pro-
perly maintained and balanced ; it doesn’t appeal
to the sympathies of the audience at any one
point; it is amateurishly acted for the most
part—nevertheless, “Oh! Mr. Pennilove” will
bear thinking about. An ordinary, neatly-built
farce doesn’t bear thinking about. Elton fils has
written something of a comedy in which there is
promise of future success for the author.
• * » *
Pennilove is a weak, farcical, elderly
person, controlled by Chapman, his friend.
Chapman once saved him from drowning in 3ft.
of water, and has been drawing cheques on his
gratitude ever since. Chapman’s former girl,
lately married to another friend of Pennilove
(whose marriage and place of residence are
strangely unknown to that amiable idiot) has pre-
served some letters with a view to wrecking
Chapman’s designs on the income and affections
of a recent “mash.” This new girl happens to be
Pennilove’s niece, yet Chapman doesn’t know of
her relationship until he meets her in uncle’s
house and arranges to elope with her next day.
Also uncle and aunt live through the three acts of
complication with it discovering this little thread
of love story. (. hapman persuades his meek
Pennilove to th t c ' foolishness of entering
the other (whilst the bride and bride-
groom are away at a ball) in order to steal the in-
criminating documents from beneath the mattress
of the bridal couch. Pennilove doesn’t find the
letters and photographs, but he gets discovered
himself by two burglars—one of them serio and
the other comic—who leave him to the wrath of
the lawful occupants of the bed. His wife like-
wise tracks him to the other lady’s sleeping apart-
ment, and in his wild escape from the premises
he gets damaged. Pennilove’s troubles terminate
in his earnest renunciation of Chapman, after that
airy adventurer has been put to confusion by the
married lady from the burgled bedroom. The
farce is a good acting piece, consequently some
of the characters suffer considerably at the hands
of the Hawtrey co. Mrs. G. B. Lewis, as Penni-
love’s wife, is the only convincing actress engaged
on the contract. One of the other four —a Miss
Mary Bancroft —has more brightness and less ex-
perience than the other three. Hawtrey makes
Pennilove too old and heavy, and C. P. Hammond,
as the dashing Chapman, is still an amateur in his
restlessness. He seems unable to keep his hands
off the man he is talking to. Gregan MacMahon
is a satisfactory man-servant, whereas Heggie is a
loud, inhuman captain, and the comic burglar of
Oily Deering might be more pungently played by
that humorous veteran. G. W. Elton fills in the
part of the gloomy felon, and does it very well.
Considering that Melbourne has been prone to
accept a lot of future musical celebrity on trust, it
would be only a fair and business-like thing to bill
Elton’s farcical-comedy as the work of the coming
Australian Pinero, and trust to Providence to
justify the predictive title.
* * * *
An uncommendable “ comedy-drama ” of
the most ancient American pattern came to light
at Melb. Royal last Saturday. It was called
“Way Down South,” and the central figure was
a coal-black lady who undertook to work her
fingers ter de bone for a salary of ten dollars a
month on behalf of two full-grown orphan
childer, de offspring of de plump cullur’d pusson’s
deceased massa. Maggie Moore was the black
Samaritana in question, and her feelings burst
into song what time de good Lord watched over
the orphans in their distress. This piebald narra-
tive held the stage for three nights only. “ Arrah-
na-pogue ” is the programme to-night (Thursday)
and to-morrow, and “The Widow from Japan”
will follow on Saturday, with the intention of
stimulating business for the final week of Maggie
Moore’s season. “The Widow’s” intentions are
understood to be strictly frivolous, which is all in
favor of the season’s last nights.
* * # *
At Her Majesty’s (Melb.), the bulky
gorgeousness of the latest musical comedy fills
Melbourne’s perennial want. To the public, who
must love something in the tuneful frivolity line,
it might as well be “ The Toreador ” as anything
else, and now that the tired tenor Gussie has some-
what accelerated his pace the performance is
brighter than at first. Miss Young, in a
rich red insurrectionary costume, lends a
flavor of Carmen to the plot. The weird
bandmaster from the Lunatic Asylum is a vola-
tile Spaniard, and the labored eccentricity
of Hugh Ward’s dance (Ward dances as correctly
as a problem from Euclid) has a pretty orchestral
accompaniment, like the bouquet song which Miss
Lulu Evans warbles in tremulous tones. Some
nights'last week Miss Chetwynd took the place of
Miss Carrie Moore, and the lightsome Maud was
perhaps more pleasing than Carrie. At any rate,
she gambolled with less self-consciousness, and
her eyes didn’t appear to be competing for the
cake. People complain that Carrie’s lustrous orbs
are too demonstrative.

“Charlie’s Aunt” will complete a satis-
factory fortnight at the Princess’s on Friday.
The remaining six nights of Thornton’s season
have been set apart for “The Private Secretary,”

but up to now it has owed nothing to Christian
Er de ivor. The meek and lowly victim of chronic
inf u inza is still waiting for a free advt. from the

** # *
At the Opera House they have an excep-
tionally smart bill. The Sidney Drews, always
interesting, produced their best entertainment on
Saturday, and scored a genuine success with it.
“The Yellow Dragon ” is a strong playlet wherein
Mrs. Drew appears as a nicely-gilded widow in
danger of sudden death from the Boxers at Pekin,
and Drew i 3 the alert Yank who saves the lady
and himself with the assistance of two or three
minor characters. Cinquevalli and his man
Friday are the centre of the show, as usual,
and another amazing “turn” is supplied
by the Schenk Brothers —Willie and Charlie.
One Schenk balances his alleged brother on his
hand, or his head, as gracefully as the brother
balances himself. The topmost Schenk is marvel-
lous, and the Schenk below reflects equal credit
on the family. Fred Poplar and Kitty Wager
are acceptable items in the Rickards show,
although they have rather too much, to sing for
themselves. Florrie Gallimore, who is even more
prolonged and less acceptable, will leave the
Opera House this week. The lady in question has
been there for about six months—it has seemed
six months, anyhow—and the present scribe has
heard her sing the same old sort of song in 93 dif-
ferent dresses. The variety of her skirts was only
exceeded by the sameness of her songs.
## * *
Fitzgeralds’ Circus and the menagerie
attached thereto drew a mighty crowd to the re-
opening last Saturday night. The original iron
Temple of Gee, where neither moth nor rust, doth
corrupt, and rain cannot soak through and trickle,
has been multiplied by two, and the animals in
the outer watertight compartment are greeting
their new quarters with roars of satisfaction. Also
the show has taken on some latest improve-
ments in the lighting department, so that when
Dan Fitzgerald stands talking to Commodore
he shines almost as brightly as George Rignold at
the Battle of Agincourt. Taking it altogether,
Fitzgerald’s Circus gives full. value for cash.
Many things that used to occur in the arena or on
the trapezes overhead are happening again with a
slight difference, and the differences are mostly in
favor of the present show. An exciting novelty—-
i.e., a novelty at the Circus —is the Cycle Whiz. The
Lotto Troupe introduced cycle whizzing to Aus-
tralia last Xmas, and now our old but youthful
friends Harry Cardello and Ernest Shand are
showing how easy it is for a skilled circus per-
former to bike at an acute angle. Nothing comes
difficult to an active and intelligent citizen who
has acquired the habit of turning somersaults on a
naked horse.
** # #
At Adelaide Royal, “ Mice and Men ” ran
its appointed course—the one week, beyond which
it is rash to tempt fortune in that city. Business
for that week was excellent. George Musgrove
told an interviewer that, for an absolute fact, the
piece is better played here than it was in London.
There is now being played “A Modern Magdalen,”
by Haddon Chambers—“first production in any
country.” Adelaide, remembering its early privi-
leges in connection with “ The Toreador ” and
“Sherlock Holmes,” begins to wonder whether it
is a critical centre whose approval must at all
hazards be gained in advance, or merely the con-
venient Dog on whom experiments are tried.
The Tivoli, after remaining closed for a week, has
welcomed the W. Anderson co., and settled down
to wipe out C. Holloway’s record for Dangerous
Women by looking upon “ The Worst Woman in
London.”
The WILDCAT
COLUMN
Losses made last financial year on some Aus-
tralian railway systems—through building with money
borrowed at absurdly high rates of interest. The figures
for N.S.W. and Tasmania are a close approximation only,
pending the arrival of exact details. Those for N.S.W.
are based on the Auditor General’s figures for 1901, with
an allowance for decreased revenue in 1902 :
Queensland .. .. .. £449,946
New South Wales .. .. 184,000
Victoria .. •• 163,226
Tasmania .. .. 100,000
South Australia 74,129
Northern Territory .. .. 63,093
From four of these States (Tasmania being the possible
exception) there come bitter complaints that, to prevent
the deficit looking any worse, repairs are being postponed
till the lines are getting into a dangerous state of disre-
pair. The railways of the Australian mainland earn on
an average; close on 31 per cent., but when they have to
earn nearly 4 per cent, in order to pay interest on loans,
the position becomes impossible. The railways of the
United Kingdom, with six times as many people for every
mile of line, only earned in 1900 (the last year for which
writer has statistics by him) £3 Bs. 2d. per cent. The
Australian mainland lines earn as much as British lines
do—and Australia pays better wages for shorter hours,
and its railways are expected to charge, in reasonably
thicklv-peopled districts, no more than British rates, and
in the backblocks to carry water free in drought time,
and starving stock for next to nothing. And when, with
these handicaps, they only pay as much interest on cost
as British lines do, there is a cry in many quarters that
State ownership is a failure. Australia expects altogether
too much from its State railways.
* * * *
The motion put on the notice-paper of N.S.W.
Assembly by Griffith, M.L.A., to the effect that “the
financial condition of the State” demands the imposition
of an absentee tax, is opportune. There is a general
feeling in Australia that something is about to happen.
The language used of late about our loans and securities
by the papers of Pawnbroker Bull is more disrespectful
than ever it was before. Such authorities as the
Economist talk openly about the necessity of cutting off
the supplies. The last Victorian loan was mostly left in
the hands of the underwriters. The last N.S.W. loan
was floated by offering it far below the figure at which
the Victorian loan went aground, but N.S.W. securities
have dropped so badly since then that it is practically
certain the next loan will have to be offered at a still
lower figure. M'Culloch, M.L.A., of Victoria, has come
back, after fishing in the troubled waters of London,
with the report that more loans are practically impossible.
Maoriland is trying to borrow at a high rate in Australia
because Bull’s inhospitable door has banged. If loans
really cease for any length of time, Australia brings up
hard against the fact that it has to pay more, in interest
on public debts and in incomes to absentee mortgagees
and landlords, than it can pay in these bad times, simply
because the money to pay it with isn’t here.
If a sufficient inducement can be offered to the
absentee landlord and investor to come back and live in
Australia, then several millions a year of the liability dis-
appears at once. The individual who draws £30,000 a
year out of Australia, and spends it out of Australia,
costs the country, as a whole, just that much. If the
same individual lives here and spends his £30,000 a year
here, he costs the country, as a whole, nothing at all. If
such a heavy tax is put upon the rich Australian absentee
that he has to come back and live in Australia, the
amount thus saved may just rescue the country from
bankruptcy. Certainly when Australia can’t export the
interest on its foreign debt and the incomes of its absen-
tees, because there isn’t enough money in the country to
do both things, and when it can’t borrow the money any
longer, it must either tax its absentee so heavily that he
must needs come back, or it must cease to pay interest
on its public debt. And it isn’t worth while incurring
the disgrace of public bankruptcy merely that the Wilson
and Cooper and D’Arcy clan may eat and drink with the
aristocracy in London.

This is a rather remarkable series of decreases, though the
repayment of the last instalment of the reconstructed
deposits (£111,358) partly accounts for the diminution.
The reserve of cash and liquid securities represents barely
20 per cent, of all liabilities to the public, which is a far
weaker reserve than the average. The Colonial Bank has
always had a habit of keeping its cash reserves low, and,
though this policy of investing every possible copper may
be good for the profits, it isn’t a wise policy in a time of
sudden stress. The Colonial only holds, as a second line
of defence, £12,274 in State and municipal securities,
whereas a very much smaller bank, the Royal, of Mel-
bourne, holds £95,000, and the contrast doesn’t look well.
Certainly, the earnings are very respectable and the
reserve-fund is making good progress, but much greater
care should be taken to make sure that the reserve can
be found if a sudden demand for it arises.
The Colonial Bank, however, has one immense
recommendation. Its business lies wholly in Victoria,
and wholly in the better-watered portions of Victoria,
and it has kept religiously out of the drought area. This
will mean a great deal in the long run.

The sort of language that is now used about us
by Pawnbroker Bull. Extract from the Economist
(London) : “ In face of the condition of affairs, the subject
of Australian public borrowing, however stale it may be,
ought to receive renewed attention. Without repeating
the arguments against the continuance of the borrowing
policy, the conviction may be recorded that, just as
surely as the banking crash of 1893 became inevitable
several years previously, a disastrous crisis will occur in
Australian public finance, unless British investors decline,
before it is too late, to lend any more money, a step
whichjwould compel economical management.”

* * * *
The great success of the Wilfley patent Con-
centrator since its merits became known to the mining
community of Australia, has led to imitations and
piracies under various names being placed upon the
market. The Wilfley Ore Concentrator Syndicate of
London, who own the Australian patents have decided to
prosecute all users and vendors of concentrating machines
infringing their patents, and action has been commenced
in Victoria. Similar action to protect their rights had to
be taken in America, where the Supreme Court has
upheld Wilfley’s patents, granting an injunction and
damages against the users and vendors of the infringing
machine. The sole manufacturers in Australia of Wilfley’s
patent concentrator are the Austral Otis Engineering
Company of South Melbourne, who supply customers
direct or through any of their accredited agents in the
various mining centres.*

Limited (Newcastle, N.S.W.) is a concern with a paid-up
capital of £83,888. It was registered in 1896, and took
over the Newcastle Permanent Investment and Building
Society. It took over also a heavy mass (much over
£lOO,OOO worth) of properties in possession. The institu-
tion taken over had a paid-up capital of £171,249 and
hardly any reserves. Apparently a large portion of the
paid-up capital was applied to form a contingency fund

foreclosed properties) £43,042; and undivided profit,
£BO5O. During the past four years the amount added to
reserves out of earnings has been £3859, which is a
reasonably large amount, but not too large. There
appears to be every probability that the contingency fund
will more than cover all losses (the properties in posses-
sion are now £78,177, and the total assets £154,414 ; and
a contingency account of £43,042 looks a very solid pro-
vision against these). All the same, the formation of a
permanent reserve fund is highly advisable. The New-
castle Building and Investment Co. is nL v one of the
largest and most prosperous concerns of its kind in
N.S.W. An especially good feature is that it trades
almost entirely with its own capital, deposits amounting
to only £16,237.
» * * •
Even at this late hour the See Government
would do well to pass an amending Act as regards the
Sydney land resumptions, and make payment in ‘A\ per
cent, long-dated or 4 per cent, short-dated State securi-
ties compulsory. To ask Bull for another £3,000,000 for
resumptions together with £3,000,000 to keep the
Owe’Suilivan pot boiling generally is like courting disas-
ter. The method proposed would at least make £3,000,000
of new debt a local (or mostly-local) instead of foreign
liability, and that in itself is a great deal gained.

Maoriland is trying to borrow money both
locally and in Australia at 4 per cent., which lends
additional weight to the persistent rumor that Premier
Seddon sounded the London market about another loan,
and the market snorted at him. S.A. is trying to bor-
row a little £239,000 locally at 3J per cent. Westralia
also proposes trying to raise £750,000 locally, but in
addition the James Ministry proposes to provide £212,490
out of revenue for railways and other works formerly
charged to loans. Treasurer Gardiner says indignantly
that borrowing in London and getting £B7 10s. net for
every £lOO of stock is “ buying money.” The trouble is
that, while some other States are taking in sail a little,
N.S.W. is committed to so many things, including land
resumptions and unfinished railways, that it can’t take
in sail without the mast going overboard.

The Civil Service Mutual Building and Investment Society
is considering a proposal for amalgamation with a larger
institution. There is nothing wrong with it, except that
it is so exceedingly small that working expenses swallow
up more than half the gross earnings. A very small office
can’t be worked so economically as a large one, and this
society had the misfortune to start just before the great
financial crash, when the public faith in building societies
was so badly shaken that there has been no chance since
then for a small institution to grow to profitable dimen-
sions. To keep an office, staff, and directorate to look
after £lB,OOO worth of investments is hardly payable.
• * • *
Broken Hill: B.H. Block 14, which, having
some ore contracts and slimes on hand, kept its smelters
at Pt. Adelaide going after the mine and mill had been
closed, has now run down the furnaces, too. This
doesn’t knock Broken Hill any flatter, but it adds a drop
of bitter to Adelaide’s cup of woe, and is given as one of
the reasons why the Treasurer had to amend his esti-
mates. Official (Mines Dept.) figures show that, of the
B.H. mines now working, the Central (Sulphide Oorp.)
last year sent down 21 cubic yards of mullock to fill in
worked-out stopes for 100 tons of ore taken out; Block 10
20yds., B.H. Prop. 16, South 13. This year the South
has been doing more. It was said here a while ago that
underground collapses are most common near the boun-
dary of two mines. The plain fact is that one
co. tries to steal a march on the other, and, by digging
out every possible ton of ore itself and neglecting to fill
up the old workings, throws the responsibility and the
expense of keeping up that part of the lode upon its
neighbor. If it had not been for this it is exceedingly
doubtful whether two recent slips, one of them butchering
two men, would have occurred. The matter doesn’t seem
to have had as much attention from mine inspectors as it
deserves. The crush in the Central, and the very nar-
row escape of Kintore shaft (it was only saved by a solid
60ft. pillar left standing against it), ought to hurry up the
sinking of that absolutely essential second shaft at the
neighboring South. A comparatively small movement
might completely disable the South for a couple of years,
because its one working shaft is sunk right in the lode.
The remarkably good results got by the South in its
last half-year were largely due to the re-treatment of stuff
which had previously been through the old mill but was
still rich. It was reported then that the supply of this
profitable material would be exhausted by the end of this
year ; but recent tests prove that more of it is worth re-
treating than had been estimated.——The Victoria 8.H.,
which may prove profitable with lead at about £l3, has
stopped work.

A ’Change rumor that was struck at half-a-
dozen times this month, but still refused to die, was that
Broken Hill’s water-supply was just about finished. One
circumstantial story was that the co. had given notice to
the mining co.’s that it was down to its last few jugfuls.
Well, the position is bad enough, but it isn’t quite so bad
as that. A few figures (from the co.’s reports mostly)
will probably do more good than a column of loose talk
(all quantities are in gallons, thousands omitted) :

Since Dec. 1900, an allowance of 15 per cent, has been
made for siltage. In the few cases where the company’s
figures are not available, the estimates are based on the
data of previous figures. If it were not for evaporation, the
387 million gallons would last 2£ years ; but generally, the
loss by evaporation is four times greater than the con-
sumption, or more. It is essential, then, to estimate the
evaporation. As the water-surface diminishes, the loss,
of course, diminishes. When, at the beginning of an
earlier half-year, there was 549 million gallons stored,
the loss in that half-year was 243 million gallons ; with
427 millions stored, at the beginning of the current half-
year, the loss might be 200 million —that seems a very
iiberal estimate. With a consumption of 80 millions per
half year, this would leave 147 millions at the end of Dec.
The loss by evaporation would then be further reduced,
so that, assuming that the company’s measurements are
correct, and they were checked at the end of 1900 by the
Govt, hydrographical surveyor, it does not look as though
the supply will absolutely fail until after the end of Feb.,
at any rate. What its quality is going to be when that
bottom layer is reached isn’t too nice to think about.
Already it is making the Silverton Tram Co.’s loco, boilers
very sick. And wl, .r will happen if, as is often the case,
there are no summer rains, is another thing that isn’t
nice to think about.
* * * •
The stoppage of the Block 14 smelters at Port
Adelaide will throw 250 men out of work, and will cause
a loss of £550 a week in wages. The whole plant is now
at a standstill, and will remain so until lead goes up
another pound or two a ton. The first smelting works in
Australia were established at Port Adelaide 50 years ago
to treat the copper from Burra, but they were removed
to N.S.W. long ago by the English and Australian Co.
which ran them.
* • • »
The greedy maw of the Broken Hill mines!
Campbell, one of two young men buried alive in the
Central mine the other day, had, by different accidents
in this same group of mines, lost a brother and a brother-
in-law. In 10 years 163 men have been killed, 281 seri-
ously injured (many of them permanently), and 486 others
less seriously. And these are independent of the victims
of lead-poisoning, now happily much fewer than formerly.
A man who has worked there gives a graphic
description of the place where the two miners were
trapped and buried in the latest fall of ground at Broken
Hill. They were “mullocking”—that is, filling up the
worked-out parts of the mine with rock shot down from
the surface. This stuff is tipped into winzes, or passes,
and diverted to almost any part by chutes, worked by
trap-doors or slides. As the places are gradually filled up
the “mullockers” have to work on their hands and
knees. In order to reach the chute which the two men
were “working” at the time of the accident, one man
had to drag himself forward, lying full length—no room
for him to either rise or turn. The other man stood, or
crouched, a little distance back, in case anything
went wrong. When the door was pulled back and
the 70-truck load of rock roared and rattled down
the chute to the next check, 60ft. below, the ground
used to shake, and loose stuff fell around the workman,
who, having opened the chute, must spring back as far as
his prone position would let him. For doing that sort of
work, when they are lucky, they get Bs. 4d. a day, and,
when they’re not, they get a ride in an ambulance—pretty
often with all the flaps down.

Last week maize reached 6s. 6d. a bushel whole-
sale in Sydney—the highest for many years.
• • • •
N.S.W. dredging co.s make a poor showing in
divs. Jembaicumbene is a conspicuous exception to the
rule, while Araluen Con. is an equally notable example of
it. This co. won 3030 z. in July, 4300 z. in Aug., 408 oz. in
Sept., and for three weeks of this month (one dredge idle
for a fortnight) 2440 z. It has had good returns all
through, excepting for a month or so, and, even then,
with economical management, enough to pay exes., yet
it is over 12 months since it declared a div. The second
dredge was bought from the Shoalhaven Terraces Co. on
strangely liberal terms for the latter. Most derelict
dredging co.s consider themselves lucky if they get a
quarter of what their plants cost, but this Shoalhaven co.
got 14,000 fully-paid Araluen Con. shares, worth, at the
time, 7s. 6d. per share, afterwards rising to Bs. 6d., and
£lOOO cash, or (say) £6500, the Araluen Co. having to pay
cost of removal and re-erection of dredge 1 If directors
are as generous in all their transactions no wonder that
shareholders have to complain of the paucity of divs. Not
one of the Turon or Macquarie dredging co.s has paid a
div., and there is not the remotest chance of any one
doing so. Turon was a name to conjure with, and it was
conjured with for all it was worth, as many people know
to their sorrow. Shares, which once could be got only as
a personal favor—as was alleged—at £3, would now be
dear at as many shillings, if not pence. The harm done
by the Turon boom is not to be gauged by individual
losses, but by the general mistrust of mining ventures
which it engendered.

Baker’s Creek (N.S.W.), has struck a new reef—
a 6in. vein is called a reef in this mine—running parallel
with Smith’s reef. From the latter, co. paid nearly
£250,000 in divs., and new lode is reported to be equally
rich. So far it is only a newspaper report, and the market
indicates that speculators are sceptical.
• * * »
Sydney Harbor Collieries Co. making good
headway with progressive work. Drives being extended
on coal seam met with in shaft at the rate of 25yds. per
week. Seam gradually widening. Coal of excellent
quality. Officials believe that main seam is located about
350yds. south of shaft in a direct line, but co. could not
drive straight for it without encroaching on other
property, and a dog’s-hind-leg level, twice the necessary
length, has to be driven.

“May-day”: Never heard of the mine. Any
report from that district must be taken with an extra
quantity of salt. Local men have made lying a fine art.
* * * m
Late copper statistics look a bit better. A
while back, it. seemed as though consumption had eased
off, but the deliveries are increasing again. The trouble
in the market no doubt arises from the accumulated
American stocks. Copper statistics are for the most part
a huge fake, but, as far as can be made out, supplies
increased over 70,000 tons as the result of the 1900 boom.
At the end of the year (1901) there was, it was believed,
over 120,000 tons in the States. The consumption at the
low prices is very large, but, with most of the mines still
going hard (some of them harder than ever in the hope of
levelling-up profits), it takes a long while to wipe the
slate clean.

Divs. paid by 23 Maoriland dredging co.’s in
August, £17,845. There were 119 dredges at work, which
produced 13,4050 z. ; average per dredge per month being
112 oz. This dredging business is not as profitable as is
generally supposed ; only a trifle over a third of the
product is returned in divs. For the year ended August,
Otago and Southland dredges produced 91,0750 z., and
West Coast dredges 28,2400 z. Total, 119,3150 z., of the
value of £442,975 ; average per dredge per week being
about 30oz. These figures do not include returns from
private dredges.

Chairman of Mt. Garnet told shareholders last
week that water was very short, and that unless there
was immediate rain, there was a risk that the co.
would not be in a position to meet the interest on
debentures due 28th Nov. He omitted to explain, how-
ever, that the co. had abundant water conserved, but for
the sake of an expenditure of €3OOO had failed to connect
the reservoir with the works. A reliable N.Q. paper
states that on Oct. Ist there was a depth of Bffc. of water
thrown back for nearly a mile-and-a-half, and a fortnight
before the depth was 16ft., quite enough to run the
smelter well into next year, could only half the
water have been saved in transit. It has to
travel_ four miles down Return Greek ere reaching the
receiving dam at the pump station. About half-way there
is a heavy depth of sand which had literally absorbed all
the water that had been flowing for the previous fortnight
through a lOin. pipe from the reservoir. The paper adds —
“It does indeed seem a pity that such a large body of
water should run to waste these dry times. But the only
way to avoid it was by the laying of piping at an esti-
mated cost of something Hke £3OOO, and this the co. is
not in a position to do, as all works being carried on are of
necessity of a purely productive nature.” Surely, under
the circumstances, laying of pipes to utilise this water
was about the most legitimate productive work that the
co. could undertake ! Comment on such management
would be superfluous.
* * » *
In the absence of a balance-sheet and printed
reports it is quite impossible to comprehend the exact
position of the Mt. Garnet Co., but such information as
the directors have thought fit to give reveals a shocking
state of affairs. Failure is admitted, and it is quite as.
ghastly as was that of Chillagoe. The figures supplied
show that from Ist January to 6th October, 41,500 tons of
ore were smelted. The average value of the metal is
stated to be £4 6s. per ton of ore, so that the gross value
of the product would be over £175,000, yet on working
account there has been a loss of £1350. For the
first six months the loss was £15,679, while from
Ist June to 6th October there was said to have
been an approximate gain of £14,329. There is such
a disparity between the two sets of figures that it would
be interesting to know how the approximate gain was
arrived at. Anyhow, assuming that it was a legitimate
gain, it reveals anything but the economical management
which was promised when the co. was floated. The value
of the product from June 1 to Oct. 6 was over £78,000 and
the profit was but a trifle over 15s. per ton. Position of
co. now is that after spending £62,000 on plant and treat-
ing about £250,000 worth of ore it owns nothing. All the
liquid assets in the shape of matte and surpluses
on consignments belong to the bank, and the free-
hold is liable to be seized by the railway con-
tractors, who hold it as collateral security to
the debentures which were issued to cover the
cost of the railway. So far, the mine is practically
worked out. It is assumed that there are reasonable
chances of further payable ore-bodies being found, but
who are likely to put up the money to do the necessary pros-
pecting work, seeing that a start will have to be made
with an admitted debt of £lOO,OOO ? Chairman Higgins
summed up the position accurately when he said that
“ the whole story emphasises how desirable it is to make
conservative estimates with regard to the quantities of
ore available, and to thoroughly prospect a property
before erecting extensive works and accumulating large
quantities of stores.”

re Coolgarra Tin Mine (20/9/’O2) is not correct. The par

are neither more nor less than another man’s estimates,
based on very rough and ready sampling. The only
crushing, which was of stone from No. 1 shaft, took place
prior to my visit to the mine, and returned 4 per cent,
tin."

The extent of the boom and collapse in Charters
Towers stocks is shown in this statement of the market
value of quoted stocks on dates given : March Ist,
£2,547,400; June, 19, £3,102,179; July 14, £3,622,488;
Aug. 28, £4,200,181 ; Oct. 1, £2,578,968. Taking the
market value of new co’s. (not included in these figures)
at £150,000, the total value of stocks at the zenith of the
boom was not far short of £4,400,000. The highest total
value in the ’9l boom was £3,395,348, or over £1,000,000
less than that of ’O2. The fall in the five weeks preceding
Oct. 1 was £1,621,213. There has been a slight recovery
since then, but even now on the law of investment
averages the general valuation is too high. On nett
profits the value of stocks is equal to about 12 years’
purchase, and he would be an ultra-sanguine Towers man
who would allege that there is a hope of anything like
present prosperity being maintained for that period.

*** * •
Bendigo, which used to run a close race with
the Towers, is now behind Gympie. Record for past nine
months : Gold, 136,4140 z.; div5.,£134,799; calls, £100,657;
surplus, £34,142. “ The figures afford indisputable evi-
dence of the sound condition of the mining industry.”
This is a local opinion which an outsider will hardly en-
dorse. Certainly, the third quarter shows increases on
the first quarter of gold 95430 z., divs. £23,897. The
profit for the first half of the year was only £9357. Ex-
cepting three Moon mines and two Hustler’s mines, the
field is being Worked at an enormous loss. These five
mines have almost a monopoly of the divs., and on pre-
sent outlook are likely to retain it for a long time.
Prospects of the Moon group were never better ; another
mine—the North Moon—gives promise of soon appearing
in the producing list. Last week’s divs. from the three
Moon mines, £7850. Were the output of this section
doubled, as it might well be, the figures of the field would
look much healthier.
* * * *
Burbank’s (W.A.), in driving south, has found
that their reef bends to S.E., through the Lady Robinson.
This discovery is rather a sell for Birthday, as it holds
ground on S. W., through which it was supposed that the
reef ran. Rate of development of property has been
terribly slow, as, after having been at work seven years,
lowest level is only 430 ft. Run of rich ore, from which
recent big crushings of 25dwt. ore came, has been pretty
well worked out, and prospects of continued divs. look
blue. Working costs are so high that ore of loz. grade
leaves a profit of barely 12s. 6d. a ton.
* « • •
Island Eureka, Lake Austen (W.A.) reported
to be looking better than ever, especially going south, and,
from present developments, average of 25dwt. can be easily
maintained for some months. Monthly div. of 3d. per
share said to be assured. Ore being stoped from the three
levels which have been opened up. Island Eureka South,
which is now daily quoted, is being rapidly equipped with
hauling and pumping plant, and should soon develop into a
gold-producer, judging from past results from surface pig-
rooting. Shaft down about 140 ft. In the early days no
work was done below 90ft. owing to the heavy influx of
water, so that no great depth has to be sunk to get a
good lot of backs. Shares should be a good gamble for
speculators who go in for long shots, and can lift the
scrip off the market.
* * • «
North Kalgurli (W.A.), under the management
of Henry Nutman, is gradually developing into a gold
mine. He is devoting all bis energies to the eastern por-
tion of the ground, which, prior to his time, was wholly
neglected, and is opening up the continuation of the Kal-
gurli lode in the oxidised zone with highly satisfactory
results. At 600 ft. level some patches of very rich sulpho-
telluride ore have been met, but little work on the lode is
being done at this depth, as the upper levels offer suf-
ficient Bcope for one man’s energies, and, when opened up,
should provide a lot of ore. Long ere the money pro-
vided by the recent reconstruction of the co. is expended
the full strength of the property should be known.
* * • *
Kalgurli (W.A.), publishes, for the first time,
full details of working costs : Mining 10s. Id., reduction
21s. lid., development 3s. 5d., capital 3s. 5d., and general
25.; total 40s. lOd. As the ore is worth 785., there is a
profit of about 375. per ton exclusive of London exes, and
depreciation. September yield was 37110 z. from 3701) tons,
nearly 200 oz. worse than Aug. This co. always states the
yield in standard gold. Mine looking well, and, as the
stone is stoped in places for 60ft. wide, the supply of
ore to be mined is well ahead of the treatment plant.. As
there are only 120,000 shares in the co. a quarterly div. of
2s. 6d. ought to be distributed out of current profits.
* * • »
North White Feather, Kanowna (W.A.) after a
hand to mouth existence since its reconstruction two
years ago, has closed down. Mine has been producing
about £l6OO worth of gold monthly at a cost of a,bout
£lBOO. The original prospector, Mackenzie, took it on
tribute for a year, after its first collapse three years ago,
and netted a nice nest-egg out of the venture. He wanted
to renew the tribute but his offer was disdainfully re-
jected, and a new manager appointed. The main shaft
was put down fully 100yds. the wrong side of the reef and
had to be abandoned, and since then a system of go-as-
you-please mining has been indulged in with the usual
result.
* * * *
The Associated Northern (W.A.) cut very rich
ore, apparently a second bonanza chute, in the second
bore-hole sunk. From its position, this is supposed to
be an entirely new lode so far as the property is con-
cerned. It may be the underlay of what is known as the
Brown Hill Extended lode, but is apparently much
richer than anything so far found by the latter co. This
discovery has quite decided the co. to resume sinking
the main shaft, but, as Oapt. Rodda is not enamored of
its puny dimensions, it may possibly be enlarged from
the surface first. About 2500 tons of Boz. ore are either
at or en route to the smelters, so that a div. can’t be
far off.
• * * •
Perseverance (W.A.) directors have issued a
circular in which they state that the ore reserves of the
mine are now greater than they were at the end of 1901,
notwithstanding that since that time over 100,000 tons of
ore have been treated. Only the tonnage actually
blocked out is included in the estimate. It is added—
“ The condition of the mine was never so good as at the
present time. The Sept, output (over 16,5000 z.) will be
maintained.” Ralph Nicholls, the manager, is noted for
his extreme caution, and he certainly would not subscribe
to this statement unless he was assured of its absolute
accuracy.

N.S.W. Premier See is communicating
with other Premiers about making the already-
forgotten birthday of the late Queen Victoria a
perpetual public holiday. But just now Australia
doesn’t want any more public holidays. If Mr.
See would proclaim a day of extra hard work,
with corresponding extra wages, on which day
Australia would endeavor to make £lOO,OOO
worth of goods that are now imported, he would
hit the mark much better. The great mass of
day-labor workers and piece-workers are more
than tired of losing a day’s pay on account of
somebody else’s interested loyalty.
* • * •
Murderer Tisler, hanged last Monday,
gained 301 b. in weight before execution, on a
physical diet of steak, eggs, porridge, tea, bread,
butter, jam, &c., and a mental one of religious
magazines. Tisler’s stodgy, agricultural intellect
enabled him to sleep and feed well to the last.
But for the gruesomeness of it, the situation of
Mrs. Sangal would be dramatic—husband and
lover dead, waiting till her baby be born to be
slain.
» • • •
They should ask Premier Irvine in Vic.
Assembly how long he intends to give Mrs. Sangal
after child-birth before hanging her. Possibly, as
she is a fairly robust woman, she will be able to
stand up sufficiently well to be hanged seven days
after the birth of her infant.
* * • »
Constable Johnson, of St. Kilda, Vic.,
shot by a criminal who then committed suicide,
having quite satisfied the public craving for melo-
drama by the manner of his dying, received a
highly complimentary funeral, the other day.
Men, women and children poured in from all the
other suburbs, and crushed and fought to see the
coffin carried from the door, and all along the line
of route spectators were gathered, as if it were a
royal duke or a world’s greatest songstress passing
through, instead of a dead policeman. Women
wept openly and visibly as the hearse passed by.
This is the meed of the victim of mischance, whose
taking-off fills all the romantic requirements of
the crowd. The incident must be sensational,
it must be strong in sentimentality, and
have a basis of domestic incident. The shooting
of Johnson and his amazing sprint of 200yds.
with a bullet practically in his heart before falling
dead from his bicycle was a most melodramatic
business. The victim was one of the handsomest
policemen in the force, and Lady Tennyson had
just visited the young widow. Popular taste
could ask no more. Poor Johnson was buried
like a national hero. In the words of a local
editor of Irish extraction, any man living might
have been proud of such a funeral.
» * » *
The day’s takings on the St. Kilda line were
practically doubled on the day of the funeral of
Constable Johnson, and the traffic was big on all
suburban lines in consequence of the popular
desire to view the funeral. There’s a moral here
for Minister Bent. If only a few superfluous
politicians could arrange to be sensationally mur-
dered ! Perhaps Mr. Bent would give the move-
ment a start ?
* * * •
Re the statement that the man who shot
that Melbourne policeman had been threatening
to “assault ” an eight-year-old girl, it is unlikely
that an offender of that stamp would have had
nerves steady enough to enable him to kill his
pursuer at the first shot, walk 500yds., coolly
sharpen a knife on his boot, stab himself in the
throat, and finally shoot himself instantaneously
dead. The sexual criminal is generally a quivery,
neurotic creature. The Bulletin, as a rule, avoids
dead-men pictures, but gives in this issue por-
traits of the man shot in Melbourne side-by-side
with those of the man very strongly suspected of
the murder of Constable Guilfoyle. About the
identity of the two there can no longer be any
doubt.

“ N.” writes about an increasing craze :
Any male who finds himself walking in a Melbourne
suburb—to say nothing of a public park —runs great risk
of being locked up if he gets within 50 yards of a girl-
child. Everywhere now abound the old-cat females who
see a child-outrager in every male who is not hustling
himself. Probably that St. Kilda policeman would never
have been shot if a hysterical mother had not made a fuss
over probably nothing at all. (Burglars and policemen-
killers are quite a different sort of people from child-
tamperers.) The mere fact of a poorly-dressed man being
in such a “ toney ” spot as St. Kilda on a Sunday morning
would be almost enough to raise a hue-and-cry. Sooner
or later, either men or children will have to be excluded
from the lungs of the city.
Victoria is “ a civilised country.” Yet no
one there raises a voice in protest when the
Commissioner of Railways, prodded on by Minis-
ter Bent, recommends that men should be
discharged from the railways and women put on
in their places. The Commissioner, in a report to
the Iceberg Cabinet:—
There are already 175 stations at which women are in
charge. At 159 stations no one is in charge. At the
cost of additional inconvenience t.o the public, this
system can be extended. It is considered that women
can replace men at a number of other stations, and that
at some of those where women are now employed, they
can be taken away, and efforts in this direction are now
being made.
In the name of economy, and (p save a land-tax,
the “ strong ” Government is to send men to walk
the streets, while their wives take charge of rail-
way-stations. Yet Britishers jeer at the German
who gets his wife to plough !
Minister Bent’s idea of .abolishing the
wages of luggage porters on Vie. railways and
making them dependent on tips, will naturally
make them obsequious to the rich and con-
temptuous to the poor. It is the first authorised
setting up, in nominally democratic Australia, of
the bad old English system of encouraging crawl
and hat-lifting and wealth-worship. And it’s
just the thing one would expect from T. Bent.
* * * *
Burly Minister Bent’s reformation of Vie.
Railways system begins to be amusing. Bent has
decided that “persons residing just outside the
nine-mile suburban radius should get greater con-
sideration in railway fares than those within the
oircle.” The most devoted of Bent’s constituents
happen to live just outside the nine-mile radius.

“ Buzz ” writes
about cases when
happiness descends on
a girl suddenly :
Apropos of quick court-
ships and sudden marri-
ages. In 1873, at Mackay
(Queensland), a house-
maid (an emigrant) was
coming to me from Rock-
hampton. The overseer
from the next station
was going to port in his
buggy, and promised to
bring her back with him.
They met for the first
time at 11 a.m., and were
married by a J.P., 10
miles from town, at 12.30
or thereabouts. The fact
that they did the 10 miles
in 1£ hours shows they
didn’t stop to court on
the road. He broke it
gently to me when they
reached the station, paid
the girl’s expenses, and
took her straight on,
leaving a deserted and
betrayed female (which
was me) to wash up.
What’s more, the marri-
age was a decided success
up to a few years ago,
since when I’ve lost sight
of the parties. Again, in
1884, I allowed my ser-
vant girl to nurse a
neighbor (a mother of
six), who eventually died.
Servant girl Ellen went
to the funeral to carry
the two-weeks-old in-
fant. When she returned
at 5 p.m. it was to in-
....
form me that she was very sorry to leave me
so suddenly, but she had married the
bereaved husband on the way back from the
funeral. The O.E. clergyman who read the
burial service read the inarriageservice also,
and so did two jobs in one day. The adaptable servant-
maid had seen her new husband for the first time
two days previously. This marriage also turned out
all right, in a plain kind of fashion. Probably they never
developed a grand passion, but there is an every-day sort
of adaptability—a capacity for shaking-down, like a cow
in a new paddock—about people who can get married
coming home from the funeral like that.

any case the
subject was
getting used
up.

“C.H.R.”: Concerning the remarks of
“6xB” (B. 20/9/’02):
Here is a list of meat-sales at Launceston (Tas.) :—Beef,
40s. to 455. per 1001 b., sd. to Bd. per lb. retail; steak, 6d.
to Bd. per lb.; mutton, 375. 6d. to 40s. per 1001 b. whole-
sale, sd. to 7d. a lb. retail; loin chops, 7d. per lb.; spring
lamb, 9s. 9d. to 17s. 6d. ; pork (carcase), 4Jd. to s£d.
per lb. wholesale, 6d. to 7d. per lb. retail; corned beef,
375. to 40s. per 1001 b. wholesale, 6d. to 7d. retail. And
men who are too poor to buy at these exorbitant prices
are fined if caught killing the English trout, without a
license, or with more than one rod and line. This in the
interests of the gilded amateur anglers, who leave heaps
of fish rotting along their route. Wanted badly in Tas.,
a free run to meatless fishermen and a substantial tax
on fashionable rod-wielders.
A PORTRAIT OF HER GRANDMOTHER.
(From. Phil May's “ Summer Annual.")
Consider the daily-paper manner of re-
porting the death of an old age pensioner. Two
decent old men lately died, and three papers ap-
peared with the statement “Death of an Old Age
Pensioner.” Being of no social importance they
wouldn’t have been mentioned at all but for the
fact that they were old age pensioners. And
when Brown, after loafing on Government for 30
years, is pensioned off and dies, the dailies print
the “Death of a Highly Respectable Citizen”
and say nothing about his pension.
* * * »
There are 12 men engaged mending a
Sydney suburban road, at 7s. a day. Their fort-
night’s pay, however (owing to unsettled weather),
only runs from £2 3s. -to £2 Bs. per man. Super-
intending these 12 men are—one overseer at 10s. a
day, one at Bs. a day, and a time-keeper at Bs. a
day. Their pay goes on, wet or dry. “That’s
the way the money goes.”
* • • •
One effect of the after-damp at Mt.
Kembla has been to blot out the memory of those
who recovered in hospital. “A remarkable in-
stance of the dreadful blank which the effect of
the after-damp leaves on the victim’s mind has
come to light,” says Illawarra Mercury. “ Prior
to the explosion one man had planted in a secure
place his hard-earned savings, amounting to a
considerable sum. He was rescued on the night
of the disaster and taken to Wollongong Hospital,
where he remained for over a month. All he can
remember now is that he has a lot of money put
by, but where it’s put by he doesn’t know, and
can’t think.”

’Tisn’t often the Jewish Day of Atonement
falls on Caulfield Guineas day (which is a truly
Christian way of putting it), as it did this year.
When it does, instead of dwelling in sackcloth
and ashes and repenting of their shortcomings,
the hook-nosed tribes mostly serve their God in
the Old Testament fashion—by falling on the
Gentiles and routing them with great slaughter.
The Hebrews were supposed to be fasting all day
Saturday, but if they weren’t any faster than
some of the favorites their atonement must have
been a failure.

The expected has -happened at Victoria
Barracks, Brisbane:—
Some men were drilling with their instructors, when
one private complained to the captain that the instructor
was pulling them about instead of ordering them to do
what he wanted. The captain would not listen to the
complaint, so the soldier refused to go on with the drill.
Subsequently 14 more soldiers were placed under arrest.
The men made an attempt to release their comrades,
but were overpowered.
That looks very like a mutiny. What are Col.
Tom Lay-’em-low Price and General Hutton
going to do about it ? They have 15 prisoners on
their hands whom they cannot punish, as there is
no Federal Defence law yet. Then there was an
attempted rescue, and the .rescuers “over-
powered ” (how or by whom is hot stated) —appa-
rently by. the combined force of Col. Tom Price,
his staff officers and sergeants, who must in Bris-
bane, as in most of the Australian capitals, far
outnumber the privates.

HIDDEN.
[For The Bulletin.l
In the white flower of her heart
So much of honey lies
I wonder me the golden bee
Its sweets doth not surmise.
On the red blossom of her mouth
So much of beauty shows
I wonder why no butterfly
Mistakes it for a rose.
O folded flowers of mouth and heart!
O cruel maid and fair !
Nor butterfly, nor bee, nor I,
May know the sweetness there.
MAGGIE SULLIVAN.

“Travels in Western Australia”
ing at Albany, King George’s Sound, May Vivienne
journeyed to Perth and goldfields, whence she made
excursions to objects of interest, mines, caves, forests,
fruit farms, with indefatigable industry, covering nearly
2000 miles. On the goldfields the author visited by
special privilege many of the mines which are familiar to
observers of share lists, descending deep shafts and
traversing drives and stopes (workings between the
levels), being generally rewarded for her intrepidity with
presents of nuggets, or allowed to extract specimens
-from rich ‘ pockets,’ and do a little rock-drilling herself.
The wealth and extent of jarrah, karri, and other
timber are almost incomputable, but the author was
charmed most of all by the luxuriance and abundance of
wild flowers spreading over the country. . . . This hook
is, in fact, remarkably full of information.”— Athkn.kum
London.*
Sergeant “Charley” Cawood easily holds
the record for continuity of service in N.S.W.
military forces, having joined*a corps at Parra-
matta on October Bth, 1860, and served ever since.
W. B. Melville writes from S’Africa to an
Australian friend:
I must not forget to ask you to warn workers of every
kind against coming to S’Africa. They will be doomed
to the most dismal of disappointments. I know what I
say, having been pretty well, through the place, and made
all inquiries. There is more mendicancy in Pretoria,
Johanesburg, Durban, and Capetown than in the whole
of Australia. Every second man asks a stranger for the
“ loan of a couple of bob ” —the smallest price here for a
feed. Do you want any more ?
Wild’s, in the Sydney Arcade, is the most
fashionable gentlemen’s hairdressing saloon in
Sydney. Every attention possible is paid to the
customers by six expert hairdressers. Address.—
30 Sydney Arcade, entrance from George-streeb
or King street, Sydney.*

Sectarianism is rampant in Wyalong
(N.S.W.) just now, and it only requires a Dill
Macky, or an “ Escaped Nun ” to make things
red-hot. To get a job in the mines, even, you
must be of the “ persuasion ” of the bosses. One
half of the place is a municipality; the other half
isn’t. Some want incorporation; other don’t.
Altogether Wyalong is a pleasant place to dwell
out of.
* * * •
“ Carey ” gives us the real facts :
Re your article on sectarianism (27/9/’O2). Sorry I
can’t see through the same keyhole as you do. You’re
not in the know. I am. I’ll give you some facts. Car-
dinal Moran is to bring out a cargo of arms ; rifles dis-
guised as crutches and consigned to the R.C. hospitals ;
cannons labelled “ statues,” and consigned to the con-
vents ; and stands for field guns, branded “ mangles,” and
booked to the R.C. laundries. Troops are being drilled
in St. Pat.’s College grounds, Manly. (This is a military
college.) All the debating (R.C.) clubs have air-guns,
with which they are getting their eyes in. The plan of
campaign is already settled. On the date arranged all
the R.C. bells are to be rung at 3 a.m. St. Pat.’s will at
once raid St. Dill’s, while the students of the military
college at Manly will skip across to South Head by means
of a tunnel (engineered by Tom Slattery; you will
remember that T.M.S. lately retired from his solicitors
practice ; that was in order to devote more time to his
tunnel). The boys from North Head will capture the
guns at South Head and turn them on to the city ; the
Irishmen in the garrison are to assist; also General Finn,
Paddy Crick, and Owe’Sullivan. Hundreds of tons of
foodstuffs are being stored away, which is the cause of
the high prices at present ruling. Ammunition is being
prepared in cellars under convents and colleges. A new
explosive has been invented, and beside it lyddite is a
penny hunger : a shell of “ ballystrong ” will kill every-
body within a radius of 25 miles. These are the facts. I
have some more facts when you’ve paid me for these.

SHE : “No, it was time to get up."
Extract from prize essay on the subject of
engagements from Pastor Pitchett’s son’s recent
publication, The New Idea :
Many a commonplace marriage follows a long engage-
ment. The romance, the charm of novelty have gone,
the supply of agreeable surprises is exhausted, having
been drawn upon during the years of waiting.
Just what The Bulletin often points out: the
springing of these “agreeable surprises” is re-
sponsible for a big percentage of marriages, just
to keep the population going, as it were. But
then a rude paper like The Bulletin can say a
lot of things which a nice ladies’ paper of religious
tendencies should carefully eschew.

• * * *
Melbourne’s Yarra has just added two
more victims to its list, a young man and young
woman drowned through the staving in of their
boat by a snag. Now the various powers scattered
along the river are talking of combining to weed
the gum-trees out of the stream. The river above
Richmond is full of death-traps, and young
couples boat on it in crowds on fine Sundays.
And when young couples are in boats they’re
sometimes too busy to be careful of snags.
* * * •
Babies really seem to be becoming the
fashion again. Sydney’s birthrate for the quarter
ended Sept, last was the largest since 1893, and
relatively the highest since 1897. Possibly the
departure of the last contingents and the suppres-
sion of a certain class of advts. in the public press
have had something to do with securing this result.

this year shows a comparative decrease of 2000.
Meat is dear, but one can still get sausages cheap.

The cantankerousness of the incomplete
person has often been noticed. Consider the
number of wooden-legged men who appear in the
police court in proportion to the size of the
wooden-legged community. Also note that they
are mostly charged with battery and language, or
smashing of property and language. Given a
wooden-legged man and a plate-glass window, the
chances of wilful wreck are at least twice as great
as if the man had both legs.
The other day a charitably-disposed person
found, in a village near Sydney, a family, or rather
two families, numbering ten persons, three of
them adults, and most of the children over ten
years of age, living in a two-roomed hut, with bags
and boxes for furniture. This state of affairs had
existed for some time before anyone drew atten-
tion to the danger of allowing the children to
grow up under such conditions.

It demands salaries for its officials and a news-

not ?

* * * *
Somebody has sent a pathetic letter to the
Argus re the proposed demolition of Bishopscourt,
hitherto the dilapidated domicile of Melbourne’s
Anglican Church bishops. The tearful corres-
pondent said : “ Bishopscourt is in a conveniently- .
situated central position ; there is something
historic about it. There lie the bones of Bishop
Moorhouse’s dog, Tim ; there is the old gum tree
that has seen 500 years. Shall we not say to some
purpose, ‘ Woodman, spare that tree ’ ? ”
A humorous (and true) story from a
N.S.W. gaol. A warder had been placed in
charge of two prisoners, told off to work a
piece of farm land adjoining the gaol. He fell
asleep and left his charges to their own
devices. Being honest criminals, seemingly,
they didn’t betray his trust in them, but
worked on till closing time. Then, as their
guard showed no signs of awaking, they gently
raised him and carried him back to head-
quarters.
It is not all joy being in a Royal Family.
Australian daily papers published this tip in their
cablegrams the other day :
London, 27th Sept.
It is expected that the Princess of Wales will be
accouched in November.
Now how would the average woman like that sort
of announcement ?

Conduct of the yearly Eight-Hours Art
Union in N.S.W. is at least open to question.
It is not a fair gamble, seeing that the prizes are
in ridiculous disproportion to the proceeds ; and
there is a suspicion that many butts of tickets are
not included in the lottery barrel. As the Govt,
especially countenances this affair, it should take
steps to have it efficiently supervised.

N.S.W. Arbitration Court’s recent deci-
sion raising the wages of bread-carters by ss.
weekly has been quickly responded to by the
master bakers. Bread has now been raised a
farthing per loaf all round, which means that
Sydney and suburbs are paying £lC')O per week
more for their bread because 160 bread-carters are
collectively receiving £4O additional wages.
What’s the matter with a municipal bakery ? It
will come.

“ J.R.” re the Drayton Grange Commission :
The Commission blames all concerned in the trouble
except the ship’s officers and crew. Hard to say why they
escape, since it was in evidence that some of them did a
roaring trade with the swaddies in the grog which caused
much of the mischief. Drs. Williams and Greely, by'the
way, want to furnish a “ supplementary report ” on the
scientific aspects of the voyage for the instruction of
transport officers, who would doubtless receive it with
derision. “ Science ”is wasted when ordinary common-
sense is so evidently lacking. The most surprising of the
numerous protestors against the finding is Col. Lyster,
who says he was told “ only to answer questions.” His
remedy is simple ; he can demand to be tried by court-
martial.

Ballarat.
And here:
Geelong.—An application for dispensation was
made on Thursday in the Insolvency Court, presided
over by Judge Chomley, by Victor Emmanuel Hester,
railway employee, whose debts are £55. Insolvent
was unable to pay anything since sequestration of
his estate on Feb. 7, his salary being 355. per week of
seven days. Out of this he had to provide for a wife
and nine children, the eldest of whom was 15 years,
while one of them was blind and another delicate.
His Honor granted the application.
From which it would seem that the fat persons
who are trying to squelch the Factories Act can’t
give many points to the Government at sweating.

Women who purchased at the sale of goods
said to belong to a recently-retired cleric will be
chagrined to know that few, if any, of the things
sold had belonged to him. The house was packed
with goods from a warehouse. The trick is fre-
quently practised in Sydney. A popular clergy-
man occupied a furnished house in a fashionable
suburb ; time came when he had to leave. With
his consent, the goods were sold as his, the house
being further crammed with stuff from the stores.
Some, perhaps, bought to get souvenirs of the
popular clergyman, and, if they only knew it,
they got souvenirs perhaps of the last “abbess,”
whose gilded home of sin the time-payment furni-
ture man had sold up.

A Bulletin correspondent wants to
abolish a degrading show:—
These States are fond of publishing statistics
to prove the decrease of crime, but it is a pity
they do not put their energies to the more
practical direction of hiding the criminals
they still have from the public gaze, and so
increase that decrease. Writer has travelled
ibout Australia and Maoriland a good deal of
recent years and has been disgusted and
horrified at the way prisoners—and not
always convicted prisoners—are paraded to
the public gaze. In Sydney every court day
Black Maria drives up to the front door of
the court house and collects all the
offenders—men, women and children —and
bundles them publicly into the vehicle;
ditto, when a consignment is sent off to
Cockatoo. In fact this is one of the
daily free shows for the loungers on
the Quay. Much the same thing goes
on in Melbourne. Went to Perth a
couple of years ago, and first thing
seen on leaving the railway station
was a string of men—black, white
and copper colored —chained two and
two, marching through the streets.
In Auckland, a few days back, saw
a squad of broad-arrowed prisoners
in charge of an armed warder, mak-
ing their way, during the busiest
part of the day, through.one of the
principal streets. Maoriland is about
the worst offender in this respect, and
the parade of shame is apparently con-
sidered part of the sentence, for the oily
Acting-Premier, in reply to a deputation
which protested last week against the
practice in Wellington, declared, in ex-
tenuation, that only second offenders
and those convicted of serious crimes
were so exhibited. In Lyttelton, (M.L.)
a few years ago, a gang of prisoners used
to push an empty hand-cart up and down
a steep hill all day, while in Timaru they
levelled the streets.
THE BISHOP’S DAWG.
O, woodman, spare that tree
Which dates from ages dim ;
Beneath its shade
A bishop’s spade
Did dig a grave for Tim.
Tread softly on the place
Where Tim is sleeping sound.
Disturb it not:
The verdant spot
Is surely holy ground.
The dear, departed tyke
Was not of common clay;
Full sanctified
It lived and died—
For dogs must have their day.
Now, leave poor Tim at rest
Beneath the grassy sod,
Until at last.
By trumpet blast,
He’s summoned to his God.
Amen.

Words of wisdom from a Bulletin corres-
pondent :
Why are some men such illogical fools as to depend on
religious testimonials when applying for a job—at any
rate on work where knowledge or skill is called for?
Writer had to engage some men recently on a certain
engineering contract, and among others an application
was received from a young fellow, enclosing one reference
from an engineer he had worked under ; one from a Pres-
byterian Bain-Fakir to say he could recommend his
acquaintance to any other Christian young man desirous
of forming it; another from a Wesleyan Sky-Pilot to
testify to the regularity of his attendance at Sunday
school, whilst a fourth emanated from a J.P., who simply
mentioned that he knew the applicant’s father ! Now,
the work happened to be of a kind requiring a certain
degree of skill, and had that young man based his claim
on the trade testimonial he possessed, the chances are he
would have got a job, but as he appeared to depend more
on his reputation as a regular churchgoer his application
was passed over. I required a whole engineer—not a
person who was one-fourth engineer and three-fourths
regular churchgoer and consistent believer.

are essential to perfect beauty. Lacti, the

develops and gives a charming roundness to neck
and arms. Price 7s. fid. per jar, post Bs. 3d.
