“BE SURE IT’S
CHARLES
HEIDSIECK
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, October 21, 1902.
My Dear Arini. —
Melba-mania, Sandowism, and the return of the
Premier have filled up Sydney’s hollow society
places as fully as Venus cream, or other beauty
mixtures “bring out the perfect curves,” &c. The
postponement of the concerts by the siren was a
great set-back to some who had come long leagues
to hear her, and had to return unhearing. To a
few of these Melba has written expressing regret ;
instead of a song an autograph. A relaxed throat
does not mean unriveted works elsewhere, and
Melba has been doing the local beauty-spots, and
dining with the State Govt, people and with the
Lassetters. This week the Finns (Major-General
brand) are giving her an orchid time at barracks
and picnics.
Last Friday, Sydney’s Mayoress, Mrs. T.
Hughes, gave a Melba reception at the Town
Hall, when between seven and eight hundred
persons “met” the singer. Melba stood on a
dais, Mrs. Hughes by her fawn voile side.
Madame has that security of manner that belongs
to the very successful or the very rich, and the
unflagging vitality that comes of sound sleep and
perfect digestion ; her success in life comes not of
her mere voice, but equally of her physique and
her temperament. She stood, or sat, or made
excursions from the dais ; she drank tea, laughed,
chatted incessantly ; paid the Mayoress many
compliments on the success of “the meet,” and
hugged the youngest Hughes child, a pretty boy
in a big blue tie and knickers. Her hands, .eyes,
feet, and mouth were never for a moment in
repose. For two hours she was in action, her
vitality as effervescent when she departed
at five o’clock as when she arrived at
three. With no necessity to lift up her
v«ice in song Melba had laced her waist severely,
with the result that outlines were too obviously
defined, her back rounded and her appearance
shorn of much of the grace it presents on the con-
cert platform. Among her ornaments was a
diamond chain with a great pink pearl and a black
one. When she met old friends, such as Mrs.
W. A. Macdonald (the Miss Kate Slattery of Mr.
Dan O’Connor’s panegyric), she purred over them
effusively. Mrs. Macdonald is a very vivacious
lady with a curly mop of fair hair, who might well
have carried, so Melba says, her vocal wares to
Marchesi, but married instead, and has lived
happily ever since.
Lady Forrest and Bigjohn came in for a large
share of the singer’s attention. Lady F. was in
a black lace gown (the kind of lace that looks as
if it were woven of fine silken cords). Mrs.
Bland Holt (looking capital, in spite of a frantic
hat of red poppies—a Garden of Sleep hat) was
another favorite. Melba’s brother-in-law, Mr. T.
Patterson, whom half Sydney mistakes for a
detective guarding Melba’s jewels, hovered alone
with folded arms on the outskirts of the crowd,
his gaze always directed towards the platform.
He really has the look of the detective in fiotion—
a Sherlock Holmes, mysterious-looking person.
The real detective looks mostly like a mild,
shabby nurseryman or a caretaker. A large
number of women are more interested to know if
Melba wears anything but silk tights under her
clinging Felix gowns than they are in her voice.
These Felix creations are shotted round the
trains, so they never turn turtle or bunch.
A M.L. correspondent writes: “Most mining
men in Australia know, or know of, J. W. Walker,
of this island, commonly known as ‘ Long-Drive ’
Walker, one of the foremost mining managers of
his day, and until lately manager of the famous
Waihi. His wife was a Miss Dow, a sister of
Melba’s mother, a short, plump, black-eyed,
Hebraic-looking lady. Mr. Walker’s daughters,
first cousins of Melba, are all handsome and very
musical, and one had a lovely voice, which she
merged in early matrimony.”
Madame Melba is making one exception to her
general rule to hear no budding nightingales, and
has graciously intimated that she will hear Miss
Hilda Mulligan, of Manly, grand-niece of Vincent
Wallace, warble a test-song for critical purposes.
Certain returned distinguished Coronators seem
to have earned unpopularity here on account of
their visit to the Pope. If the Barton-Forrest
route had been through Thibet, and the Grand
Llama were on view, no one would ever dream of
hinting that the politicians and their wives
shouldn’t pay that ancient institution a call. A
friend of mine, from Ballymacquew, said, in
reference to this visit: “If you go to Rome, it’s
manners to roam wid the Romans.” Among the
batch following the Australians into the Vatican
was a Presbyterian gentleman who says he is a
personal friend of King Edward, and should be,
therefore, of irreproachable Protestantism.
The Barton-Forrest quartette looks somewhat
travel-worn and worried. Reports say that dur-
ing the Coronation rush the Cecil wouldn’t
answer your bell till you had rung 10 times. Also,
it made you wait from 15 to 30 minutes for your
chop or soup. The Forrests ranged themselves at
the Queen Anne Mansions, where for two small
rooms and a bath cubicle they paid 21 guineas
a week. Everything else, from a cup of tea to
the electric-lighting, was “a hextra.” Bigjohn,
who was ill most of the time with influenza and
bronchitis, is 2st. lighter than when he left. Per-
sons with cocked hats, sea-legs, and epaulettes
sat amongst the medicine-bottles all day and
talked defence of the Empire at him, squadron
subsidies, and mobile forces for service beyond
the Commonwealth. Lady F.’s Coronation gown
was white lace over white silk. The quartette
ran up against several of the type who, five years
ago, invited the Premiers to visit Ireland, and
“by all means to wear their native costume.”
Returning by America, they thought it very
“fresh” to be charged in New York equal to
four guineas for the carriage of their personal
luggage from the steamer to the hotel. The For-
rests return Westrali a wards about the New Year.
The Bartons’ house at North Shore is let until
December, so they are living at Petty’s Hotel,
which is much better than a hollow log, any
day.
Miss Mu ret, a member of the “ Messenger
Boy ” co., has married Charles Trood, who takes
the part of Cosmos Bey. Her sister, Mrs. Willie
M‘Evoy, came over from Melbourne for the
wedding.
Captain Goulding (of the trader General Gor-
don) who was killed at San Francisco through
10 tons of lumber falling upon him, and Captain
Green, of the trader Mt. Stewart, who narrowly
escaped a like fate, were both married to Aus-
traliennes. Mrs. Goulding was a Miss Ella Evans,
of Sydney, clever and musical, and had been mar-
ried only two years. Mrs. Green was a Brisbane
girl, also married but a short while. Both ladies
were so bactty injured by the falling of the lumber
that it is feared they will be permanently orippled.
Captain Green’s escape was due to his returning to
the ship for his wife’s purse. Makes you think of
the Persian line: “On two days it steads not to
fly from thy fate, the appointed and the uuap-
pointed day.”
Grace Palotta, writing to a Sydney friend from
Lucerne, where she is stopping with “the little
mother,” describes the beauty of the place, which
she evidently considers finer than Sydney. Also,
she mentions the many street singers, men and
women, with beautiful voices. One in particular,
was “a ragged girl singing outside a cafe the
Jewel Song from ‘ Faust ’ with so lovely a voice
that if it were heard in Australia you would—
well, she would not be any longer in rags or singing
in the streets.”
Miss Fitzmaurice Gill, who starts on the
prickly-pear track of theatrical management,
financially well backed, was for some time with
the Bland Holt co. She also played with Nance
O’Neil, and between times did some literary work.
Miss Muriel Milson, only daughter, of Mr.
Alfred Milson, who was married to a son of the
late Mr. Alick Osborne, of Moss Vale, last week,
is a grand daughter of one of Sydney’s oldest
living identities, Mr. James Milson. The family
at one time owned all that part of North Shore
known as Milson’s Point, and other properties.
A sister of this Osborne bridegroom has recently
married cricketer Jessop, who, when he is not
cricketing, is a master in some college or other.
Concerning matrimonial affairs, the engagement
of Miss Alice Mack (a sister of Louise) to a White
Star officer is announced. She will probably
return to Sydney to be married.
Sydney’s first Dental Hospital was officially
opened last Friday by State Governor Rawson;
as a matter of fact it has been doing good work
quietly for something near a year. The president
is Dr. R. E. Magnus, and it is supported by nearly
every reputable practitioner in tne city.
This is from The Stage, a prominent English
paper:—
Te Rangi Pai, the M.L. contralto, gave her first concert
last Tuesday before a fashionable and numerous audience.
The concert was under distinguished patronage, most of
the Westralian Premiers were present, including the
Right Hon. R. Seddon.
_ A woman, well-dressed and bearing an extraor-
dinary likeness to Melba, got out of a hansom and
went into an oyster-shop in Pitt-street. A crowd-
let gathered, and when she re-appeared she knew
from the comments for whom she was being
mistaken. Glancing angrily round her, she
re-entered the hansom, and remarked, “ Melba
(sniff)! Where are your eyes ? I’ve got a neck
(sniff) — her head grows out of her shoulders; mine
grows on top of a neck ! ”
The new Vice-Admiral (Fanshawe), who will
sail these summer seas early next year, is about
50 years of age, and comes of a Royal Navy stock,
which started its Viking march in 1815, and has
given dozens of men to the navy. The Vice-
Admiral’s newly-appointed Flag-Captain is an-
nounced as Commander Purefoy Richard Purefoy.
Sounds like a Chevy Chase ballad name ; for that
matter, so does Fanshawe. Mr. Purefoy is
reputed to be immensely wealthy, and we are all
delighted to hear it ; poor people are not respect-
able, especially on the quarter-deck of a flagship.
A Brisbane correspondent writes :
“Brisbane owns a warbling boy (the son of a
well-known station owner on the Dawson), who
can imitate scrub magpies, butcher birds, doves,
laughing jackasses, etc. Brisbane’s enterprising
Mayoress has originated the idea of asking Melba
to shine at a garden fete in aid of the Lamington
and Children’s Hospitals —tea, Melba and dis-
tinguished company for an honest sum, and the
warbling boy thrown in. He is to be stationed
in a bushy tree, and the diva will be entertained
immensely by his repertoire, no doubt. Maurice
Evans —as the child is called —is an adept at
imitating a cat or dog, and a cat and a dog fight
also. It is to be hoped that he won’t treat Nellie
Melba to all his tricks at once.
“Sir Herbert Cherrfiside, his sister-in-law (Miss
Webb), and the A.D.C., paid a flying visit to
Stanthorpe, and one or two other obscure Queens-
land towns, last month, with the idea of choosing
a summer residence far from the madding crowd.
They accomplished a large tour of inspection in a
short time and in a business-like manner.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent•
“There was much blow about the Caulfield Cup
this year. The wind set the trees of the course
into convulsions and made havoc with the Cup
crowd, tearing skirts, sweeping chiffons over
heads, dragging at hat anchors, scattering fluff
and millinery scraps to Heaven. Women on
arriving at the course, dashed straight away to the
dressing-room and there fought desperately for a
place at one of the three big mirrors. They
removed hats and veils, struggled for the one comb
provided by the V.A.T.C., caught baok the run-
away tresses, pinked and powdered openly with
the chamois that performs this dual office, replaced
hats and veils, sniffed the salts, tried the scent,
and, refreshed, valiantly faced the terrace.
“Vice-Regal parties were lost to sight in the
grey stormy scene. Now and then, the greater
Excellency’s silk intellect-lid, askew on his
billiard-ball head, showed up on the Terrace.
Lady Silverlocks and her daughter sported cream,
the young person adding stole-ends and vest of
blue to her outfit. Also, she wore a small hat in
blue tones, to match her eyes. The Guv’nor
strolled on the lawn with various people. Sassiety
is murmuring. It is beginning to think the
Clarkes are too naice. What avails it to Mrs.
Philadelphia Brown to hobnob with their Exes, if
some quite outside person is treated just as cour-
teously a few minutes after ? The Clarkes seem
determined to be naice to everyone.
“The shearers’ wives were, as usual, the most
gorgeously dressed at the Caulfield Cup. None
of the sassiety swells can come near the bookies
for ostentation. A real lace dress cover, with silk
groundwork, chiffon feet billows, flying pelerine,
flirting elbow frills, and hand-painted decorations,
was the most telling turn-out of the day. Garish
motifs and bizarre garnishings were the means
sought, most popularly, to attract attention.
Frocks in good taste, like the white and pin-priclc
voile of Mrs. Arthur Moule, the strawberry
foulard of Miss Rita Kiddle, the black-and-white
delicacies of Mrs. Percy Chimside, the cream trifle
of Mrs. Ernest Raleigh, the fruity red silk of Lady
Williams, were drowned in the blare of the
Hebrewesses trumpeting their husbands’ wealth.
“ The new figure with its pouched blouse struck
the eye everywhere at that race-meeting ; and the
ugliness of the fashion came home to the under-
standing. When women, with blanc-mange- like
figures, sport the kangaroo dress-front, the side
view is that of a bulky flesh-slip—one wonders if
the fall has been finally arrested or whether a cas-
cade of flesh wijl not tumble down over the knees
in a moment or two. False hips wagged where
Nature was scant. The pneumatic air-pad had its
day out, or rather in. The fair every year become
more and more upholstered. Hair frames, “trans-
formers,” false switches, artificial teeth, improved
complexions, paddings, and shoe elevators are the
necessary accoutrement of many smart women.
And the effect of so much artificial aid appears in
the anxious frown, the ill-tempered air, and the
apprehensive care that mark all these painstaking
slaves of Fashion.
‘ ‘ Melba’s dinner entertainments are described
as ‘sensational.’ The hostess herself designed the
table, a sort of circular affair built round a
double-flowering peach-tree. The company sat
under the pink-blossomed boughs. The chairs
were gilt Chippendale. At a dinner given by the
Mars Buckley family to Melba, the flowers were
hugega ( y tulips, their colors shown up by shaded
lamDs of the same hues. This decoration glowed
on die polished dark-wood centre of the table, the
white hem-stitched dinner-oloth only bordering
the edges.
“In their welcome address, old schoolmates
congratulated Melba upon always having guided
her steps by the college motto: ‘ Olme Hast, Olme
Rast ’ ! Between themselves, they agreed that
the only motto observed by the Nellie Mitchell
they had known was *On for larks ’! Melba still
loves games of all kinds. She doats on ‘bridge,’
and plays it all day during long train-journeys.
She takes more care of her figure and her voice
than we have been led to suppose by English
scribes. Nowadays, Melba touches neither tea
nor bread. Bread is fattening and tea bad for
the digestion.
“ Melba had her first and only outburst at her
old school shivoo. Since that day she has drawn
in her horns and been silent at public functions.
At the Conservatorium kow-tow she wasted no
words. A shrug of the shoulders, a laugh, a
gesture proved a substitute for speech. ‘ I can
only sing my thanks,’ she said once; ‘ I cannot
speak them.’ Someone, with a quick hope danc-
ing in her eyes, cried back, ‘Sing them,’and all
are agog. There is a pause. Melba bridges it
with a smile, the appropriate shrug, and turns to
hear someone ask her to have tea.
“ Girls who have been flattered from time to
time, at ‘ Stonington,’ by Miss Clarke seeking
their musical help for her accompaniments, will
be nervous now that Melba has performed the
duty. At Government House, t’other day, when
a little party collected at St. Kilda-road mansion
to meet Melba and her harpist, the singing of
Miss Clarke, accompanied by Melba, was an item
on the impromptu programme. Melba quite
gushed over the familiar effects of Marchesi train-
ing displayed by the local Gov’nor’s ewe lamb.
“Mr. Justice Williams has been spouting to
the country lads and lasses on the burning subject
of ‘ Love and Marriage.’ Great excitement pre-
vailed on the announcement of the lecture. The
local hall (up Hamilton way) was packed. It was
in the main a stale drone talk. Once, however,
he differed from .St. Paul, and called his Biblical
view a ‘bestial one,’ and the orthodox sat up.
Later, when he declared that, 4 in order to protect
her skin and hair’ from marital slave-drivers,
woman ‘developed guile, deceit, and untruthful-
ness,’ there was another unpleasant sensation,
but no one heaved a brick at the placid judge.
His recommendation of a good life partner was
summed up in three qualifications—good health,
good temper, good sense. Sensible doctrine,
certainly, but no judge should lecture, or act, or
sing, or fall off a bicycle, or play the piano in
public, or walk the tight-rope, or engage in pie-
biting contests. In fact, he should do nothing
which exposes him to criticism or to competition,
lest the man who can beat him at bun-eating or
singing acquire thereby a contempt for the law.
There’s only one attitude for a judge on or off the
Bench—that of frozen dignity.
“ At the opening of the State Reform Parlia-
ment (Oct. 15) it was hard not to wink the other
eye at the so-called ‘Reformers,’ who, lolling in
the lap of luxury themselves, menace the poor
little ‘ perks ’ of railway porters, and shake their
heads ominously at old-age pensions and free
education. Where was this rage for economy
when our legislators fitted up for their own con-
venience the western annexe of the Exhibition
Building as a gorgeous fac simile of Parliament
House proper, at a cost of more than £30,000?
Viewing the ruby-velvet fittings of the Legislative
Council Chamber, the crimson carpets and picture-
strewn walls of the smoking corridors ; the library,
with its blue-books (of both sorts, dry and other-
wise), and new novels; the hot-water pipes all
over the building; the lace and buckles of officials;
the uniformed attendants; the silver-gilt mace
(said to be more valuable than that of the House
of Commons) one seemed straying in a land where
thousands grow on trees.
“ There was an extra glossiness about Duncan
Gillies when he fitted himself into the Speaker’s
chair behind the gilded bauble. He was self-
possessed and self-centred. After a respectful
glance at the President—his vis-di-vis —his shrewd
old eyes fell to a stony stare at George Reid’s back-
head, with the fox-colored hair descending in-
terraces to the locality of the baggy neck.
“ At the Wrixon 'Parliamentary afternoon tea,
the grey-haired hostess, in dark-blue, splashed
with white, had, as a co-entertainer, her tall,
fresh-faced, unpretending step-daughter. Old
hands compared the ‘ opening tea ’ with those
given of yore by ex-President Zeal. His table-
flowers were always arranged by the George
Jenkins household—not by caterers who mix red
gladioli, white daisies and green rosemary-in equal
quantities and tie the blend up into cauliflower
posies.
“Mrs. Alec. Peacock looked attenuated. Her
recent illness and subsequent nursing of her
spouse have reduced her to an almost transparent
slimness. Back in her old place, as if George
were still in power in the State House, sat Lady
Turner, in exuberant spirits. The Premier’s wife
aimed at youthfulness and gaiety in a charming
turn-out of turquoise-blue with a tropical hat.
Lady Clarke presented a pleasant Excellency in
bottle-green.
“State Commandant Maria Gordon cut a
tremendous dash among the polished brass
escortees of the Excellency Clarke at the Parlia-
ment opening. Glasses help his vision, and seem
to take the gilt off his warlike trappings. Fancy
Mars in spectacles !
“The collapse of Mr. Alfred Deakin was ex-
pected. His family have been concerned for some
time, and towards the end of the Federal session
some of the Deakins were always in the gallery.
Mr. Deakin began to lounge languidly; a debili-
tated tone crept into his speeches, and the mighty
effort he made for that Ballarat review of Com-
monwealth measures finished him. He collapsed
next day and just dragged himself through one

worst enemy. And, as Edward Dyson once wrote,
the worst of modern diseases is Nervous Ability.
“Melba has forwarded word to Cave Hill—the
Lilydale station —that she is going up for a day's
fling. The poetio-looking place where the famous
hams are cured is putting on its best bib and
tucker for the event.
“The softgoods people do things in a large,
handsome way. On Friday Mr. and Mrs. Reid
brought off their party at the Balwyn snuggery in
fine style. ‘Be a business man, and all fat things
shall be added unto you,’ as Marcus Clarke used
to say.
“Much ado over the smart marriage of Mr.
Clive Money Miller and Miss Clare Wilson, of
Toorak, last Tuesday. The go and dash of the
bride prepared her friends for a special array of
chiffons. No one was disappointed. Scots’
Church, Collins-st., has seldom seen such
a triumphant bridal array. Miss Wilson
wore ivory satin, and fine lace adorned the
corsage. Four stunning girls befriended the
bride and helped in the picture. Misses Eva
Miller, Rita Kiddle, Fanny Wilson and N. Mac-
kinnon—the ladies in question—carried off their
topaz-yellow and ecru lace dresses with a dashing
air. Their hats were of tuscan, trimmed with
roses. All four heads had elaborate coiffures—
dressed low on the head, like the bride’s. Guests
mostly sported the same hair mode. After a
festive tea at South Yarra the cooing doves set off
for Sydney with the intention of storming Mel-
bourne again Cup-time.

“ Iceberg Irvine is as cold and hard and rigid,
as his wife is soft and gentle and docile. She
has a fancy for rainbow colors—wearing the
brightest blues and pinks. Her smile is propitia-
tory. She desires to please. Her greatest vanity
is high-heeled, silver-buckled, gaudy shoes, which
she wears even for day functions.
“ The wife of the Acting Gov.-General is not
maintaining the formal dignity so closely observed
by Lady Hopetoun. She attends private parties.
The other day she turned up at ‘ Cloyne,’ when
the very elegant wife of the Waxelleney feted
Melba at a tea. Tenor Kirby was once more
switched on for a number. By this time all the
professional warblers in Melbourne are emphatic in
their opinion that the Sassiety pet has had more
than his share of patronage, favor, and good for-
tune. At the Madden party the guests were
armed with autograph books, opera scores and
poetical volumes for Melba’s big, hasty scrawl.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
N.S. W. can boast, as a record, of a K.C.
who has been ca. sa.’d by a Chinaman.
Tom Bent has one real distinction. He
was the first Victorian native to be elected
Speaker.
Let B. R. Wise look out for falling
bricks: Mr. Young, M.P. (N.S.W.), has styled
him “one of the noblest sons of Australia.”
Rhodes was ever weak on details. In
codicils to his 1 will in his own hand he mispelt
the names of Rosebery and Michell, two of his
executors. He wrote “Roseberry” and “Mitchell.”
If King Dick Seddon goes to S’Africa as a
politician (English papers assert so with strange
insistence) you can bet that there will be some
connection between the move and Rhodes’bequest.
As soon as Bigjohn Forrest got back,
Major General Hutton definitely announced that
he didn’t intend to resign. The pressure, which
had become almost unendurable, had been re-
lieved.
The traditions of the Speakership are
redolent of dignity, honor, courtly gentlemanhood,
and lofty principle. In Victoria, the last four
Speakers’ names are Matthew Davies, Bent,
Mason and Gillies ! !
Said General Hutton to Federal Minister
Lyne on one occasion recently : “Oh, but we
can’t fly in the face of the Imperial authorities.”
“Pardon me,” said Bill; “you, plthaps. can’t,
but I can.” True bill.
J. H. Smith, hunted out of the S.A.
Railway Commissionership a few years ago so
that the politicians might have more room to nose
around, has gone to Natal to report on the
railways there, with a view to taking command.
The Leahy family will soon own Queens-
land in fee simple. Janny bullies the Assembly
while brother Pat blarneys it with his flute-like
soprano, and the “ bhoy ” Danny is coaxing
Lockyer electors with soft soap and flapdoodle.
Almost the first proceeding of the new
Victorian session was ex-Premier Peacock's
apology “for certain references to the personal
appearance” of Mr. Bent, when he said the
Government had taken that person’s “ great, fat
carcase ” to its bosom.
The Laborites who are arranging John
Burns’ lecturing tour of Australia can’t make up
their minds whether they ought to “run” him
themselves or let the cunning hand of Smythe
manage the reins, as it wants to. But there
should be no two opinions about that —the cunning
hand should have the job. The Labor bodies can
organise for him just the same; but Smythe,
working on his own lines, will get in not only
coin but also men the others could never move.
Run by the unions, the lectures will be mainly to
just the men who go the new John’s way already;
and the more of the other sort that he can call to
repentance the better.

“I was engaged to him once , and I can tell you
he isn’t such a fool as he looks.”
“ Oh, he couldn’t he or he’d never have got away.”
David Syme has now quarrelled with every
decent democrat in Yic. politics, and has allied
himself with the Tory crew. “If the Conserva-
tives serve our turn, why not employ the Con-
servatives ? ” says David. But it is David who is
being employed by the Conservatives. David is
getting old.
A sister of W. H. Richman, the Adelaide
squatter who recently bequeathed large sums to
local charities, married Sir James Fergusson,
M.P., when he was Governor of S.A. Fergus-
son’s first wife died in Adelaide, and the lady he
wedded in Adelaide died in Ceylon, his present
wife being the third.
Divines Harper and Tait, who had steered
the Presbyterian College in Melba’s school days,
were not present at her welcome, “owing to
extremely important business at the General
Assembly,” then sitting. The gushing fell to the
lot of the present staff, who, not having known
the guest in her girlish days, had no scoldings to
take back, no solemn reproofs or awful warnings
to wipe off the slate.
“P.X.”: “Hop” will surely be rein-
vigorated by the knowledge that at the Austra-
lasian Church Congress, in Adelaide, an arch-
deacon, no less, gratefully recognised the artist’s
" contribution to the sentiment of mission work.”
Every time “Hop” draws a fat missionary, a
cannibal, and a bubbling pot, the plate, it seems,
comes back from its next trip flooded with tears
and heavy with silver and gold.
Dr. Roth, N.Q. Protector of Aborigines,
is most assiduous in dealing out marmalade to his
departmental chief, Home Sec. Foxton, His
official reports and speeches nearly always include
some flattery for Mr. Foxton ; and, as that gentle-
man isn’t too popular in most quarters, he
appreciates the unusual incense.
Obit., Charles Davis, solicitor, of Sydney,
at 63. The many years Davis struggled to make
the Sydney Quarterly Magazine a literary voice
were the tragic ones of his life. The small pam-
phlet was a chronic sufferer from literary anaemia,
but the more it languished the more tenderly its
proprietor and editor nursed it.
Chief-Justice Darley’s last letter shows
that improving health allows him to contemplate
a visit to Ireland. It is 20 years since his last
visit, on the occasion of the death of his mother.
The Darleys are strong in Irish legal and social
circles —almost all Conservatives; “Progressive
Conservatives,” to use their own phrase.
Westralia’s first-born white died the other
day, aged 72. She was the daughter of Captain
Roe, for 42 years the local Surveyor-General, and
founder of a good old “family,” whose members
are still to be found in high places. The daughter
became Mrs. S. P. Phillips, and that ,\vas the
beginning of another strong branch in ’Groper-
dom.
Melb. Argus, respectable as an ancient
courtezan at a christening, published an obituary
notice of Zola in which there was no sort of
reference to “L’Assommoir,” “Nana,” or any
other blue-tinted offspring of his brain. The
British reputation of Zola is based on “ L’ Assom-
moir.” Every Total Abstinence Society in Aus-
tralia ought to send condolences to the widow of
the man whose' novel was dramatised into
“Drink,” by Chas. Reade.
George Venables, well-known in all big
printing houses as the one-time champion type-
setter of Australia, is now part proprietor of a
Waikato (M.L.) paper. Venables has been a
lightning snatcher in his time, and has put up no
end of records. He picked up all the points of the
game on one of the big Melbourne dailies. In a
type-setting championship at Melb. Exhibition he
beat all comers, with a good margin to spare.
“Melb.”: Allow me to urge that Vic.
National Gallery ought to possess the admirable
life-size oil-painting of George Higinbotham, by
Miss Thomas, now buried at Brighton Public
Library. It should be replaced at Brighton with
the portrait of Brighton’s brass god, T. Bent, now
at Melbourne Aquarium, in his Speakerial robes,
such a speaking likeness that you expect it to
blurt out, “ What the devil are you looking at ? ”
Melba still retains the bold scrawl, pecu-
liar to generations of Melbourne Presbyterian
Ladies’ College girls. She dashes off a splendid
note, concise, yet kind, on Frenchy note-paper.
Every sheet and envelope has “Nellie” in gold
letters on a round disc of pink, blue or lilac
enamel. The paper is grayish with just a sugges-
tion of the same color as the enamel seal. The
cunning color tone of this paper is another instance
of French observation of Nature’s methods.
Swinburne, who beat Barbour, the figure-
man, out of the Hawthorn (Vic.) Assembly seat,
is chairman of Broken Hill Water Supply Co.
It is significant, by the way, that the men who
make the money out of B.H. investments go into
Vic. Parliament; the men who do the “ hard
graft ” in the B.H. mines go into N.S.W. Parlia-
ment. Of the former are Knox, M’Bryde, Orr,
Staughton, Swinburne; of the latter, Cann,
Ferguson, Thomas (now in the Reps.), Sleath and
Williams.
Speaker Jenkin Coles indignantly denies
Minister Lyne’s assertion that a Federal legislator
was “ unceremoniously bundled out ” of Adelaide
Parliament House because he unwittingly strayed
into the quarters reserved for State members.
This was one of the arguments used by the Home
Secretary in favor of the establishment of Federal
political clubs in the various Australian capitals.
As a matter of fact, Federal legislators are given
the use of library, reading, smoking and dining-
rooms in the Adelaide marble palace.
Not many young Australians have been
struck by harder luck than Adelaide Porter, who
won the Elder (Adelaide University) Music
Scholarship some years back, but who, perman-
ently injuring her right hand, had to return from
Europe when half-way through her studies. Yet,
though disabled, she has persevered, and at a
complimentary concert lately she gave a remark-
able performance with the left hand only.
With the death of Rev. S. M. Creagh, of
Strathfield (Sydney), Australia loses a splendid
linguist, notable in particular for his
studies of South Sea dialects. A mis-
sionary of tongues and creeds he would
dispute as vehemently over the signifi-
cance of oa or ao in a Polynesian ex-
pression as over the Athanasian creed.
He spent most of his life in the
Islands and pushed the Bible at
naked natives in their own lingoes.
He also translated “Pilgrim’s Progress”
intS Kanaka tongues.
PROFESSOR MILLER, AUSTRALTA’S
VETERAN STRONG MAN.
The late Mr. Justice Hensman, of Wes-
tralia, was somewhat of a Radical in politics and
a strong supporter of female suffrage. He was
speaking on the latter subject in Perth one day,
and was putting in a claim on behalf of the other
sex for equality with rnan in intellect,' judgment
and courage, when a piercing shriek from near the
entrance of the ball suddenly broke into the thread
of his discourse. Inquiry elicited that one of the
ladies had nearly stepped on a frog, and that she
caught sight of it as it hopped out of her way.
Little Chief Justice Way has been very
subdued during his present stretch of office as
Lieut.-Governor of S.A., and has apparently
realised at last that there is no local demand for
the highly-flavored and over-colored butter which
he is accustomed to purvey to the people in his
public speeches. He speaks now almost as seldom
as Lord Tennyson did, and he is manifestly
attempting to abate the florid character of his
utterances. The result is that his presence as a
would-be controller of thought is less bitterly re-
sented than before.
When Carlo Dani, the well-fed tenor with
a soft suggestion of maccaroni and butter (lots of
butter) in his voice, was in Sydney, he was rushed
by girls, who kissed him and begged his auto-
graph for photos of him which they had bought.
Unlike the Duke, Carlo didn’t give away photos ;
he only gave away the signature. Now, those
girls will be well pleased to find that anybody can
get Carlo’s signature and portrait out of a news-
paper medical advt. —with a full description of
what he was cured of. The B. trusts, for the sake
of the remnants of Australian loyalty, that the
Duke wasn’t cured of anything while here.
Rumor varies as to Bent’s motive for his
quick-firing volleys of sensational proposals and
edicts in Vic. railway management. With one
voice she opines that he is after the snug £3OOO-
--year nest of the Commissionership, which has
not been occupied permanently since Mathieson
left; with another she hints at revenge on rail-
way men for support given to a successful oppo-
nent at a bygone election; and, thirdly, she
suggests a mere ambition of Tommy’s to have two
columns allotted to his doings in the morning
dailies, which have treated him as a somewhat
greasy back-number for so many years.
Mr. Tom Mann has accounted for the
water in the beer :
He had for two and a half years kept a public-house at
96 Longacre, London. While aeting as landlord he took
up the position of organising secretary to the National
Democratic League and placed a man in charge of the
public-house as manager. On his return the inland
revenue officers served a summons upon him to appear at
Bow-street police court. Though he had had nothing to
do with adulterating the beer, he was fined £lO for
having it on his premises, and it was quite right that he,
as licensee, should have been punished.
But, after all, a pub is a bad investment for an
active labor leader—almost as bad as a terrace of
houses.
N.S.W. Labor leader M‘Gowen has now
learned that “a dignified differentiation of private
from public concerns” is sometimes misunder-
stood. When B. B. O’Conor asked him if he
wasn’t building his new terrace of houses by con-
tract, Mr. M‘Gowen replied that the matter was
not one that concerned the House. As a matter
of fact, he explained to friends later, the houses
are being built by day labor, and he merely
wanted to snub Mr. O’Conor “as a protest against
the habit of invading the private life of the Labor
members.” But what a poor spec, for a Labor
leader is a terrace of houses ! How will he ever
be able to collect from recalcitrant tenants ?
The row between Deputy-Speaker CTann
and Sleath, M.L.A., which bubbled up in N.S.W.
Assembly, t’other night, and ended in Sleath’s re-
moval, runs back to the days, 10 years ago, when
neither of them was in Parliament. Both, in
those times, were working miners at Broken Hill;
and when the first Parliamentary nomination
loomed both had expectations. But though Sleath
was the more effective man with a crowd, his
suave opponent could handle an executive better;
and so Cann won and went to Parliament while
Sleath stayed at home and presently went to gaol
—over the big strike-trouble, and only reached
Parliament via the “jug.”
N.S.W. acting-Chief Justice Stephen has
put on a little stiffness and strength with his new
dignity, and isn’t quite the meek target for rowdy
barristers he used to be. He still needs, however,
Borne of the spirit of his father (in that regard).
Once Darvall and Windeyer (afterwards Judge)
quarrelled before old Alfred Stephen in court.
Windeyer called Darvall a liar ; Darvall promptly
smacked him in the face with a heavy brief.
Chief Justice Stephen sent them both up to
Darlinghurst gaol for contempt. Darvall took
his punishment like a gentleman, and on his
release called on Stephen and apologised. Win-
deyer never spoke to the judge again.
An Australian writes from S’Africa :
Concerning W. P. Schreiner, ex-Premier of Cape
Colony, Australia has quite a wrong impression. He is
credited with wonderful strength, subtlety and caution.
As a matter of fact, Schreiner is the jellyfish of S.A.
public life. He is an Imperialist of the most accommodat-
ing order. He was in office when the Briton-Boer trouble
loomed on the S’African horizon. Had he shown back-
bone, Chamberlain almost certainly must have heeded
him. But directly Schreiner saw that the Imperial
administration preferred the mailed fist, he lapsed into
silence and inactivity. A protest from him would have
helped the peace cause wonderfully. He has German
blood, but “Dutch” courage. England has largely
to thank him for her mountainous war debt, her sorrow-
ful death roll, her abortive victory. The Dutch have to
thank him for their unparalleled miseries.
Mr. Danyell O’Connor, re Friedman case
in N.S.W. Leg. Assembly (amid much loose elo-
quence about “dishonoring the memory of his
father and mother,” about being “rocked in the
cradle of liberty,” about “Moses and the Tables
of the Law ”) unpacked his heart of this woe:—
But when the jury came to examine the goods as they
were unpacked, of what description were they ? They
came from one of the greatest firms in England. A man
who supplies not only England and her colonies, but
other parts of the world, who, as a knight, has been
specially decorated by the King, who has been sheriff,
and who has been Lord Mayor of London, and who is a
special friend of the King—"this gentleman is the head of
the firm who sent these goods out to New South Wales.
They are untouched, and they are of priceless value.
The horror of it—buying cheap goods which were
manufactured by a man who “is a special friend
of the King ! ”
Like hundreds more, “Jack” Reside,
Labor member for Hannan’s
(W. A.), who fell dead in the
street the other day, owed
a good deal to Bendigo
School of Mines. As a
youngster in a battery
be studied at the
school, and got an
engine-driver’s
certificate at so
young an age
that his mates
talked about
him, and
soon made
him their
union presi-
dent, and
that gave him
a lift when he
went over to
the West. So
far as his inti-
mates knew, he
had not the faint-
est idea that he had
any constitutional
weakness ; yet, at
only just past 30,
he had to die on
only a moment’s
warning. Though
he didn’t take it
as a virtue, he
used to claim that
on his mother’s
side he was of the
family of Watt, of the steam-engine.

WATSON’S WHISKY.
Aboriginalities.
To Contributors.— ln the ease of ethnological and
natural-history items , etc., always give date and
place of experience ;or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations ivill have scientific
value.
A ghastly drought item. At Bowie station
(172 miles N. of Jericho, C.Q.), the family has
had to desert the homestead and camp at a water-
hole, three miles off. Last month, over 300 head
of cattle perished in and round that one hole,
while other holes on the run can show the whiten-
ing bones and sun-dried carcases of hundreds more.
Twice in the last three months the stockmen have
found saddled-and-bridled horses dead a few miles
from the tracks, but they saw nothing of the
riders ; and, in that now waterless country, search
is impossible, beyond the radius of a day or two’s
travel. When rain does come, there will be some
dreadful finds and dreadful stories to tell of dead
men’s bones and the wanderings of thirst-mad
travellers. During one of the severe duststorms
now so prevalent in C.Q., a hot blast as from a
furnace swept over this station, withering what
little vegetation remained and driving the stock
so mad with terror and thirst that they ran them-
selves to death or took refuge in all sorts of strange
places. _ Three days later, w r hen the homestead
was visited, a mare was found in the dining-room,
dead ; and, in the washhouse, three more horses
lay dead—one of them being an “ outlaw ” they
had never been able to get near since she was
branded.

lhe vagaries of Australian climate i
Geraldton (N.Q.) is suffering (so the residents say)
from a drought. They have had no ram for jive
weeks. A great part of Western Queensland is
also drought-stricken—no rain for nearly five
years. Breadalbane station, however, near Bird-
ville, got 4in. a few days ago in one storm. A
leading hotel in a West Queensland town, usually
rented at £lO a week, is at present leased out at a
nominal rent of one shilling /

. . . ~ .
The experiment of the traction-engine
which was proceeding with many vicissitudes and
10 tons of loading from Broken Hill to White
Cliffs (8., Oct. 4), was a rank failure. The un-
made track proved far too great a strain, and the
camel-man had to hooshta to the rescue.
“N.Y.”: When the sluice-gates of the
weir, near Elmore (Vic.), were opened recently to
send water down the Campaspe to Echuca (for the
benefit of the runs abutting on the river), and
before the water reached the backwaters and billa-
bongs, those parched places became alive with
hundreds of tiny grey frogs, while the croaking of
larger frogdom gave warning to the ants, beetles,
etc., which were seen making for higher ground
as fast as they could go. It took five days for the
stream to reach Echuca (filling all the river-bed
holes on the way) about 25 miles distant.

Wragge’s Stiger - vortex rain provokers
were set off at Charleville, t’other day. Two of
the guns burst, and remarkable were the escapes
from sudden death. One of the guns was a
veteran at the game, having burst at Mt. Morgan
some time ago. They’ll bring rain one day, those
guns—a rain of bits of Wragge.
•** • *
Upper Hunter (N.S.W.) graziers have
concluded that prickly-pear as a fodder is a
failure. Cattle, although they will eat it, cannot
subsist on it for any length of time without losing
condition.

Writes “ Equa ” :
Salt cannot be removed from water by filtration
through sand. The fresh water found on sand-hills on
the coast is soakage from the hills or surrounding
country finding its way to sea-level. Know a system by
which thousands of gallons of excellent water can be
obtained from salt lakes or sea water. No incrustation
can occur, and no cooling water is wanted to condense
the steam. This method is subject to a patent. Have
seen a plant at work on W.A. lake water carrying 30 per
cent, solids; boiler, distilling lOOOgals. daily, showed
absolutely no scale. Condensed water is thoroughly
aerated in a process which is cheap and the method of
working so simple that fresh water can be got at any
time for stock, so long as there is a supply of salt water
to work upon. There is a fortune awaiting the man who
will take up this matter and work it. Writer will gladly

“ Glenara ” : The Melbourne press talks
largely about the “ national calamity ” that will
ensue if mallee settlers are not given seed, hone-
feed, etc., to enable them to take up their weary
old struggle again. But, according to Fenton,
the total yield-value for the past seven years from
the cultivated portion of the 11,000,000-acred
mallee has been less than £250,000 per annum.
Surely Victoria ought to make sure of better
results than this from its rain-makers, irrigation
schemes, etc., ere it sends farmers into the mallee
again. Above value, without deducting expenses,
works out at about £2O per annum yearly. Verily,
the mallee and Wimmera are spoon-fed, State-
supported, coddled industries.
■» * * *
The successors of old Joe Bosisto in the
eucalyptus oil distilling business lately gave the
Victorian mallee farmer some information for the
good of his health. Joe’s successors held that, if
he is the ruined, drought-stricken man the papers
says he is, ho himself is mostly to blame. The
eucalyptus people, whose distilleries are right in
the mallee, possess some 40 horses and 5 cows, to
provide which with feed they farm about 100
acres, and in the wheat portion of this they
expect to reap about 8 bushels to the acre ! They
have the same soil and the same rainfall (4.17 in.
to date) as their neighbors, and they do not
irrigate. Says the manager
Proper fallowing, combined with the use of the seed
drill and manures, is the only way to farm in these dry
districts. We will not have an utter failure even this
year, dry as it has been. Last year we experimented on
30-acre blocks with three different classes of manures, and
proved the superiority of Florida superphosphate for our
particular district. A few acres I put in this year with-
out fallowing is looking very miserable and going back
daily, while the other part is holding its own, with a good
chance of improving l .
It is the story told by the Agricultural Depart-
ment experts over again—if the land-holders
would sell half their land, and buy manures and
farm, the remainder they would be a vast deal
better off.

Larry Dooley kept a pub. on
M.L. West Coast in the early days.
He was totally “no scholard,” and
had an original way of recording
accounts. Each debtor was repre-
sented by a potato, kept on a shelf
under the bar counter, and, with the
aid of certain hicroglyphical cuts,
Bung Dooley was enabled to arrive
at a moderntely-correct estimate of
amounts owing. On one occasion
“the gintleman that pays the rint ”
invaded the sanctum and made
short work of the potatoes. A
customer called for his bill, and, on
looking up his “ books,” the loss was
discovered and lamented by Larry.
“Be me sow], if the pig hasn’t
scoffed up me lidger! ”

“ Croyez ” : Be par anent
bandicoot (8., 2/11/’01). First ate
bandicoot in 1858, and still eat
it with avidity when I can get
it. There is nothing to equal
it when fresh killed and dressed.
The flesh has a delicious aroma.
Was once in a S. Gippsland district
where they were plentiful. Got a
dozen rabbit-traps and baited with
apples, peas, carrots, etc. Used to
catch them all ways—by legs, tail,
forearm—and one left a part of its
nose in the trap. Had many with
smashed legs, etc., but the nose
finished it—the traps were too cruel,
and I pitched them into a deep
waterhole; caught with springers
thereafter. By the way, what is the
difference between bandy and small
kangaroo rat ? Never could get any-
one to define it
* * * *
“T.G.8.”: I was keeping an
eye on the sheep, reading The
Bulletin meanwhile. Combo, the
nigger, was asleep, which he had no
business to be. Presently, hearing a
strange noise, I looked round and
saw the tail of a large brown snake
disappearing up Combo’s trouser leg.
Combo was awake, but was main-
taining an intensely reticent atti-
tude. After a deal of trouble the
snake managed to crawl under Com-
bo’s belt—which was tight owing to
Combo having just had dinner—and
after ages, it seemed to me, his head
appeared through the opening at the
shirt-neck. Meanwhile, Combo was
growing old before my eyes, but he
preserved a sphinx-like air of uncon-
sciousness. I was afraid to bash the
snake for fear he would bite the nigger, and waited
as patiently as I could for him to get about clear.
Then I hit out wildly three times, hitting Combo
twice and the snake once, and when the snake was
quite dead Combo sat up and made this memorable
remark : “ My word ! that fellow’s breath bad.”
Be pelicans’ eggs. “D.H.R.” (B. 20/9/'02):
“ Junius ” : Plenty to be got on Bulloo Downs station,
near Thargomindah, S.W. Queensland. In good seasons,
round the edges of a lake there, I have seen hundreds of
eggs.
“ R.E.K.": On an island in Boola Boolka Lake, on
Tolarno station, 60 miles from the Darling, pelicans’ eggs
are to be found in thousands when the lake is full of
water. The eggs are white, and larger than a goose-egg.

“Werrimee”: Re pelican eggs: “Nym-
sie” (8., 4/10/’O2) has made a most remarkable
ornithological discovery. What ordinary observer
would have guessed that that highly-respectable
and sedate-looking bird produced eggs at one end
and chickens at the other? It makes t&e old
tradition look less ridiculous, that the pelican
feeds her young on her blood. The white pelican
makes its nest in reedy marshes, and in the tall,
mangroves that cover the floating beds found in
most 3 lakes. Hidden deep in these mangroves
there are often broad, clear pools of water, the
ideal home of numerous kinds of waterfowl, and a
great variety of nests and eggs can be found in
season around the margins. The black-and-white
pelican, which frequents the same secluded haunts
as its white cousin, builds in trees, and the wood,
twigs and leaves with which the nest is con-
structed would fill an ordinary dray. Have found
them mostly in low tea-trees, and on one occasion,
at Woodlands, near Casino (N.S.W.), only a mile
from the homestead.
BERTRAM MACKENNAL'S BUST OF
MADAME MELBA .
A queer tale of a “ wild man ” comes from
the Tas. back-country. A prospector named
Rose was near Howell’s Plain, on the head-waters
of the Mersey, when he saw a tall object like a
“hairy man ” creeping along the edge of the plain.
When Rose stopped the creature jumped into the
myrtle forest and disappeared. More recently a
stockrider named Shaw took refuge in a hollow
tree during a snow-storm. As he entered a “ man
covered with hair” ran out of the opposite
opening, and cleared towards the plain in
a succession of springs. The tracks were
afterwards found to measure Oft. between each
mark. Shaw describes the “ thing ” as an elderly
man, about 6ft. high, covered from head to feet
with long hair. Howell’s Plain, the haunt of this
“ monster,” is a white grass marsh, almost treeless,
two miles long by J mile average, lying between a
low range and the "Mersey River. Just north of
the plain, in the fork of a myrtle, two musket-
barrels were found some time ago, the stocks
having rotted off. How they got there is a
mystery, but that they are in some way connected
with the “ wild man ” is now believed locally.
Possibly they belonged to him before he became
wild and grew his mat of hair.
* • • *
A little exciting diversion comes the way
of the bush pedagogue now and then. T’other
day a furious female rushed into Hindmarsh
(S. A.) public school, and demanded the immediate
release of her son from school duty. Being in-
formed that the boy was kept in to do an “impo-
sition,” she yelled, “If you don’t let him out at
once, I’ll sliow yer wot I can do ! ” She then
sparred up, and began to hit out furiously;
and when the astonished, teacher skipped behind
the desks, she shied the ink wells, and the school
manual, and the chalk-box at him. In the after-
noon si io returned, reinforced with her other half,
who knocked the pedagogue down, chased him
among the pupils, and over the desks, until he
finally escaped into the paddock. That was his
story. Her yarn was to the effect that he had
punched her in the face and loosened her back
teeth, and, after tearing her blouse and bedraggling
her generally, had pulled her at full length along
a form by the few hairs she had left. She had
then sent for the old man to protect her, and had
shied the school property at the ferocious teacher
in self-defence. The P.M. accepted the teacher’s
version, and fined the belligerent couple £4 12s.

ANOTHER SPRING POEM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I sit amid the carcases of sheep ;
Around me blowflies musically sing
A requiem o’er the ghastly rotting heap,
And I—l read a page of Bards on ‘ ‘ Spring.”
A bare, brown Continent around me lies,
Drought-smitten, aching from the sun’s fierce sting ;
And green’s a color that these poets wise
Must see in readers when they chant of Spring.
For nowhere else on this sun-smitten isle
Can it be seen—the Crow is chosen King,
And lie’s the only thing that I’ve seen smile
When thousands questioned: Where the deuce is Spring?
MATCHBOX.
THE SHEARING AT DYINGFAST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Now, this is a tale I heard last week,
In a pub. where beer flowed fast,
From shearer Brown, who arrived in town
From a run called Dyingfast:
“We reached the shed with pockets slack,
But a smile that was good to see.
The boss came down to the little town,
Said ‘ Men, now listen to me :
“ ‘ Ten pounds a hundred’s the rate I give ’;
Then up went a mighty cheer.
He walked away, then turned to say :
• There’s only one left to shear.’

* * * •
The A.W.U. organiser, who, as a rule,
hates “ smoodging ” worse than sin, has in hand
just now a job that turns even his tough stomach.
Under the new dispensation he has to go out
enrolling the men that, only the other day, he was
fighting all he knew, and whom in a good many
cases he still loathes; and he has to get them
inside a union which, in a good many cases, he
loves. “ With them in it, and part of it,” one
said, the other day, “it won’t seem the old
A.W.U.; and yet I know it’s got to be done.”
Well, perhaps it will not matter so much, after
all, if it isn’t the same old A.'VWU. The A.W.U.
could only win hitherto by its members sticking
together; with the Arbitration Court in fair
stride, it will be able to win if its case is good,
even though some of its members are, as the
others think, a bit weak in the back and gone in
the knees.

“Wanda,” re method in story, “Myall
Tragedy,” for persuading Australian “niggers”
to guide white men to water in dry country :
Such method was common during early pioneering
and later gold-prospecting days of Westralia, A day in
the blazing sun with mouth and throat filled with lake
salt (part alkali) generally proved an effective form of
“ moral suasion.” Sometimes the “ nig ” was solid grit,
and refused to divulge, in which case he was commonly
murdered, but the fact was seldom reported in the morn-
ing papers. Two prospectors, away east of Ooolgardie,
captured and held a nigger for 48 hours (about 115° in
the sun, no shade ; night heat average about 80°) without
water, at end of which time the prisoner showed signs of
a physical break-up, but still “no surrender.” (There
was no salt used on this occasion, none being handy—for
a wonder, in that country.) Prospectors, not aspiring to
murder, then released their prisoner, thinking, any-
way, that his thirst would send him straight to water,
while his physical weakness would admit of his being
followed, but the blackfellow circled and doubled about
for yet another half-day, and then dodged his pursuers
through a clump of the impossible gimlet-wood. Same
summer, owing to soaks and rock-holes being used up by
the desert rangers, several of the wild blacks were forced
into the goldfields, to cadge their water from the whites,
some of the older and weaker ones perishing by the way.
For this reason the blackfellow objects to reveal his
tribal water supply. Have often been tempted to try the
salt and sun-bath method of inducuig nigger to show
water, but refrained, as, to a person in whom the bump
of brutality is not perfectly developed, it is not so simple
as it may seem. The writer could always find water in
that county, when, by seeing nigs., he knew it to exist.
**. * •
“Chass.”: Be possible volcanic happen-
ings in Victoria (B.,"ll/10/’02)
I have been over the whole chain as far north as Mt.
Hamilton, and have explored caves in various places ;
without exception, they were as cold as a refrigerating
chamber. A volcanic hill close to Skipton has a cave
which is the home of thousands of bats. Tons of fairly
good fertiliser have been collected from it. Mt. Hamilton
has a crater about 100 ft. deep with a shallow lake in it,
and there is a narrow rocky cave on the south side into
which one has to be lowered by a rope. About a mile
below the hill is a small basin in solid rock, with a narrow
bridge of stone like a handle across it, and a perennial
spring of fresh, cool water in it. Further north, small
volcanic hills without craters extend till they strike the
Grampians. Victorian past volcanic eruptions have all
been comparatively trifling, and the Argus scribe who
predicts a renewal of fiery activity must have let Dr.
Torrey get on his nerves.

“ Buzz ” re the
Kanaka question:
Apropos the great
Kanaka mortality in
the earlier days on
N.Q. plantations.
Many causes combin ed
to raise the death-
figures in those old
days. Of these, home-
sickness and death by
the hared of an enemy
(usually entered as
“ by peradventure ” or
“visitation of God,”
in the coroner’s
minute - book) were
perhaps the most po-
tent. A batch of boys
would be made up of
natives of five or six,
and, maybe, a dozen
different islands, hav-
ing each, perhaps, va-
rious tribes with noth-
ing in common but
bad blood. Also, as
no care was ever taken
by the agents to see
that brothers, fellow-
islanders or tribesmen
became the servants
of one master, or were
employed in the same
district, it often hap-
pened that two 01-
more brothers parted
immediately upon ar
rival, and never met
again.
Under these circum-
stances, life, with the
unaccustomed • and
continual work, be-
came unbearable, and
“ boys” (often middle-
aged men, with wives
and families in ’the
islands) would turn
their faces to the wall
and die in from 24 to
48 hours, of sheer
misery and hopeless-
ness.
Poison was another
prevalent cause of
Kanaka mortality.
Boys would suddenly
develop symptoms of
rapid consumption,
and die within a few
weeks. At “ Mag-
nolia,” near Mary-
borough, in ’7B, five
fine, healthy Tanna
boys died thus within
three or four weeks,
and several other boys
at other plantations in
the district died in
the same way ; about
20 thus perishing during that one year. Present
scribe nursed two of them, who protested. to the
last that they were dying of poison administered
by a murderer sent from the islands to kill off the whole
of their tribe. Mr. W. Boughey, of Magnolia, tried hard
to clear up the matter and find the culprit, but he was
laughed at for his pains, as the doctors were quite satis-
fied that the deaths were due to phthisis.
But mark. About six weeks before the first of these
deaths took place (on the next plantation) I was one day
cutting a Tanna woman’s hair—which stood a good four
inches above the cranium—when I came upon several
tinv bundles of dried herbs wound closely round with
fibre. The woman explained that she was a medicine-
woman, and that these were her stock-in-trade. Now, as
all the victims were Tanna boys this incident afterwards
gave me food for much serious reflection.
A peculiar find of a
watch was made by a
drover taking cattle
from Echuca to be fat-
tened on a Gippsland
run. He had noticed
something on the horn
of a big red beast but,
as the animal was wild
and could notbehandled,
did not investigate.
Later on it was found
that the article in ques-
tion was a silver watch ;
it was secured to the
horn by a steel chain,
and hung close to the
bullock’s ear. How the
watch came there was
at first a mystery, but
one of the hands subse-
quently remembered the
narrow escape of a way-
side publican who had
been rushed by a beast
in the mob. The man
escaped unhurt, but the
bullock had hooked his
watch out of his pocket
and got away with the
trophy, without the pub-
lican being made aware
of his loss.

“ C.H.L.”: Re con •
version of sawdust into
fodder (8., 4/10/’O2). The Berlin Horse-Tramway
co. has fed its horses for years on cakes prepared
from sawdust by a chemical process which con-
verts the wood-particles into sugar, and which is
not made a secret of.

An ancient lately told a South Australian
land board that he held a block once before and
lost it ; but, he added, the new man wasn’t hard
on him—let him and his wife and children live in
the dairy, and when that wasn’t convenient for
all of them the boys had to move out to the
reaping-machine.

Does any church ever part with any
money? A member of the wealthy Esterhazy
clan (Count Paul of that ilk) made over a sum of
£30,000 to the Jesuit Order when he joined its
priesthood. Now that he has tired of his pious
dissipation and renounced the priesthood. Ester-
hazy wants his money back, and can’t get it. He
is no worse off than if he had presented £30,0,00 to
the Salvation Army on being gazetted a “ cap-
tain.” The Society of Jesus and the Society of
Booth have the same big bump of acquisitiveness,
and about the same minor bump of charitableness,
but Esterhazy threatens to take his “gh vance”
into the law courts all the same. If he badly
wants the money he had better go into the Con-
verted Priest business and start a lecturing tour.
“The best remedy for the ‘barmaid
trouble, if there really is any barmaid
trouble,’ ” writes The Bulletin’s Loudon corres-
pondent, “is the automatic bar. There is one
here, just opened on the Thames embankment,
but being teetotal—as yet—it doesn’t drink. In
appearance not unlike the get-up of an American
soda fountain depot, the goods (eatables) are
arrayed in lifts behind a glass window. Drop in
threepence, the lift descends until one shelf is in
front of an aperture, and you take out a tray
holding a cup and saucer, bowl of sugar, and a pot
with dry tea in. The hot water is near by, and
your tea, after ‘ drawing, ’is ready. Ices, smokes,
buns, sandwiches, &c., likewise. Eor drinks only,
drop in coppers to price, turn tap, and fill the cup
or glass beneath. A cup of te-a, coffee or chocolate
goes at one penny a time, and, as there are no
waitresses to pay, shows a profit. Oh, for the
automatic public-house which never, by any
chance, would call you ‘Fairy ! ’”
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-st., Melbourne, is the largest buyer of
Opals, Sapphires, Pearls and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find it to their advantage
to send their pa,reels direct to him. The firm has
a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, which is a sufficient guarantee
that they will be honorably treated. A trial par-
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance,
is solicited.

Headache is most often caused by con-
stipation or a torpidity of the liver. When
the bowels are constipated the poisons in the
excreta are absorbed into the system and
cause headache. When the liver is torpid
and fails to perform its functions, the bile is
absorbed into the blood resulting in head-
ache and often in a derangement of the
whole system. Two of Chamberlain’s
Stomach and Liver Tablets taken im-
mediately after supper will correct the dis-
orders of the liver and move the bowels.
This should be followed by one tablet each
day until the bowels become regular.
Sick Headache arises from a disordered
condition of the stomach and a torpid liver
Take two of Chamberlain’s Stomach and
Liver Tablets; they will cleanse the
stomach and tone up the liver to a healthy
action. Those who are subject to such
attacks usually feel dull, have little or no
appetite, and are constipated the day before
the attack comes on. In such cases the
attack may be warded off by taking three
tablets as soon as these symptoms appear.

The Inky Way.
Mr. Sam Cook, in objecting to tell the Select Committee
on newspaper trains the circulation of S.M. Herald,
was on safe ground. In the case of Parnell against the
London Times, the circulation of the Times was the
cause of extended argument, and the court decided that
if the proprietors admitted a “considerable” or “very
large ” circulation it would not be necessary to state a
number.
• * • *
Outlaw : Comparison of advt. pages of Melb. and Sydney
dailies (“ Inky Wav,” July 12) would have been more effec-
tive had allowance been made for difference in length and
width of columns, sizes of type, &c. To decide a wager,
the advts. in Agf, and Argus of Saturday, May 31, were
carefully counted, result being : Age, 4600 ; Argus, 2880.
In this case, the cols, numbered respectively 102 and 92,
affording no criterion of the Age preponderance.
Sydney daily papers are cutting down expenses to meet
the newspaper postage. The size of papers is to be
materially reduced, and by some journals “the depart-
ments ” will be retrenched, such as Chess, Fowls, Dogs,
Parsons, &c.
• * * *
There’s an uneasy feeling among Melbourne pressmen
because the dailies are suspected of an intention to
“ retrench.” One daily paper emissary worked up some
hundreds of “reform petitions” in the country, and,
before he could get back to ask for the boss’s congratula-
tions, got the sack.
* * • *
It would be interesting to know how much the two big
Collins-street dailies, which are howling so fiercely for
retrenchment, pay to the Victorian Railway Department
for the carriage of their papers on the lines. “Paper”
trains leave Melbourne at an hour that suits the paper
proprietors probably better than it suits any other users
of the trains. On the ordinary trains that carry the
papers they have the pride of place in the guard’s van,
and are the first “ goods” delivered at the stations. The
guards assist the circulation of the papers to an extent
that is probably little known, by distributing them to
employees, settlers, &c., along the line between stations.
All this may be necessary—is necessary—for the public
convenience ; but do the proprietors of the papers pay
sufficient for such services rendered?
• * * »
“ Cohen and Harmsworth have bought the Johannes-
burg Leader.” That is a significant bit of news for Aus-
tralian journalism. In the nature of things Cohen and
Moses and Harmsworth and Pearson will one day turn
their attention to Australia. What cha .ce would the
average Australian daily paper have of competing with
enterprising capitalists who understand that the art of
making newspapers is by spending, not saving? The
cream of the various staffs could be drawn away in a
month from most of the existing proprietaries;
the work of a great body of writers who, whilst
not shorthand writers or regular journalists, can
produce brilliant occasional articles could be utilised, and
a decent cable service instituted. The result would be
a newspaper which, from the point of view of readable-
ness, would make any existing daily look foolish. And
the daily newspaper proprietary does not, as a rule, pro-
vide by its policy any reason of patriotic feeling which
would lead its readers to prefer it to a better paper run
by foreigners. The Australian daily paper is only Aus-
tralian to the extent that it is published in Australia. A
Cohen or a Harmsworth paper would be just as Austra-
lian, and if better, brighter, more up-to-date (as it would
be with intelligent expenditure) would win the pennies
and the advertising and give another great industry over
to the outsider. Australian newspaper bosses are con-
sidered cute men, but it is too much in a grubbing and
economical kind of way ; their policy is yet to be tested
in th" fire of really vigorous up-to-date opposition.
» • • *
David Syme’s apparent knuckling to Fat, Esq., at the
present juncture is not reflecting itself in his attitude
towards Chamberlain and co. It is long since the Age
has displayed so strong an anti-imperialistic quality in
its leaders. The paper that barracked with a sort of
Berserker fury for contingent after contingent to go
farm-burning and fowl-stealing in Africa, is hotly opposing
Brummagem Joe’s new Auxiliary Squadron plan which
Barton is bringing back in his hat. David has started
out to barrack for elective State Governors, too.
* • * *
Sydney D.T., July 31, had 3J columns about the hunt
for the murderers of Constable Guilfoyle. That afternoon
came the Mount Kembla calamity, and the issue of Aug. 1
disposed of the incidents of the chase for the criminals in
14 lines. It’s the papers that make the news. Give a
dog-fight three cols, with double-line headings, and the
tram-passenger will discuss it as eagerly as a European
battle.

The King, the politician, and the Saviour.
• • • *
A feature of N.S.W. journalism, fostered probably by
free postage, is the “ trade journal.” Several large firms
run papers to advertise their businesses. Certainly the
best of them is the Golden Fleece, the proprietors of
which made the experiment of putting a literary jour-
nalist (F. J. Broomfield) in Charge, with happy results for
the reading public.
* * * •
The Deniliquin (N.S.W.) Chronicle ceased publication
a few months ago, after a career of over 30 years. Died
of thirst practically. The drought kills newspapers as
well as sheep.
* * * *
Recently a N.S.W. country paper wanted an “ editor,”
and in response to a letter of inquiry as to the duties,
salary, &c., one of the applicants received the following
reply :—“ Dear Sir, —I hardly think the billet would suit
you, or you it. What we want here is a man who can
report, fix up a leader now and again, pick up stamps
when his services are required, read the proofs, take a
hand at machining, collect and canvas, and make himself
generally useful. If you know of a likely young fellow
who wants breaking in, who will come along for thirty
shillings a week, we will guarantee his fare (second-class)
if he remains six months.”
* * * »
David S.vme is the subject of an interesting notice in
the somewhat belated and mostly wearisome Cyclopaedia
of Victoria, edited by veteran journalist James Smith.
King David sat for his photo for the Cyclopiedia, so he
presumably supplied his own material for the biography.
It states that when Ebenezer Syme died in 1860 leaving
his brother sole boss of the paper “the fortunes of the
Age were at their lowest ebb . . Mr. Syme advocated
Protection at a time when the commercial element not
only controlled the markets and dominated the channels
of advertising, but also exercised its sway over local
politics, the importing interests being almost strong
enough to crush the paper out of existence. . . During
the time Mr. Syme’s paper was incurring the antagonism
of vested interests by supporting a liberal land policy and
advocating Protection the Age was not a success
financially. At length; early in 1868, its proprietor
ventured uponthe bold policy of reducing the price of
the paper from 3d to Id. The effect of the change was
that . . . in a few months the circulation rose from a few
thousands to 20,000 a number considered very large in
those days.” After all, the story of King David’s
greatness boils down to the simple fact that he reduced
his paper to a penny some 25 years before the Argus
followed suit. If the fatinen importers of 1868 had
pooled the necessary cash for a half-penny daily rag in
opposition to the Age there might possibly be no
David whooping for a disguised Tory Ministry to-day.

Veteran writer James Smith now does the bulK of the
first-night notices for Melb. Age, as evidenced by the
liberal supply of Shakespearean and other quotations. It
is years since the Age possessed any regular critic. Vari-
ous member's of the staff have done the work at intervals,
but they all settle down eventually to political writing,
that being the best way of reaching the heart of boss
Syme. t
“Outlaw” tells this really elegant lie: “Genial Sam
Winter, who edits Melbourne ’Erald while the irre-
pressible Col. Reay does ‘ special commissioner’work in
England, with added worries, has increased his stock of
objurgations. T’other day the ‘ boss ’ was in the throes
of writing a sub-leader, when he struck his gong sharply
for the messenger boy. There was a new boy on hand, and
he didn’t know enough to run for his life when he heard
thatbell. The editor had cursed creation and hit the clapper
a dozen times before the new boy got a move on. The
final smack at the gong was disastrous for the boss, who,
instead of hitting where he meant to, impaled his left
hand on the copy-spike. When the boy wafted himself
up and asked politely, ‘ Did you ring, sir ? ’ he got for
answer—* Did I ring, you blanky blanky ! Send me
a blanky undertaker I ’ The boy, electrified into action,
left in one leap. What the boss said when a man in a
black suit came upstairs five minutes later, and men-
tioned that he was the undertaker and understood that
the editor wanted him, simply can’t be printed in a
family journal like The Bulletin.
P # P P
Acting-Premier Deakin receives pressmen with a breezy
geniality, which serves him well, for when pressed with
an ugly question, he invariably turns it off jokingly.
“ You had a bad time over the fodder duties,” insinuated
an interviewer one day. “ Yes, that’s because they gave
us so much chaff,” replied Deakin. In this frivolous way
he often avoids expressing a serious opinion. Barton has
not the same happy knack of evading awkward interro-
gatories. He is liable to become irritable, and is far less
approachable than Deakin.
“ Gumleaf ” (Vic.) : The political machine masquerading
in the guise of a newspaper is certainly not an educator.
To educate means to develop and cultivate the mind —
to teach men to think and do for themselves. The aim
of the political newspaper-machine is to hustle men into
believing its political creed without thinking, so that it
may have a firmer hold upon them for its own aggrandise-
ment. The instruction given by the newspapers is like
that given by the priests and parsons a few centuries ago.
And it is given for the same reason—to bring in the
pence. Indeed, the daily newspaper is fast stepping into
the empty shoes of the priest and parson in the matter of
controlling the minds of the people. Political freethought
is what Australia is just now badly in need of. The
people have for the most part broken away from the
religious boss, only to place themselves under the thral-
dom of the political newspaper machine. And their
second condition in worse than their first. The religious
boss worked chiefly upon one only of men’s weaknesses—
fear. The political newspaper-machine works upon
the whole gamut of men’s baser qualities. It is
always ready to sool the big dog on to the little one,
apart from the merits of the quarrel. In Victoria nearly
all the newspapers went frantic in their endeavors to set
the public against the public servants when the Govern-
ment proposed to rob the latter for the benefit of the
former. The public had the most pennies. Among other
things, too, it threatens to sweep the genuine newspaper
man off the face of the earth. There will soon be no use
for a “ news-getter” in any newspaper office in Australia.
The man who knows what news is, knows how and where
to get it, and how to dress it up so that it will be of in-
terest to the greatest number of people, will not be
wanted unless he is willing to become a petty political
intriguer.
• * * *
“ Gorolver ” : Re “ Kiwi’s ” and “ Quilp N.’s ” remarks
about copyright in Maoriland : On the Order Paper of
the House of Representatives the following appears :
“35. Mr. Guinness to ask the Colonial Secretary—(l)
Whether the Government has received any communica-
tion of the Law Lords of the Imperial Parliament amend-
ing the Copyright Act? and (2) do they intend during the
present session to introduce a Bill amending the law with
a view of granting further protection to authors and the
holders of copyright of,literary and dramatic works, from
having their property pirated or performed by persons
without obtaining the necessary license from the holders
of such copyright ? ” In Maoriland there is probably more
pirating of the work of English and American authors
than in any other country, and the foregoing question
appears to be aimed at that evil. A local writer recently
sent stories to two Maoriland weekly papers, and received
in each case a polite note stating that the editor would be
pleased to print as honorary contributions. He could not
expect payment, said the blessed editor, when he had the
whole magazine literature of the world to select from.
Substitute “steal” for “select,” and we have the whole
thing in a nutshell. But the editors are not to blame.
The editor of one local weekly paper once told me that he
wished he had power to pay contributors. These
“ weeklies ” are run on most mangy lines.- The fiction is
“selected,” the cables, &c., are reprints from a daily,
usually run by the same firm ; the social notes cost about
£1 a week, and athletics are supplied by enthusiasts free
(in return for one copy of the paper). Of course, if every-
thing were paid for half the papers would succumb (good
job, too). If the Government can be induced to introduce
a Bill for the promotion of literary honesty there will be
rejoicing among authors on both sides of the world.
