POLITICAL
POINTS
The Sydney Metropolitan Branch of the
A.N.A. has just met'and declared a small deficit
for the year. There can be no further doubt of
the purely national character of this organisation.
It has not yet been decided to float a loan to pay
off the deficit, but doubtless that will come.
The territory whicli Jenkins Iscariot, of
S. Aus., proposes to give away, not in return for
a transcontinental railway, but merely as a bonus
for building the uncompleted section of the line,
is greater than the combined areas of Victoria and
Tasmania, and one and a half times as great as
Maoriland!
The late R.C. Archbishop Vaughan peri-
odically voted for and sang the praises of the
budding freetrader, G. H. Reid. “The Arch-
bishop is for him. Read this letter—and this
letter ! ” were the cries of Reid’s election-agents in
those days. Now they will take round a Dill-
Macky letter.
The “ Secession ” movement, which has
already been jeered out of two Parliaments, made
an appearance last week in N.S.W. Assembly.
Eden George brought the secession idea forward,
and a thin House hurried out to have a drink,
and left him talking to the furniture—which
couldn’t get away.
Melb. Herald, which is ferociously Kya-
bramitic, draws the line at cancelling Execu-
tive Councillors’ railway life-passes. Fink, the
jumping-Jack of Jolimont, who, during a momen-
tary spell in his acrobatics, chanced to be a
honorary Minister, and accordingly became en-
titled to a free pass for life, is not only, at
time of writing, a whole-hog ’pootra, but a con-
trolling influence in the counsels of the “im-
partial but not neutral r vendor of Melb. evening
garbage.
A WARNING.
Minister Bent has deprived the Vic. railway employees
of their half-rate and privilege tickets (even in travelling
to work). He has done away with their allowances for
fuel, light and water. And yet, on Premier Irvine’s
intervention, he withdrew his proposal to abolish free life-
passes to Executive Councillors.
PREMIER IRVINE: “ Now Tommy, kill as many
porters and common cleaners as you want, but
remember this is the close season for fat men! ”
Sydney D.T. ferrets out, and prints with
joy, a Canadian paper reference to Premier Bartpn
as “ looking like a R.C. priest.” Evening News,
of the same city, drags into an editorial foot-note
on titles an allusion to “ the Pope’s medal which
Mr. Barton received.” By such means is the sec-
tarian flame industriously fanned.
The Rhodesian and Kimberley gangs, who
conspired to suspend the Cape Constitution, were
circumvented by “Toby” Barton. Premier Sprigg
assured present writer that Barton’s grasp of con-
stitutionalism and subtle advocacy of retention
overpowered Chamberlain. On the other hand,
the Imperialistically-sodden Seddon went with
the gold and diamond bugs ; but he was snuffed
out.
Last year one Klein, supposed Bessara-
bian agent, worked the Philp Govt, at his own
sweet will. Klein, having represented that a
considerable number of Bessarabians desired to
settle in Q., was supplied with a free passage to
that State, was carried free over the local railways
in various directions, and received a free passage
back to London. To accommodate Herr Klein
the Philp Govt, also passed a Bill to make con-
cessions to his compatriots who were represented
as yearning for the flesh-pots of Bananaland. For
months now nothing has been heard of Klein ;
the Act has lapsed, and the Agent-General mourn-
fully reports that Klein had negotiated with the
British-India Co. for passages from Odessa, but
his menu was so elaborate that the co. could not
undertake the passages at the stipulated price.
Mr. Dick, M.P., scored in N.S.W. Ass.,
when he challenged the Govt, to refer to the
‘‘ 1J L an Committee the accounts connected with
ue ’- le R °yA visit, the Contingents and
the Resumptions. "’ Those are the four extra-bad
financial smells kept bottled up at the Treasury
just now; and not until there is a change of
Government will the exact truth as to them be
known.
As showing how the Public Service Board
does not control N.S.W. Public Service, the
i ncreases in salaries for the present financial year in
public offices, outside the Board’s jurisdictio - j
amounted to some £63,000. So when the Bor d
claims that increases under its control amount io
only £90,000, it is easily seen that the Board
itself is powerless as a check against extravagant
employment of Govt, servants.
Minister Bent’s Victorian railway per-
centage reductions are scooping in the pool with a
vengeance Recently a casual hand in the depart-
ment, rated at 10s. a day, but not in regular work,
had, at the end of the fortnight, 3s. 9d. to draw
(some2hrs. work after 5 p.m.). But because he
would have earned over £125 a year if he had been
employed all the time he was docked of a per
centage, and the unfortunate casual received the
munificent sum of 3s. 7d. !
Federal Attorney-General Deakin dropped
a delicate hint to the Q. secessionists at the Barton
welcome when he stated that Separation would
mean absolute ruin to the sugar industry in that
State. In the event of secession on the ground of
Kanaka labor on the plantations, Federated Aus-
tralia would have no object in differentiating
between Q. sugar and that grown by colored labor
outside the Commonwealth. And a high duty on
its staple industry would soon convert the piebald
crowd now running Philp, into rabid Federationists
again.
Mr. Lesina has been suspended from
Queensland Assembly for accusing the Govt, of
corruption ” in giving huge advts. to little rags
with a circulation of about 20 but with a profound
admiration for the said Govt. Mr. Lesina has an
unwholesomely suspicious mind. The gilded
kindness of_Q. Govt, to hard-up pressmen of its
way of thinking has long been a subject of
comment in newspaper circles all over Aus-
probably only due to charity.

advertising with a certain obscure little Brisbane
rag. The subsidy stopped a few weeks ago, and
the organ aforesaid promptly died—its feet in the
gutter and its head on the Treasury doorstep.

Premier Seddon, being interviewed in
London on his return from Ireland and asked his
opinion on the eternal Irish difficulty, “replied
cautiously that his stay in Ireland had been too
brief to enable him to form an opinion.” Thereby
he avoided making any remarks that might
offend the Tory and Coercionist British Govern-
ment. Yet the same Seddon was ready to form
all sorts of tremendously violent opinions about
the Transvaal difficulty without setting foot in
the country at all. There seems to be a large
discrepancy here somehow.
No sooner is Vic. Factories Act a thing of
the past than organised sweating—the sweating of
the sub-contractor—starts as of yore. A partner-
ship case at Collingwood (Melb.), last week, dis-
closed that certain boots, made by the small firm
implicated for a bigger firm, were actually sold to
the latter at Is. Bd. and 2s. 2d. per pair, and that
the man who made the boots could only earn £1
on 60 pairs. As the Chamber of Manufacturers
has distinctly declared it is averse to sweating,
will it kindly say what it calls this ? And as it
objects to wages boards will it propose some other
remedy ?
r , Under S.A. Premier Jenkins’s Northern
Territory land-grant railway job the syndicate
will not only get a grant up to 90,000,000 acres,
but the grant is to carry with it all gold, silver,
and minerals, and (professedly) 10 years’ exemp-
tion from taxation. The absurdity of the situa-
tion is that the Commonwealth has power to levy
land-tax, income-tax, absentee-tax, railway-pas-
senger duty, and any other imposts it pleases all
over the Commonwealth, and unless Jenkins can
persuade the Federal Parliament to guarantee the
10 years’ exemption the agreement doesn’t amount
to a row of pins. Jenkins, doubtless, has the
cheek to profess to bind the Federal Parliament,
but it isn't bound by Jenkins’s cheek. Whether
it takes over the Territory or not, its power to tax
land there, or in any other part of Australia or
Tasmania, is beyond all question ; and if Jenkins’s
syndicate doesn’t know this, that is a matter for
Jenkins and the syndicate to settle hereafter.
THE PRESS TRUST.
SYME : “ Now, Willoughby, there’s just one other little

The necessity of having a successful busi-
ness man as the Ministerial head of Govt, depart-
ments is insisted upon by the Tory class of
politician, among whom most of the successful
business men are to be found. Robert Reid is the
most successful business man in the Irvine Minis-
try, but the scheme works out badly in his case.
He is rarely at Cabinet meetings and is
hardly ever to be found in his department—that of
Education. When he held office on a previous
occasion he spent most of his time abroad. The
Iceberg crowd has not been on the Treasury
benches long, but during that time Reid has con-
trived to be out of the State twice.
Mr. Ogier, a Victorian Black-Wednesday’d
P.M., has been highly elated by the ver-
dict of a minority of a Vic. Bar committee
on his theory that the death of the almost-for-
gotten Queen Victoriaihas shattered ad the Aus-
tralian Parliaments and their recent statutes into
unconstitutionality, He has now regaled the
local Geographical Society with a lecture wherein
he annexes Riverina and the Bombala and Dal-
gety Federal capital sites to Victoria by re-assert-
mg the ancient theory that the real boundary of
Victoria is not the Indi source of the Murray,
but the Eumeralla source. As the capital site
has to be in N.S.W., Mr. Ogier can be trem-
blingly advised not to put in an unarmed appear-
ance in the affected districts.
The spectre that is now troubling
Westralia: It is alleged, on respectable engi-
neering authority, that the great reservoir, which
is to furnish the goldfields’ water-supply, after
collecting water for two years, has only enough in
it to supply the goldfields with 2,500,000 gallons
daily for six months. And all the calculations
were based on the assumption that it would supply
5,000,000 gallons a day all the year round. Other
estimates make things look much worse than this.
Anyhow, for good or ill, the works will be finished
in a few months, and then the inflow of the great
waterworks loans must cease, and 1000 men will
be thrown out of employment on to a very weak
labor market. No wonder Westralia has high
jumps in its sleep.
The Bill of Dr. Creed, M.L.C., for the
alleged reform of N.S.W. Leg. Council turns up
again. Dr. Creed proposes that the number of
members in the Council shall be fixed so that that
body can’t be swamped when it collides with the
Assembly by the appointment of new men ; that
each M.L.C. shall have a seven years’ lease of his
billet ; and that when a batch of members retires,
'the Council and the Assembly shall sit together
and elect their successors, so that the old fossil
shall help to choose the new fossil. There are
also provisions for a joint sitting in case of dead-
lock, but the radical defect of the whole scheme is
that it professes to treat the Council as a live body,
when it is really defunct, and is only sitting up
to save burial-expenses.
The sham and hypocrisy of politics have
been well illustrated in Victoria by the unopposed
election of Duncan Gillies to the Speakership.
The unanimity of the election was something
tearful to see. Having been violently pointed at
by the Clerk of the House, Iceberg Irvine drifted
to the table and proposed Gillies. Peacock
seconded the nomination with the emotion of a
sick man. Gillies was conducted to the chair
amid wild cheers. Gillies has been lauded as a
high constitutional and Parliamentary authority.
He may have been all his friends claim for him,
but if that is so, then it is wonderful how much a
man can forget. It is a long time since Gillies
made a speech that could be understood. All his
recent utterances have been absolutely incoherent
and silly.
Referring patronisingly to recent Vic-
torian criticisms (probably to ex-Attorney General
Bryan O’Loghlen’s in Melb. Herald) on the un-
fair operation of the Commonwealth Claims Bill
(now Act) on persons wishing to sue the Common-
wealth, Prime Minister Barton pities the “ignor-
ance ” of those who do not know that the Crown
can never be sued as a matter of right. Barton
is evidently of opinion that the N.S.W. practice
of putting up a nominal defendant (the Colonial
Treasurer, for instance) as the Aunt Sally for the
Crown, is universal. He is eertainly “ignor-
ant ” of the fact that in Victoria the subject has a
right to sue the Crown direct by petition in all
matters of contract, without the necessity of a
nominal defendant The litigant against the
Commonwealth has now only the inferior N.S.W.
right.
Mayor Shoppee (quaint name,
Shoppee !), of Ballarat, proposes
that a memorial (Shoppee is a per-
fect whale on memorials) should be
raised (presumably with the rate-
payers’ money) at Kyabram to com-
memorate “the great work done in
the cause of reform.” Wouldn’t it
be better to allay this violent pre-
viousness, and wait till something is
done ? The reforms haven’t arrived
yet. though Bent has; and the man
who puts up a statue to the thing
he is going to do is too sudden for
this world.
The old cry of “ Measures,
not Men,” was raised at the late
Victorian election, and did some
service. But if you don’t get the
men, the measures don’t follow, so
it is a fool of a cry. Every great
political measure has some man or
other of corresponding size carrying
it. A cockroach doesn’t carry a
quart pot, nor does a Bent carry a
Factories Act. The measure with-
out the man is only a somnolent
piece of literature abandoned by the
roadside with no power of move-
ment or progress or usefulness;
and it has just as much chance
to get ahead of itself as has an
empty bran-bag left in the middle
of Sahara.
The just-closed session of the Australian
Parliament lasted 520 days. The Reps, sat 218
times, the Senate 179. Of the Reps., Mauger and
King O’Malley missed no sitting, Tudor, Crouch
and Speaker Holder but one, and Treasurer
Turner but three. In the Senate, Dobson and
Smith stand highest with 166 attendances each.
Those who adorned the Chambers least often were,
in the Reps., Opposition-leader Reid, M‘Lean and
Bruce Smith —all of N.S.W. The most consistent
absentees in the Senate, not reckoning cases where
absence was caused by illness, were Harney and
Millen. Longest sitting was on 3rd December,
1901, for 33 hours, when a beaten, malevolent
Opposition stonewalled the tariff. For the record
of work done, the session will compare well with
one of any Parliament, in any country, of late
times. Out of 32 measures introduced, only seven
were postponed or shelved.
The Bulletin grows dead tired of seeing
this alleged “ reform ” motion on the business
paper of N.S.W. Assembly :
Mr. J. C. L. Fitzpatrick to move: “That, in the
opinion of this House, the system at present in vogue of
treating as ordinary revenue moneys received from the.
sale, by auction or otherwise, of the public estate, is one
that should be immediately discontinued ; and this Hdus©
urges that all future receipts from this source should be
set apart as the nucleus of a fund fi'om which to meet the
State’s obligations in regard to interest on the National
Debt, and for the purpose of meeting, as far as possible,
the cost of extending our railway system for the benefit
of the producers.”
The question is : Supposing the motion is carried,
and about £2,000,000 a year of land revenue is set
aside to meet about £2,700,000 of interest, how
much will be left for railway extension, and how
long will it be before the fund gets to be anything
more than a nucleus ?
THE PIG.
CUSTOMER (angrily): “ Waiter, this stuff is not
fit for a pig to eat.”
WAITER: “Well, don’t eat it, sir.”
Sydney D.T., which has a bitter anti-
Labor fit just now, describes a bill introduced tu
N.S.W. Assembly with the object of getting rid
of the “Taff Vale decision” as regards trades-
unions, as “ a bill giving trades-unions the right to.
break laws with impugnity”—a grossly unfair
and ignorant statement. The fact is that by the
growth of custom, to fulfil a very distinct and
useful purpose, trades-unions in English-speaking
countries have been allowed certain powers and
privileges. These, after standing for many years,
have now, apparently, been swept away by the
Taff Vale decision of the English House of Lords.
The question at issue is not to give trades-unions
any new rights, but to restore the old ones. In
N.S.W., by the way, the Industrial Arbitration
Act provides a haven of refuge for threatened
trades-unionism, and to an extent does away with
the necessity of new legislation on the subject.

Minister Bill Lyne suggests that the pro-
posed trip round Australia should commence about
February, “ in order to escape the fever season in
British New Guinea.” But it is always the fever
season in British New Guinea, and a shuddery
kind of fever it is. Also there are fevers to be
met with by the unacclimatised at Port Darwin
and in the Gulf of Carpentaria. Lyne should
remember that if the expedition catches strange
diseases it won’t improve its opinion of tne
Government, and may affect its vote very seriously.
Vice-Admiral Beaumont last week paid
his first official visit to Adelaide, and he takes
several of the vessels of his fleet with him.
Although S.A. pays (somewhat grudgingly, it
must be admitted) towards the maintenance of
the Auxiliary Squadron, it is seldom that even one
of its blue-jackets is seen in that State, and no
money is ever spent there for stores or repairs.
The Irvine Government has effectually
applied the gag to a number of its supporters.
None of them was allowed to utter a word on the
debate on the address-in-reply. Andrews from
Geelong, who took a manly independent stand at
the election, refusing to be patronised by
‘ Reform ” leagues, Govt, or press, made a slash-
ing attack upon the inconsistencies of the Premier
and his “strong” Ministers. Andrews proved
himself a master of raillery and invective. He
mocked at the Premier’s newspaper reputation
for strength and recalled to mind the week-kneed
namby-pamby speeches made by that coldly
superior person when Opposition leader. Irvine
takes criticism badly, and as Andrews drove home
hjs Points he S at biting his lips until they nearly
bled. Andrews has a keen sense of humor and
illumined his speech with many telling gibes.
Previously he was known as a quiet, modest young
member, but in one speech he has won a reputa-
tion as a slashing fighter.
R.I.P. Henry James M'Cooey, naturalist,
writer, Bohemian, who died in Wangaratta Hos-
pital, Oct. 8. His greatest wish was “ that he
should be missed.” Had H. J.M. grubbed among
humans for his analyses of life, he would have
secured a large reading public, and his pecuniary
reward would possibly have been greater; but his
information would have been less important, as
only a few have the advantage of observing what
the white wood-grub has for supper, or how many
rings the double-tailed iguana has on his neck.
Poor M'Cooey will have his wish—he will be
missed weekly by hundreds of men who, lying
now helpless in hospitals and asylums, knew the
natural life of the bush as he recounted it.
Solicitor-General Davies, M.L.C. (Vic-
toria), on the proposed reform of the constitution,
and the attitude of the House of 48 Rich
Landlords :—
Although there would be no difficulty in passing
economic measures, there might be some in passing some
portions of the Parliamentary reform scheme, but when
the House recognised that it was the will of the country
it would take heed of the expression of that will.
Seeing that the proposed economic measures don’t
touch the Landlords personally, there will cer-
tainly be no difficulty about them. And as regards
the breaking down of its own legislative mono-
poly, the Council will consent to that just as soon
as it recognises that it is the will of the country,
and it always recognises that just as soon as it is
scared out of its wits by public clamor and fancies
that its life is in danger, but no sooner. Then, as
Davies, M.L.C., puts it, the 48 Landlords will
“take heed.” Heaven knows that is almost the
only thing they haven’t taken, so far.

From a well-known Anglo-Australian now
in London: —
Things are mighty bad here ; there is a slump in nearly
everything. The “ loyalty " slump is not far off. The
loyalty boom has been on for a long time and must go off
sooner or later. S’Africa caused it, and as sure as night
follows day, will also bring about the slump. The
£220,000,000 has been spent not in bagging Boers, but in
destroying a country—probably the total cost in money
and destroyed property verges on £400,000,000. The
country is desolated for hundreds of miles. Firesticks
have been put to thousands of farms, tens of thousands
of cattle have been killed for food, and a greater
number simply to get rid of them. The butcher,
Kitchener, having retired, things are left in the
hands of Milner, the baker, who will soon be
expected to supply the people with bread. I would
not be surprised to see some bread riots there before the
end of this year. The Kaffir is the best black miner in
the world, yet, with the cheapest labor in the world,
these big Rand mines only pay divs. by enormous pro-
ductions. The profits per ton are so slight that
they will not stand white labor unless it can be got
for ss. a day, and even at that they would be heavily
reduced. The training of Kaffirs to become miners,
which takes from two to three years, has been completely
stopped for that time. The nigger has made so much
money during the war that he does not care to work in
mines, where quite a number of him used to be killed every
week without the slightest fuss resulting. One of the
managers told me that he used to work 32 at one time in
a shaft 28 x 8, and would sink 200 ft. a month by hand
lain r ! You can imagine how many blasting accidents
would take place during a year. But it didn’t matter.
The Chartered Co., too, will cause trouble. Sooner or
later that concern must become bankrupt, unless the
British Govt, comes to its rescue—which it probably will
do. Surely, the people out there won’t like that, as it
will cost the country many millions. In some form or
other S’Africa will be called upon to pay for the war and
to redeem the loss of wasted millions by the
Chartered Co. As sure as the last war was for
the destruction of two small republics, the next fight
will be for the creation of a new and big one
I am enclosing some cuttings re the London and Globe.
If any further evidence was wanted of the indifference of
the British public this case supplies it. Ever since I have
been here this time every effort has been made by some
people to bring about a prosecution. Petitions to the
public prosecutor and Attorney-General, questions in the
House of Commons, letters and articles in newspapers,
and so far without result. Reason not far to seek—the
memory of the dead—Dufferin, Loch, and Robinson ;and
the so-called reputations of men not yet dead but who
ought to be han.ed. Many millions were wasted. It
went into the pockets of the Globe gang, and much of it
was employed in bribing newspapers to remain silent.
It was a gigantic steal, and big men as well as news-
papers assisted in the robbery. So many things have
leaked out that some men who were the victims are
determined to see the thing through, but if nothing is
done within the next few months the matter will pass
into oblivion. Meanwhile, Whittaker Wright, whose
wages-sheet for men working on his estate used to be
over £2 :00 a month, is considerably reduced. lam told
he now employs less than IPO men on his domestic affairs.
I am sure that Australia, which has to bear fully half the
blame of these Westralian robberies, and did not receive
one-twentieth of the plunder, would insist upon having
this matter cleared up by a jury or commission, and if
the Government refused, as it is doing here, it would be
fired out.

The Secret of Flight,
Hundreds of clever men have been trying for
the last century to discover the secret of flight.
Many flying machines have been invented, but all
have failed to answer the purpose. Now, if there
is anything in what we are taught, these ambitious
inventors are trying to bring about what we will
all get to know in a few years—that is, if we are
good. Consequently we must acknowledge that
these flying machine men are in a hurry to know
just what.a few hundred million of people don’t
want to know very particularly for a few years at
least. None of us want to fly sooner than need
be, and it is to keep back the flying state as long
as possible that such letters as these become valu-
able: —“Itis with much pleasure that I am able
to send you a few lines in praise of Dr. Morse’s
Indian Root Pills,” writes Mr. A. Brown, New-
market, Vic. “For the last two months I have
been a sufferer from neuralgia. I think I tried
almost everything in the hope of finding relief,,
but failed. About two weeks ago one of your
books was left at my house, and on reading that
Indian Root Pills had cured many others of
neuralgia I decided to give them a trial. After
taking about half a bottle I was delighted to find
that all the pains had vanished. I can thorouglily
recommend them to all who suffer as I did. You
are at liberty to make this case public if you think
fit.” Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills are a perfect
blood purifier, and a positive cure for biliousness,
indigestion, constipation, headaches, sallow com-
plexion, liver and kidney troubles, piles, pimples,
boils and blotches, and for female ailments. Sole
proprietors, The W. H. Comstock Co., Limited.
(Australasian Depot), 58 Pitt-street, Sydney.
Packed in amber bottles and the full name blown
thereon.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
If the totahsator is ever legalised in N.S.W. or
Vic. a limit should be fixed to the percentage deducted
by racing clubs. There’s no such limit in the Q. Act, and
when Govt, first imposed a totalisator-tax of 21 per cent
the clubs calmly added it on to their 10 per cent, and
made the investor pay! This year the Philp-Leahv
Ministry, in its efforts to stop the hole in the State
pocket, increased the tax to 5 per cent., and race clubs
now take 13J per cent, from the total amount invested in
the machine. With the fractions added this means that
the deduction from one day’s investments frequently
averages as high as 17 per cent, (no fractions of a shilling
are paid in Kanaka-land, except where ss. tickets are
issued), but the clubs grab this extra imposition. Result,
appreciable decrease in tote investments and corre-
sponding increase in bookmakers’ profits. The small
investor growled at the 12£ per cent., but the additional
lilPer cent, has made him rebel outright, besides making
it harder for him to figure-out what his dividend will be
* * * *
Surely the wary “Tatt.” must have some
cunning emissaries masquerading in the ranks of the
cranks who would mind everybody’s business but their
own. Hardly a week passes but a letter appears in the
columns of the “ religious ” weeklies, drawing attention
to the easiest and promptest method of sending to Hobart
for tickets. Even the usually astute Pastor Fitchett
printed, as a footnote to his correspondents’ wails (?), a
long yarn embodying the essentials of “Tatt’s” circulars
just the thing the consultation-promoter wants.
* * * *
Winner of Caulfield Cup, Lieutenant Bill, was
named after his owner’s brother, then an Australian
officer in S Africa. Though heavily backed months ago,
when weights saw light, his subsequent public and track
doings were not encouraging in the slightest degree, and
he dropped completely out of reckoning with punters
generally; Lieutenant Bill was bred at Warbreccan
(Deniliquin) by Mr. O. C. McCulloch, who still owns him.
The nag is a 4yr.-old gelding by Pilgrim’s Progress from
Kingarooma, by Tubal Cain, from Lurline, from Traducer.
At 2yrs. he figured in the Riverina district, but did no
S°°d- Af ter frequent unfruitful starts in and around
Melbourne last season, Lieutenant Bill won Aspendale
Park Trial Handicap in April, and next Eclipse Stakes at
Oorowa and then Flemington Highweight Handicap (in
July) fell to him—three successive wins. Lieutenant Bill
is in Melbourne Cup with 8.2, including 101 b. penalty.
Caulfield Cup morsels: Said that when weights
first appeared, owner of Lieutenant Bill secured £5OOO to
£lO his nag and Strata Florida, which is now capital
hedging money. At card-reading, night before race,
hundreds to 2 and 21 went begging. Extent of owner’s
betting on the course was acceptance of £5OO to d&l2 10s.
People who won money were those who took it early and
were compelled to hold it. Lieut. Bill came on very fast,
as he only finished seventh in Toorak and Ooongy Handi-
caps, ran at same meeting. Mo, generally, has made a
big haul. First, third, and fourth in the race were Pil-
grim’s Progress gets. Time (2.36) for the race equals
Gnaroos (’9l) and Hymettus’ (’9B), and is only Jsee.
slower than the record put up by Hymettus last year.
Stakes won at the meeting totalled £9200. Lieutenant
Bill 8 owner got the biggest portion (£2500), and Mr. E.
Fraser (owner of Debutant Stakes winner) followed with
£lOOO. Then came Messrs. W. T. Jones, £650- C L
M’Donald, £590; F. M'Grath, £6OO ; Rupert Clarke, £370.
• * • •
The “startling reversal of form” necessary to
the victory of any outsider in Caulfield Cup was not
generally expected from Lieutenant Bill. A while ago
when the C.O. betting was young and struggling, the
Lieut figured for a few days as a nominal first favorite.
Then he began to get beaten for minor handicaps under
conditions which seemed to put him out of reasonable
reckoning for the big race. For instance, Oct. 4, he
finished simply “nowhere” behind Combat, Abington,
Progredior, and Vanity Fair in the Stand Handicap (lj
mile) at Flemington. Oct. 11 he finished sixth, behind
Kinglock, Air Motor, and Ibex, in the Toorak Handicap
(1 mile). And on 15th, only three days previous to Caul-
field Cup, the sorely-defeated animal finished in the rear
of Vanity Fair, Free States, Progredior, and The Idler for
the Ooongy Handicap (1 mile 3 fur.). Lieut. Bill’s
“reversal of form” was startling, seeing that Vanity
Fair and Progredior, sticking close together for the third
successive time, were placed third and fourth in Caulfield
Cup.
Flagship improved considerably on his last
year’s running. Then he finished a moderate third to
Hymettus and Wakeful. This year, he carried 221 b. more
weight, and got within a length of the winner, but, as he
will meet the Lieutenant on only 41b. better terms in the
Melbourne Cup, the penalised four-year-old looks to have
nothing the worst of the weights with him. And the
probability of one or two of the Derby colts beating all
the Caulfield Cup form over the Flemington two miles is
now stronger than ever.
Ikey Mo and the tipsters were quite of one
mind about the Caulfield Cup chances. With the one
exception of Flagship—the “ tip” of fully a dozen jockeys
riding in the race—lkey was disposed to lay more
liberally than usual against every horse that had been
badly beaten in recent handicaps. The “ books ” on the
course reduced the odds against the favorites, and en-
larged their offers against the outsiders as the time drew
near. Anyone could get 50 to 1 against Lieut. Bill by
diligently seeking it.
Now that a well-advertised “dream” horse has
won Caulfield Cup we shall have Isaac de Moses inventing
further vision stories for the beguilement of superstitious
punters. The objection to bogus “ dreams ” lies in the
fact that their well-informed authors invariably select an
animal which—in their opinion—can’t possibly win,
whereas the bona-fide pork-supper dream horse has, at least,
a fair chance. For the benefit of dream-backers, The
Bulletin may mention that artist Alf. Vincent, who
wears rather long hair and is tinged with ruminative
melancholy, has dreamed that Melb. Cup winner will be
No. 9on the card. Two years ago Mr. Vincent dreamed
Ingliston for the Caulfield Cup, so he is not a visioner to
be utterly despised. In any state of indigestion he is
addicted to seeing things, but his crayfish results are the
most reliable. It was a nocturnal diet of crayfish that
led to the appearance of No. 9 upon the phantom black-
board. Back No. 9at the post, and you will probably
wish you hadn’t. Punters are mostly born to wish they
hadn’t.
* • * *
Previous to the scratching of Seclusion for
Caulfield Cup it was known that Homeward Bound had
been beating the mare easily in their work. Seclusion
won four races in August, beating comparatively unripe
animals, and when they began to “ come on ” she began
to “go off." Winning form in August is always a doubt-
ful recommendation for a Cup favorite.
* t, ■ » ' * *
Wakeful’s victory in the Eclipse Stakes, at Caul-
field, only served to show that nothing, save Footbolt,
was trying seriously to beat the mare. In the first place,
the betting told the tale, for Footbolt, with the worst of
the weights, was backed at 6 to 1 to beat Wakeful, where-
as the others went out practically friendless. The race
was run in strict accordance with the betting. Footbolt
seemed to be ridden out .to finish third, a head behind
Air Motor, time 2,26. The next event, the Ooongy Handi-
cap, was run over the same course in 21sec. faster time.
* * » *
The backing of Loyal for Caulfield Guineas, on
the strength of mysterious “information,” was an in-
teresting study .in punters’ credulity. The colt had no
apparent pretensions to beat Strata Florida and Great
Scot, yet he carried more money, in small investments,
than either of the pair that held him, safe on paper. In
the race he performed like a non-stayer, and was subse-
quently scratched for Caulfield Cup and the Derby. The
whisper that brought so much Loyal money into the bag
of Ikey Mo was probably put into circulation by the same
people who boomed the mediocre Bon Voyage for last
Epsom. There are still some people disposed to believe
that Bon Voyage must be a first-rater because he beat
Seclusion and Strata Florida in August and was backed
at a short price for the Epsom, but the stable was never
sweet on the five-year-old gelding.
At moment of writing Strata Florida has sup-
Slanted Abundance as favorite for V.E.O. Derby, and his
telb. Cup prospects also seem rosy. When this colt
fetched 1300 gs. as a yearling, his breeder, Mr. John Mayo,
was astonished, as he’d have gladly accepted a fourth of
that sum. Pedigree of Strata Florida’s great grand-dam,
Maid of the Lake, on the maternal side, has been lost.
She was bred by Capt. Russell, of Ravensworth, N.S.W.
• • * •
Kiota, brother to Daimio, gave a suggestion of
his quality by taking the Steeplechase at Caulfield last
Saturday, and if the handsome fellow goes on all right
for the next fortnight, he will be worth backing in both
the steeplechases at Melb. Cup meeting. Scobie has had
a lot of trouble with Kiota, and the horse is not yet
“ wound up.” Weight doesn’t stop 'chasers of his class
when they feel like winning.
* * • *
Mona Nursery, at V.R.C. meet last week, added
one more to the thick crop of turf surprises. In a field of
18 Lady Lettice wasn’t fancied at all, while runner up
(Velox) in previously decided rich Debutant Stakes, went
out a hot first choice. Several others were weightily in-
vested upon, but Lady Lettice beat them all comfortably.
Winner is by Beauchamp from the Drummer mare, Lady
Love, and owned by Mr. W. A. Gardiner, Gobolion,
N.S.W. She was bred at Windsor, and brought 165 gs. at
Sydney yearling sales.
• * • *
The Victory — presuming that his kidneys, or
whatever was the matter with him, give no trouble
before the day—should make a good bid for Melb. Cup.
The 4-year-old is so muscular that the recent stoppage in
his work will do him no harm. Of all the “beaten”
horses in the Eclipse Stakes, this nuggetty invalid showed
to most advantage when running alongside of Wakeful.
The Victory should win something at the V.R.C. meeting.
Present scribe would not be surprised to see him beat
Wakeful for the Melbourne Stakes on Derby Day.
• • * •
Lieutenant Bill, winner of Caulfield Cup, paid
£23 Us. on a Sydney tote—over 94 to 1.
• * » •
The luck of it. Trainer of Lieutenant Bill
wanted him struck out of Caulfield Cup and run in
Moora Welter, but owner wouldn’t have it.
* * • *
Profits of Caulfield Cup meet ended Saturday
were materially less than in past years—£7oo this time as
against £2055 in '9l and £2700 in ’9O. Shortage is
accounted for to some extent by the fact that stakes this
year were £7OO bigger ; and more liberal terms conceded
owners in Debutant Stakes, and other events, meant
that the V.A.T.C. handled £3OO less revenue from those
sources than previously. Cup-day takings showed a
falling off of £2OO.
• • • •
Brisbane can boast a rival to Barney Allen in
the wholesale horse-owning line. The northern man
works on original methods, and apparently makes them
pay. He will buy anything from a yearling to an aged
horse in training on the recommendation, possibly, of
some reputed judge. Then his purchases are leased to
small owners or trainers who have to pay all expenses
and “part” a share of the prize-money won. Result :
If the nag wins races the lessor pockets his share of the
stakes, and can get a profit on his original outlay when
he wants to sell; failures (very few, so far) are turned out
or got rid of at any price. At one time this turf magnate
owned nearly 100 horses, all being trained in Brisbane,
and it often happened that he owned all the starters in
one event. This, of course, had to stop ; and now the
wholesale owner has a private trainer, a big stable, and a
long list of wins.
* * * •
A’ tote continues to flourish in Melbourne,
under the nose of P.M.G. Drake, although that moral
autocrat has put Tatt’s sweep beyond the pale of postal
service. Since the so-called suppression of Tattersall
there has been a steady increase in this illicit business,
and the police, no doubt, are getting bigger bribes than
ever out of it. The owner, nowadays, “ runs ” one of the
bookmakers on salary and commission. Last year he con-
trolled the result of the Austral Wheel Race. The pro-
prietor of this tote is a power *in the sporting
world, and from a man of mystery he has developed into
a well-known personage. One may see him at a Mel-
bourne restaurant, perchance, talking business over a
bottle of wine with his agent-bookmaker and a well-
known detective or two —a nice little family gathering !
Chief Secretary Jack Murray, who formerly battled
without avail for the legalisation of the tote on Victorian
racecourses, now enjoys the satisfaction of presiding over
the Police Department, which winks its other eye.

The flying Maoriland youngster Achilles
(N.S.W. bred), whose hoof had been causing trouble, was
sent for classic Wanganui Guineas, after a spell of indif-
ferent training, and won in a canter in the hot time of
1.42 2-sths. His hoof had been clamped for the occasion.

Mr. Gr. M'Leod, of Burrygaa station, was badly
savaged the other day by the steeplechasifig stallion
Mikado 11. Mr. M‘Leod was passing the horse’s box
when Mikado, theretofore regarded as a good-humored
animal, grabbed him by the arm, lifted him off the
ground, carried him round the loose-box, and then threw
him out of the stable again. Amputation of the arm may
yet be necessary.

“ Gun ”: Inexcusably late withdrawal of Seclu-
sion proved a staggerer for punters. Mo. beamed ; it gave
him a firm foundation to work on as the mare had been
top of the market or thereabout with coin coming for her
in piles, for weeks. Stable never had a cent on. Eleventh
hour elimination of “Oxy’s" Acrasia was another
sharp poke in eye for punters, the Sydney section par-
ticularly ; she did big things before leaving Randwick, and
had for additional endorsement that meritorious show in
Air Motor’s Epsom. Appears the stable proposed coming
with its dose at card-reading night before the race, but the
“ commissioner ” found that though the nag was quoted
at hundreds to four and five the betting was blocked,
Ikey absolutely refused to lay beyond a very narrow limit.
A wire to Sydney late at night conveyed this information
and Acrasia went out at 10 next morning. Said that her
trainer, in blissful ignorance of what had been done, sent
a wire to headquarters on the day of the race tipping
Acrasia or Kilt as sure.
* • • *
Second round of Sydney first-grade cricket
opened Saturday, with conditions favoring batsmen
Waverley, opposed to Glebe, topped the day’s run-
accumulations with nine for 340, towards which Mackenzie
and Ebsworth subscribed centuries. The fact recalls that
in same club’s previous match, same two men stretched
out ahead of all others—former just missing his 100 by
two, latter making 80. Ebsworth smote with a mighty
smite, and gathered eight s*s and twelve 4’s, and per-
formed that rare feat, 5 for a snick in the slips. Mac-
kenzie’s total was hit up in 20min. over the hour, and
included two 6’s and 14 4’s. Last season’s premiers
(Sydney) engaged Redfern, and compiled only 172 on a
good ground—s 2 of these were opposite Cuffe’s name.
This was the smallest total of the day. At stumps-draw
Redfern had two down for 43, and, with 130 to win, the
fight looks like proving tough. North Sydney made 251
against Central Cumberland, to which veteran F. Wade
brought 68 not out. O.C. have 11, with no wickets down.
Leichhardt-Balmain held the pitch from ’Varsity all the
afternoon, having nine out for 324 at the end. Ashdown
(104) and Diamond (85) knocked the venom out of the
bowling. The Burwood-Paddington match saw former
make 223, and latter 8 without loss. Garnsey hit up 78
and old-hand G. P. Barbour 40, of Burwood’s lot. ’

“ Down Sydney harbor a few weeks back, at
a Sunday-school picnic, some rascal had given a ss. prize
for a boys novelty race, the conditions of which were
that each boy was to stop in the middle of the race, eat
three buns provided by the committee, and then con-
tinue his run, fastest eater and runner to win. A parson
was in charge of the event. Eight boys started, and the
way those youngsters “ scoffed ’’ 24 tough buns made me
ill, though the parson laughed heartily. I asked an
intelligent policeman if he was not empowered to summon
that preacher for cruelty, but he was sorry to say he
could not. I have an idea that the prize was given by a
“ specks-before-the-eyes ” specialist. Twenty-four buns
eaten in hot haste leads to custom later on.

The championship (eights) at N.S.W. Rowing
Association regatta was won easily by the Mercantile
crew, Glebe a bad second. Mr. Upward, the Melb.
expert, who came over especially to judge the rowing
exhibition at the ’Varsity regatta a fortnight ago,
adjudged these two crews as the best on the river, but
he placed Glebe first.

Grafter’s racing career is ended. He has been sold to a

" The big race-meetings next summer promised
so profusely by N.S.W. League of Wheelmen threaten to

the champion sprinter, “ Major ” Taylor, on whom the
league depended so greatly in the matter of its £lOOO
handicap, will not visit Australia while those willing to
come over are mere names to the local public.

At a committee meeting of Gore (M.L.) Racing
Club, a letter was received from a “ tonsorial artist,”
resigning his membership of the club on account of his
name appearing in the list of members in the annual
report (this he considered detrimental to his business,
mostly done with severely pious Scotehbyterians), and be-
cause'he didn’t receive adequate support in his business
from members of the club. Resolved unanimously : “ That
the resignation be accepted with thanks, and that he be
appointed honorary barber to the club.” Four very bald-
headed members of the committee were deputed to convey
the resolution, and at the same time get their hair cut.
* * • •
“ Jab ” : M‘Call-Doherty heavy pug-supremacy
struggle, at Sydney National S.C., last week, is now
referred to in many places as having been cronk, but ’twas
not so ; only thing crooked about it was that Doherty, im-
pressed by allegedly shrewd judges, thought he could easily
beat the N.S.W. champ., and, consequently, didn’t train
more than two weeks. When the job proved more severe
than anticipated (M‘Oall is awkward, hard, strong and
armor-plated), Doherty had no reserve-power to fall back
upon. Best line in such things is the money. Though
big lumps of M'Oall coin were offered, there was compara-
tively none for Doherty, which is explained by the fact
that last-named is little known in Sydney, and also that
his careless preparation was noticed. Further, though
heaps of offers to take s’s to 4 or 10’s to 8 were made,
M'Oall people wouldn’t spring the slightest shade, but
said, in effect, “ Even money or nothing at all.” Had
the thing been “ fixed,” those “ in the know ” would cer-
tainly have laid odds on sooner than miss making a big
scoop,
• • • •
Lightweight bruisers Harry Punch and
“Monty” Andrews thumped each other harder and
more often, at Gaiety A. 0 (Sydney), last week, than
most ordinary pugs thump in half-a-dozen fights. Ribs
and stomach received most treatment from both. The
densely massed “ gods ” swayed and roared with excite-
ment. Referee Tom Taylor lost his head completely.
Directly the men bore into each other, clinch or no
clinch, he’d obtrude his huge sweltering body between
and force them asunder, thus preventing in-fighting.
The house vehemently hissed, hooted and shrieked its
condemnation, but Taylor still offended and butted them
apart with sheer weight of beef, and consequently almost
shut Punch out from what he was best at. The
scrap was full of sting and force till the 9th round,
and then it fell off, Punch growing weak through
having to scale below himself—9.l2. Ultimately Punch
lost on a foul and thus saved his side bet, which,
articles provided, should be paid over on a knock-
out only, and the purpose was achieved in the 14th
round, when Punch secured disqualification. Andrews
got the verdict, but the £5O side bet went back into each
fighter’s pocket. Insistence upon a knock-out (and that
only) deciding the destination of stake money is a
frequent provision in fight articles nowadays, born of the
fact that boxers generally have little faith in latter-day
referees,' who mostly know nothing, or less, of the game.
* » • •
N.S.W. amateur boxing championships induced
44 poddy pugs’ entrants, but some failed to materialise,
through scare and other things, and excess of weight
barred a few more. Competitions extended from Wednes-
day till Saturday inclusive, drawing big crowds to the
newly-opened National Sporting Club, Sydney, each
night. Opening lot of battles were so vimful and
enthusiastic that six successive forfeits occurred in
the next batch. Displays were a big improvement on
last year’s. Every man who shaped had himself fully
attuned. Weyland, previous holder of the middle supre-
macy, won it again, as well as the heavy-weight cham-
pionship. His toughest opponent in former—inches
shorter and pounds lighter—was 0. Graham, who
brought such an uncommon stock of grit into the
ring, with remarkable capacity for punishment, that
the promoting N.S.W. Sports Club gave him a
special memento. Australian Artilleryman H. M'Kellar,
a stone weightier, looked over Weyland in the final of
the heavies (contested same night as concluding bout of
middles), and roughed and punched hard from “ go,” but
Weyland, cleverer and cool always, scored most, though
in dire trouble often, and he got the well-earned prize.
Former bantam champion, H. Clifton, scored again. D.
Parbury gathered in the feather laurels, and speedy,
well-built swimmer “Snowy” Baker appropriated the
lightweight title, showing exceptional fighting power,
skill, and condition for an amateur. Gory noses, eyes in
mourning, and swells and scrapes generally, peculiar to
the game fistic, were much in evidence, but no one whim-
pered or winced. Summed up, the contests were a big
success, and proved a decided acquisition to the State’s
forefront athletic doings.
* * » *
Recent 9st. novice pug. tourney winners,
(“Jack” King and “Artie” M'Kell, scrapped vigorously at
Sydney Golden Gate A.C., Saturday night. Each had
superb condition, brought good punching power to bear
(King had a tightly closed eye after the fourth round),
and fortune varied frequently. Once M'Kell looked the
better, then King appeared to have the advantage, and,
later, it was “ up and up,” with the crowd marvelling at
the vigor of the struggle and the stamina of the fighters.
After the 10th round M'Kell gradually gained ascendancy,
and King was so completely cracked that when the
15th round opened referee “Don” M'Donald stopped the
fight and gave M'Kell the splosh.
* • « •
Melbourne lightweight, Palmer, at Sydney
Gaiety A.C., Monday night, tackled N.S.W. crack,
“ Rollo,” lately walloped by Jack McGowan, and the two
made things very hot from “ Time 1 ” Rollo jerked and
dodged his head as if it were worked by wires. Two
shaking-up lefts set him guessing in the third round, and
he sat down very much disgusted. Both hit in holds
■frequently, and the referee allowed it. This game suited
Palmer to the ground—it’s the Melbourne method of
fighting, and the best and most exciting. Rollo respon.
ded grittily, and scored occasionally, but the greater
force was on the other side, wherefore Palmer won at the
close of the fourth round. Hollo’s “corner” skied the
wipe. “Ooeker” Tweedie, recuperated by a long spell
up-country, will meet Palmer next, and probably surprise
those who think him dead to the game.
* * * *
Plugger Bilmartin and F. S. Beauchamp, after
touring the globe, came back by Yankee mail-boat
Ventura this week, with their tails' right down, having
accomplished nothing like what was expected of them.
Small and steep ’Murkan tracks broke Martin up—in two
months he sprawled 11 times. Combined successes of the
pair brought in £3OO, ’tis said, but this scribe wouldn’t
like to give that for what they really closed on. Martin
says world’s premier ’Murkan negro “ Major ” Taylor’s
talked-of visit is improbable, but cycling authorities aver
that the additional inducement will certainly bring the
speedy black. Victorian rider, W. Palmer, returned same
time as Martin and his chum ; he scored frequently round
about Salt Lake City in fast company.
• * • •
S.A. League of Wheelmen’s Spring meet
ended Saturday, and produced a surplus verging
on £2OO. Don Walker annexed 10 miles supremacy of
the continent brilliantly, also the final of one-mile Coro-
nation Handicap. Pacing up to eight miles in the big
thing was only moderately good, but matters fairly boiled
near the end. Twenty yards from stop Morgan flashed
out brilliantly to beat Walker, and thousands roared ex-
citedly. Morgan flew on, the tension became painful.
Walker streaked on ahead, but the fast-coming Morgan
drew nearer and nearer till the home-mark saw Walker
win by only six inches. Pilsell was only a length away
third, and Pye, pressing hard, fourth. Watch showed
24.42. F. R. Burden secured first motor-bike race ever
decided in Australia, covering five miles in 9min. 15sec.

N.S.W. bowling team of 16, picked from about
30 odd available, doesn’t wear a promising look. Com-
paratively few of the front-rank players sent in their
names, and choice was limited. Five of the combination
will visit Melbourne for the first time. This year’s
matches are to be played after Cup week—Nov. 11, 12
and 13—so that play may not suffer through other
influences. Victorian side doesn’t read over strong either,
but it needn’t be to have a good chance of winning. _
* * * V ft
Swimmer Holbein, who kept to the water for
22J hours in his latest splendid failure to cross the
Channel, is a man of 41. He seems to have missed the
distinction of rivalling Capt. Webb’s feat through the
pigheadedness of his advisers ; but even the route he had
chosen might not have been impossible to Holbein six or
seven years ago. He gave the best time of his athletic
life to long-distance cycling. Broke his leg while trying
to break another record, and took to swimming to cure a
stiff knee. Holbein’s water career didn’t begin until he
had passed the age of 35, after which the strongest man
begins to decline in vital power. Webb was only 32 or so
—a lusty young bachelor—when he crossed the Channel.
He was a married man of about 37 when he threw away
his life in trying to swim Niagara rapids, and ere he
left London on his fatal errand his brother begged him
to consider that he was no longer quite as good as he
had been.
* * * *
The Bulletin’s London correspondent : Obit.,
“Freddie” Ball, one time crack player with the “same
old Essendon ” Football Club. After a protracted drink-
ing bout here he stabbed himself in the throat and
abdomen with his penknife. Was found in bed bleeding
to death, and on removal to hospital sank from loss of
blood. His mother is a partner in a well-known Vic.
drapery house. A cruel ending for a fine, powerful
athlete.
* * » *
Some of the things Sandow said to Sydney
medicoes and pressmen t’other afternoon :—“I now weigh
10st., and exercis > by contracting the muscles. Ido it
unconsciously, and that is sufficient to keep me in perfect
condition. Four years ago I, for two years, did not have
any exercise at all, and yet I did not decrease in strength
or measurement. My muscles would not get soft even if
I ceased my performances, nor would I get heavier. The
secret of this is that I, by contracting my muscles, exer-
cise them. A cold bath should not be taken immediately
after running or rowing or any other violent exercises.”
No athlete needs telling what pure, unadulterated r it all
this is. Unconscious contracting of the muscles keeping
their owner in thorough condition is the height of
absurdity. Surely Sandow doesn’t think he is in thorough
condition—he panted while he talked, he swelters freely
nightly, and has flesh soft as a child’s. Let any ordinary
man refrain from exercise for one year, and see how his
muscle will fall away and (if he is healthy) the extra
amount of weight he’ll gather. Ask the rower, the
runner or the boxer about that cold bath business. He
finishes up with a big spurt specially for the bath, jumps
under the shower with the body steaming, and thrives on
it. Ex-athletes, who haven’t lost all energy, work up a big
sweat before they have a cold bath and won’t take it
without.

Irvine’s strong Government met with its
first bump on the very first day on which members
assembled. The “strong” Ministerial party dis-
appeared from the House, as soon as members had
been sworn-in, to arrange in caucus for the election
of a Chairman of Committees. Within five
minutes, the caucus was in an uproar. Irvine pre-
sided, and, in his arctic way, tried to keep things
straight. He was powerless, and for an hour his
“strong ” supporters fought each other and called
each other “ strong ” names. Shiels took a hand,
and several “out-and-out” Govt, supporters left
the room in utter disgust. The trouble was that,
having a “strong” majority, many of the party
wished to elect a chairman from their side of the
House on the principle of spoils to the victors. It
was contended by a few fair-minded men that the
principle wasjbad, and that Labor-member Beazley,
who has for two Parliaments done excellent work
in the chair, should be again elected to the posi-
tion. The caucus was a fiasco, and, when it was
over, Iceberg Irvine’s stoutest friends admitted
that he had shown no capacity to lead even a
caucus meeting.

A Loan.
At a meeting of the council of the Vic. Chamber of Com-
merce, Mr. W. Knox made reference to ill-advised letters
from Australians appearing in the London press bewailing
the drought and bad condition of affairs generally in Aus-
tralia. He was of opinion that these letters had done
great injury to the prospects of future State loans which
might be placed on the London market. — Daily paper
(N.S.W.).
Lay down your pick and shovel,
Throw by your spade and pen,
Good-bye to graft and grovel—
Good morning, Gentlemen !
Our game shall be to borrow,
And owe and pay our way,
God bless the good to-morrow,
And never mind to-day.
Is there a man so rude as (_■■
To say that we are poor ?
Hang up the crawling Judas
And make that question sure.
All through the Never-Never
The streams run full and deep.
The waters flowing ever
Are thirsting for the sheep.
It was a white, weird battle:
Between the rain and rain
Ten thousand head of cattle
Were bleaching on the plain.
But all the whole land over
The grass grew green and sweet,
The cattle rolled in clover —
The cattle would not eat.
Who dares to put in print that
The things we say are lies ?
Who dares to merely hint that
This is not Paradise ?
Work ? Work? O leave or lump it,
Each man upon his own— i
But hear the golden trumpet:
“ A Loan ! A Loan! A Loan ! ”
Lay down your pick and shovel,
Throw by your spade and pen;
Good-bye to graft and grovel,
Good-morrow, gentlemen !
CREEVE ROE.
For straight - out misrepresentation, »
recent attempt of Maoriland Works Minister
Hall-Jones to prove that his State isn’t extrava-
gant in its expenditure of loan money for railway
purposes should take the cake. The hyphenated
Jones publishes a table, at the head of which is
Natal, with a total expenditure of £143 per
inhabitant on railways, and down near the tail
Maoriland with about £22. Jones gets at this by
putting down the population of Natal at 59,000
instead of over 900,000, which, as a handy way of
manufacturing a case, beats the Dry Dog hollow.

Great Britain’s hysterical and uninfonned solici-
tude for the poor, dear black S’African native
who was maltreated by the “brutal Boer” has
given place to a regret that Tommy Atkins has
been encouraged to “ treat the natives with a
coarse familiarity which has engendered a lament-
able insolence towards other white people, already
manifesting itself a real danger. He has become
inflated with a sense of his own importance. . . .
A savage with a swollen head is not far removed
from a dangerous lunatic, and the war has to
answer for many thousands of these. ” There is
much humor in the tacit admission that the Boer
who walloped his own nig. when the walloping
seemed necessary was a wise tactician rather than
an inhuman brute. And there is sadness in the
thought that the docile nigger has been converted
into a dangerous lunatic by bad British manage-
ment. However, the evil is pretty sure to pass
away. Truculent black natives don’t usually give
much trouble under the British flag.
* GIRAROL ” returns to the charge :
Re your foot-note to my letter (B. 19/4/’O2) “ what’s
wrong in running the country for the Papuan ? ” Nothing.
The diggers are more humane to the niggers than either
the Government or the missionaries, and they don’t
object to the Government protecting the Papuans or the
missionaries ; but they do object to be taxed when the}'
get no protection or assistance themselves. The natives
are more civilised in the interior of the country, where
they never see the missionaries or anybody but the dig-
gers and their carriers, than around the mission stations
along the coast. It is safe, now to travel through the
following four tribes (over 60 miles of country, between
Elliott’s Creek and McLoughlins)—Googara, Ninevah,
Simora, and Beda ; and the diggers never fired a shot at
one of them, nor did they round-up the good-looking gins
to sing hymns, or interfere with the natives otherwise
than by bartering knives, tomahawks, calico, beads, etc.,
for sweet-potatoes, taro, and bananas. When there were
only five men left on Elliott’s Creek the Googara tribe
looted the camps, stole firearms, and destroyed every-
thingthey didn’t take away; yet the diggers did not retali-
ate, though the resident magistrate (then at Tamata, 60
miles away) refused to send his police to deal with the
matter. At Milen Bay, nearSamarai, where the missionaries
have been rounding-up niggers for years, the natives were
allowed to rob the diggers’ camps as they pleased,
Government nevei interfering until two thieves were
shot, and two white men tried for murder. Judge
Winter has tried several such alleged murder cases with-
out a jury, and has managed, so far, to give justice; but
if a weak-minded man had been here, who would have
been influenced by the apostles of black cant, there might
have been trouble ere now. But it would be better to
have a jury, because a Judge may be wise to-day and a
fool to-morrow. The missionaries mean well, but they
would be better employed in the slums of London or of
some of the big Australian towns. The diggers want
(1) protection where necessary from thieving natives ; (2),
mule-tracks into the interior, so that store goods can be
sold at reasonable prices and sick men brought down
to the coast; (3), a hospital and medical attendance at
Samarai; (4) a money-order office in Samarai, with branches
where necessary on the goldfields ; (5) no prohibition of
landing cattle, horses, sheep, goats and fowls on the
mainland ; (6) less official ignorance, less tinned-dog, and
more gold.

If You Please, Miss, Give Me Heaven.
“ Papa, I’m so sad and lonely,”
Sobbed a tearful little child ;
“ Since dear mamma’s gone to Heaven,
Papa, darling, you’ve not smiled.
I will speak to her and tell her
That we want her to come home;
Just you listen, and I’ll call her
Through the telephone:
Chorus—
“lf you please, Miss, give me Heaven—for mv Mamma’s
there;
You can find her with the angels on the golden stair.
She’ll he glad it’s me who’s speaking—call her, won’t you.
please,
For I want to surely tell her we’re so lonely here.”
When the girl received this message,
Coming o’er the telephone,
How her heart thrilled in that moment,
And tjie wires seemed to moan ;
“ I will answer, just to please her:
‘ Yes, dear heart, I’ll soon come home.”'
“ Kiss me, Mamma,-kiss your darling,
Through the telephone.”
Chorus— “ If you please, Miss,” etc.

“ Tackra ” : N.S.W. South Coast train,
2nd-class carriage ; atmosphere of stale beer,
onions, and vile tobacco. One old lady, reeking
with tanglefoot, has her arms full of stale vege-
tables, while her head reclines on a boozy old
man’s shoulder. “ She ain’t me lawful wife, yer
know,” he observes. “She’s Bill’s, but I’m ’is
chum, so it’s all right. He's under the seat.” One
gentleman is leaning out of the window hideously
ill, while two women hold him by the coat-tails.
“He’s the boss’s son,” they explain, “and we’re
takin’ ’ini ’ome safe—he’s ’ad a drop.” Close by
are two men, wrapped in drunken sleep and each
other’s arms, snoring horribly. Presently several
women, all beery, come in and laugh uproariously
at every word the half-drunken men utter. Soon
a bottle goes round, and the jokes and stories
become very risky. At last, when it comes to
candid obscenity, I expostulate, and am answered
with : “Yah, yer bloomin’ toff ! If we ain’t good
enuff for yer, yer can git.” And I “ gat ” accord-
ingly.
An indignant Auckland (M.L.) ex-Mayor
(Mr. Phillips) wants to know what The Bulletin
means by aspersing the fair name of that city and
of its trams :—
It is true in the past the tramways were slow, but sure,
and we could boast of few if any accidents (more than
Sydney can say). We are now about to start electric
trams. Auckland has good concentrated Municipal
Government (unlike Sydney). The Municipality of Auck-
land controls the water supply (at a good profit),
drainage, free library (superior to Sydney’s), art galleries
(two), fire brigade, four parks, 2000 acres country land
endowments, 70 acres city endowments, realising now
nearly £20,000 per annum (will in a few years reach
£100,000 per annum), abattoirs (new), large market and
cemeteries, receives all license revenues, including hotels,
theatres and auction rooms. The Tramway Co. pays a
large rental for the use of roads. The Auckland Gas Co.
pays about £1800 per annum rates (in Sydney I believe
the Gas Co. pays nothing !) Auckland leading streets are
now asphalted with Swiss asphalt, so that dust is con-
spicuous by its absence. The city has also - a forest
reserve on the west coast of the province, of 5500 acres,
where the noble kauri tree flourishes.
[Yes, on the whole, Auckland, M.L., can give
some points to effete Australian municipalities.—
Ed. B.]


THE ROYAL BANK OF AUSTRALIA, LIMITED.
HEAD OFFICE: fIELBOURNE, VICTORIA.
T WBUTT-UIl<rTH: IR-lEIPOie.T.
Presented to the Shareholders at the Ordinary General Meeting on 29th October, 1902.
The Directors have pleasure in placing before Shareholders the annexed Balance-Sheet and Statement
of Accounts for the half-year ending 30th September, duly audited and certified.
The net profits (including £2,019 17s. lid. brought from last half-year) amount to .. .. £9,715 7 3
out of which it is proposed to pay a dividend at the rate of 6 per cent, per annum .. . . .. . . 3’750 0 0
leaving a balance to be carried forward of .. .. .. .. .. .. .. £5 965 7 3
The business continues to progress satisfactorily, a large number of new accounts having been opened since
last balance.
The steady expansion of the business has latterly made it difficult to carry on operations in the present
premises, and the directors have for some time past been considering the question of securing a building containing
sufficient accommodation for present requirements, with room for further expansion. They are pleased to be able
to announce that they have bought, for £50,000, the building lately belonging to the Oit.v of Melbourne Bank to
which the business will be removed in the early part of next year, when possession of the banking chamber will’ be
obtained and certain necessary alterations made.
The Directors think they can fairly congratulate the shareholders on being the owners, at a low figure, of one
of the best sites for banking purposes in the city of Melbourne, and they feel assured that a very considerable
accession of business must result therefrom.
The Directors would again appeal to the shareholders to assist them, as in the past, by bringing such business
as they may be able to introduce, and they take this opportunity of mentioning that the arrangements recently made
with foreign agents enable the bank to transact business in all parts of the world on favorable terms. J
By order of the Board,
H. T. WILSON, Manager.

At Poverty Point.
The theatrical libel action seems to have just now a
wide popularity. The playwright responsible for a farce
called “The Giddy Goat ” sued the Era for d amages because
that paper remarked on the miserable character of the
piece. In evidence the play was described by an expert
witness (William Archer) as “thoroughly disgusting.”
Yet the jury gave a verdict for plaintiff—certainly only
with a farthing damages, but still a verdict; - English
libel law is said to be more “ liberal ” . than Australian,
but certainly no Australian jury would give a verdict, even
for a farthing, against a newspaper for fair comment on a,
rubbishy play. . ;
How wickedly has stageland been maligned! From
Clara Morris’s recently-published reminiscences of a long,
life on the stage, it appears. tha.t the mdst ferocious virtue
reigned all round. Though the lady must have been
more or less acquainted personally with 2000 members of
the profession, only one (a ballet-girl) is mentioned as
having lapsed. And Clara’s associates, when they spoke of
that one, used to close their dressing-room doors, and lower
their voices in pity. It was computed in ancient Borne
that the number of Vestal Virgins who fell from the paths
of rectitude was one per 200. Thus the standard of
purity among American actresses is vastly better than
that of the Vestals, though the punishment of the latter,
on proof of guilt, consisted in being bricked up in a vault
until the Last Day.
Dear Bulletin.— “ E.” says : “The average muck pro-
duced in Australia as a famous London success is proba-
bly unknown to any part of the metropolis”—which is
just what 1 said. But that it “ never gets a show in the
prosperous suburbs ”is quite untrue. And the fact that
the “ filthy portions of Islington are situated within two
miles of London G.P.0.” does not affect any of my state-
ments. As a matter of fact, there isn’t any part of
London which isn’t filthy—barring a few main streets,
which serve to cover up the dirt at the back. There are
plays other than lurid melodrama which are nonsensical,
humor, which is pathetic (as nearly all British humor is),
and pathos which is humorous. Nothing that Beerbohm
Tree could do could be anything but nonsenical (judging
by his weak-kneed Ulysses with the barber’s arms), and
nothing that Wilson Barrett could act could be anything
but contemptible as Art. And I wrote that paragraph
complained of by “ E.” not to sneer at England, but to
prompt Australia, which is better-educated than Eng-
land, to refuse a hearing to undramatic or unliterary or
unmusical tripe whose only passport is that it was made
in England. I don’t hate England : I’m sorry for it; but
I hate to see my countrymen perpetuating English ugli-
ness and English errors.—lt.B.
Minnie Palmer, the dolly little woman who came out to
Australia long ago, as “ My Sweetheart,” is threatening
to produce herself in London as a new variety of Nell
Gwynne. Minnie will, presumably, mash King Charles in
a frilled pinafore and a sun-bonnet.
The thinnest “Australian play” on record has been
written round Dan Leno, at present the particular pet of
the London “ halls.” In it he comes out as manager of
Madame Marie’s Supply Stores at Ooddletown, A tribe
of aborigines is reported to have “ risen,” and to be
making for Ooddletown, and Leno is ordered to fall in as
Sergeant of the Town Guard ! The rest seems to be pure
foolery of the “ Old Guard ” type.
“ F.C.”: Marshall-Hall had a whole two columns in
Melbourne Herald, lately, explaining the mysteries of
Beethoven’s Sonata in F. There is, doubtless, some mystic
attraction about F, but to a common or Philistine person
it appears incomprehensible why these composers write
so much about such very ordinary letters, and why
others lavish so much care on interpreting and explaining
them. Seems to me I would be interested to hear a Dirge
of the Drought (instead of B flat) and I would like to
drink in the melody of a Symphony of Falling Waters—the
Break of the Drought. Then I might expect to bring
before my eyes the deathly wildernesses I had seen, or
the springing of new' hopes and grass, as the blessed rain
fell; but there is no inspiration about G tame or D dull—
they have no soul.
Perth is likely to have a decent theatre at last; the
magistrates have granted a provisional license for it—
and, of course, also for the inevitable accompanying
place where you inquire about a bottled dog. The plans
provide for an outlay of £20,000.
Rignold says : “I have never lost, money on Shake-
spearian productions.”
“ Skull ,? f'Heard : the late Dick. Stewart tell of his
earliest Australian effort to introduce a little realism into
Shakespeare. It was somewhere in Ballarat district in the
early days—at the old Charley Napier, I imagine, and “As
You Like It ” was to be staged. There was at that time a
notorious Geordie wrestler in Ballarat, a very popular
character, and it occurred to the management that it
would be a good idea to put up the old Tynesider as
Charles. Tynesider was agreeable. He’d undertake to
throw the whole cdmpany for a fiver. It was found
difficult to convince him that he must allow Orlando the
fall, but at length, in response to many protestations, he
promised to obey.. But when the time came Charles the
Wrestler practically- threw Orlando into the middle of
next week and the hero' remained upon the boards,
wrecked,; Then there was trouble. The manager ran on
yelling profanity. “ You idiot ' What have you
done f” he shrieked. " Think of the play I ” “ Play, be
domnied.! ” replied Gedrdie coolly, “ thoo think o’ my
reputation.” The story sounds very old, but it probably
happened to several managers. The idea of getting
realism on the cheap by hiring a genuine wrestler comes
to every manager who plays “As You Like It.” And
when the hulking one is seized by a weed named Orlando
who couldn’t wrestle with a setting hen, the temptation
to throw him away with one hand is very great.
Lately appearing at Kalgoorlie (W.A.) a couple of
heavy ladies known as the “Giant Sisters.” Weights,
respectively, 39st. 91bs., and 36st.
An Australian in Johannesburg writes that he recently
paid ten “ bob ” for a seat at the Standard Theatre to see
Hall’s Australian Juveniles in “The Transit of Venus.”
As a loyal member of the Commonwealth he could have
endured the infliction of the performance if he had felt
persuaded that Hall’s Juveniles were genuine Australian
natives, but he couldn’t detect any aroma of the gum-
tree and the wattle in their amateurish display. Where
did Hall raise those Juveniles, and who is Hall, anyhow ?
“ Theorbo ”: Melba and boy Kruse, the Australian
violinist, tried a joint Australian tour about 15 years ago,
but it broke down financially.
“Pro.”: The incident of one of the Florehz acrobats
smashing a hole through the Bendigo stage recalls a bit
of greenroom fungi about that city. It is said that when
Lola Montes was acting there, in a piece called “ Asmodeus,
or the Little Devil,” a thunderstorm burst over the
theatre, and lightning actually shivered a plank of the
stage. Lola complimented the manager on his effects.
Madeleine Lucette Ryley makes one of the characters
in “ Mice and Men,” which is timed about the beginning
of the last century, say to the experimenting Professor:
“ I won’t interfere--it’s your funeral! ”

London, lately ventured into straight-out comedy, allee
same as Nellie, but with less success.
Fitzsimmons, following the example of John L. Sullivan
and other ’Murkan heavyweights, has taken to the stage,
and plays lead in “ The Honest Blacksmith ” at the
Columbia Theatre, ’Frisco. Fitz.’s dramatic work con-
sists mainly in shoeing horses and punching the enemies
of virtue. The play is a melodrama in four rounds.
G. V. Brooke was acting at Drury Lane, in 1854, while
Phelps was doing “ A Midsummer Night’s Dream ” at
Sadler’s Wells. One night Phelps, in his part ot Bottom,
said he could roar like a lion. Voice from the gallery
" Can you roar like Brooke ? ”

that the ballet girl of our time is always as young as she
looks from the back stalls or the gallery (glasses barred)

of his chorus “ girls ” from in front was a handsome St.
Kilda dame who has a daughter of 18 or 19 and a son of
21. As in the days of yore, the sprightly young thing

at the .” “Devil a fear!” was the reply “That’s
me 1 ” Washer-woman by day, ballet-£ft>( by night. How’s
that for industry ? ”
In a recent London melodrama.the .villain is known as
the Vulture on account of his huge bird-like beak and
ten clawy Angers. The beak and claws are expected to
be taken quite seriously by the blood-curdled audience,
and one of the characters in the drama is a learned
professor, who buys the right of dissecting Vulture’s
body after death, in the hope of discovering a “rudi:
mentary gizzard.” The awful human bird turns virtuous
at the Anish, cuts his claws, and removes his beak out of
the drama, and takes his hook. A half-formed hope on
the part of the audience that he might be dissected
publicly in the last act thus falls through.
It was predicted by Mrs. Brown Potter when she made
her theatrical cUbut in 1887 that she would never
become a second Mrs. Langtry.” All the same the
Chicago lady with the strange elocution, angular eccen-
tricities; and plastered brow has achieved better work
than Mrs. Langtry was ever capable of.
She was an actress ; he was an actor. She had a book ;
he had a look. “Love Me For Ever," he read. “Quite
an out-of-date-book,” she said.
A long-suffering theatrical co. in a certain town, the
other day, had to postpone its performance because a
party billed to play the part of a “ Ane old English gen-
tleman ” was held-up in his bedroom by an irate and
powerful publican, who had found him removing his
duds piecemeal, aud suspected a Aitting. He locked the
suspected one up in a top-storey bedroom, and refused
toJet him out until the co. manager sent down a ransom,
covering arrears of board. The frenzy of the caged actor
as the hour for his appearance at the theatre approached
and passed, rivalled the battle scene in “ Henry V.”
Most people talk of May Beatty’s curious voice. As a
matter of fact, the scarcely deAnable something that
makes it curious—sort of edgy metallic underlay—is not
unique, but has frequently been associated with success
in Miss Beatty’s line of comedy. Seems, indeed, almost
as if a suggestion of not necessarily tuneless brassiness in
an actress’s voice betokens at times that rare and precious
thing in woman, a sense of humor. Nellie Farren had
more than a suspicion of it, and the incomparable Judic
had it notably. Among later and lesser stars, it is con-
spicuous in May Yohe. As to Miss Beatty, she is winsome
and funny ; she has grit, sufficient audacity, and a con-
siderably developed artistic sense. Her trouble will
probably be that in a year or two she’ll be several sizes
too fat.

The incident of the lighted cigar in “ Sherlock .Holmes ”
has been adapted to a new sensation in a recent melo-
drama, where the villain handsomely challenges the hero
to Aght a cigarette duel. They each light a cigarette,
then plunge themselves into darkness by turning out the
gas (the scene is a club-room), and shoot with revolvers
at their respective danger signals. The villain nobly
keeps his cigarette between his teeth, instead of planting
it on the mantel-shelf or elsewhere, and so gets killed for
his fair dealing—a quite unusual fate for a villain.
Ellen Terry, playing in “ The Merry Wives of Windsor,”
atßeerbohm Tree’s theatre, was quite proud to let it be
known that she had a grand-daughter Aguring in the
same show, as one of the little fairies who pinch Falstaff.
How old is Nora M‘Kay, the promising short-skirted
Castles co. violinist? Judged by her frocks, Nora should
be 17 at most; but the lines of her face and chin . are
more matured, her covert glances are astute and of this
world, and she has womanly and knowledgeable ankles.
Differently coiffured and frocked and corseted, she would
easily pass for 25.
