At Poverty Point.
In Melbourne, a Collins-st. tobacconist cleared over
£lOO through trafficking in Melba tickets, but the syndi-
cate dealing in them in Sydney did not fare so well. For
the first concert, all tickets were disposed of, but only a
very few at a premium. The second concert was a
disastrous one, 13 guinea tickets remaining unsold. The
postponement of the remaining two events proved a God-
send, more especially as the management refunded
moneys to those whom the altered arrangements did not
suit. Unsold tickets were gladly exchanged for cash,
and a probable loss of £5O was changed to an even-money
event. Experience showed that there were comparatively
few buyers fqr guinea tickets, though half-guinea and ss.
seats were anxiously sought after. In Melbourne, the
guinea tickets were the most eagerly snapped.
The wildly'.ridiculous adulation that Marshall Hall
heaped upon Melba when she visited his Melbourne Con-
servatorium of Music can hardly have been quite sincere.
Marshall Hall is a sworn apostle of heavy German musical
art and a persistent scoffer at the florid Italian and French
compositions which Melba sings. Only a year or two ago,
when Musgrove was producing Wagnerian opera in Mel-
bourne under the blessing of the German cult, the critics
of the local dailies referred to the favorite airs of Doni-
zetti and Verdi as old-fashioned, meaningless things,
written for a soulless generation of opera-goers.
Booking arrangements for the Melba concerts, judging
by letters in the newspapers, were most unsatis-
factory. Scores of people allege all sorts of dodgery.
Pity that it should be so, for nothing turns the public
against a management more quickly than hanky-panky.
Mark your goods in plain figures and sell at those prices
without favor is good policy in more places than a
draper’s shop.

enders will be called for in about a fortnight, and the

“X ”: Herr Pagel, the “ German Hereules,” has been
touring the country to marvellous business. Must have
netted £5O at Maryborough (Vic.), Pagel, a flaxen giant,
is engineered by his wife, who stridently sets forth the
wonders of his performance to the listening: crowd outside
his marquee. It would be hard to overrate Pagel’s per-
formance. He lifts a horse high into the air without
much difficulty, and carries a pony and boy rider round
the tent on one arm. He lifts weights, too, but a mere
Sandow can do that. Also hoists a lion over his head.
Bulletin said (11/10/’O2) that “ the lion’s remarks when
he came down would be worth listening to.” They were ;
I heard them, and so did Pagel, for, after hoisting the
king of beasts into the air, Pagel had to escape hurriedly
from the cage, so threatening was the demeanor and so
angry the growls of aforesaid monarch. After this
demonstration Pagel let the lion alone for the rest of the
day. It is a wonder to me that the German doesn’t get
some good manager to run him. It is a pity to see the
strong man acting as his own check taker. His day per-
formances require a lot of endurance, seeing that at shows
he would probably perform six or seven times running as
fast as the tent could empty and re-fill. Such work must
kill him soon.
“ Looping-the-loop,” writes The Bulletin’s London
correspondent, “is far and away the most petrifying
pastime that has ever struck these shores. Coney Island
and the U.S. have known it for long, but it is only re-
cently the Crystal Palace has added it to its swamp of
amusements. Writer looped amidst all the mental
terrors of a smashing demise. You enter a small open
car, holding four in two compartments. A wooden bar
swings over your legs and into your waist. Gripping this
and pushing your shoulders hard against the seat-back,
you jam your feet against the footboard and sit rigid.
The car is hauled up an incline of about 50ft. Then
slowly you glide along a square 0 of gentle gradient till
suddenly you shoot a precipice of 60ft. or so—so steep
that no portion of the track is visible after the plunge.
The terrific impulse carries the car right up inside a 40ft.
diameter ring, round and out the other side. Centrifugal
force alone keeps the passengers in their seats, and it is
irresistibly crushing. Try as you will, the shoulders are
drawn forward, the body doubled up ; you feel your chin
pressing on your corns, apparently ; and the clattering,
hammering, clanging noise is like'4ooo of Tophet’s forges
in full swing. You come out with an exhilarated feeling
and a much-crumpled collar.”
Mr. E. W. Witton, of Melbourne, wishes
to point out that the petition for the release of
his brother, Lieut. Witton (of the Bush veldt
Carbineers), which has just been refused by King
Edward, was a private one, signed by 10 members
of the Witton family only. The public petition,
which has already 30,000 signatures, has not yet
been presented; and those in favor of its prayer
should arrange to sign it at once.
August Tisler, the Dandenong murderer,
was hanged in the odor of sanctity last Monday.
The pious statements which he made in his last
moments were almost word for word the same as
those of dozens of other assassins. Apparently
the parson dictates the statement every time, for
even the most ungrammatical murderer gets it off
in good English and puts the comma in beauti-
fully. If the evidence of those confessions goes
for anything, Paradise must be full of saved homi-
cides.
Christianity produces few such practical
altruists as Goodyer Singh, a Punjaubee hawker
who died recently at Temora (N.S.W.). Goodyer’s
amiable dementia began with the presentation of
£l2O worth of goods to whomsoever liked to pick
the stuff up from where he had thrown it in the
street. Next he was heard of as feeding miscel-
laneous cattle that used to assemble at his camp;
and, as he went as high as £8 in one week for
fodder, his money soon ran out; whereupon he
slew his horses, and proceeded to starve himself to
death. When the police got wind of the matter,
and removed him to hospital, he was too far gone
to be brought round.
Grit. A 12-year-old boy, living near
Milparinka (N.S.W.), who recently went out on
horseback looking for some horses, was thrown,
and got a thigh badly broken. He crawled
around until he found a stick (the only one to be
found), which had very jagged ends, and with the
help of this he hopped round for two hours ere he
could catch his horse ; then he climbed up on the
off side (the broken leg being the left) and rode 12
miles home. When he called out for someone to
come and lift him off, as his leg was broken, they
thought.lie was joking, but found the leg swinging
and the palm of the hand badly lacerated by the
jagged "stick he was obliged to use in hopping
round after the horse.
The Lewis-Bird Ministry is surely
doomed; even the monsters of the deep arise
in protest against it. _ T’other week a massive
searleppard was beaten off at the very gates of
Parliament House; now a ferocious-looking
whale, has been spouting adjacent to Hobart
wharf. _ All the local whaling skippers are busy
getting.crews together and sharpening harpoons
for the attack. If the whale is beaten off ere it
charges ashore and swallows the House of Lewis
and Dodery, the sea-serpent will probably con-
stitute the next reinforcement. Both Houses
hurriedly adjourned, immediately the whale hove
in sight, “to attend the agricultural show”; but
such an excuse is considered totally inadequate.
Full-grown whales have not been seen in the
Derwent since 1822, though a little “ nine-tunner ”
was captured near Port Arthur in ’9O.
“Galvo” compares the construction and design
of three of the principal electric tramway systems
of Australasia:
In the Sydney system the overhead construction is
splendid, and the electrical arrangements generally well,
designed and carried out; but the track work is abomin-
' able; The rails used vary in weight from 831 b. per yard
down to 451 b., and considering the enormous traffic, are
far too light and lacking in stiffness. Stand in George-
street, any day, and watch one of the heavy coupled cars
go by, and the rails will be seen to buckle and undulate
like a clothes-line shaken from the end. The result is
that the wood blocks on either side of the road project
one above the other and have the appearance of a cog-
rack. Another peculiarity of Sydney track work is that
the blocks laid between tracks are not tarred, but simply
grouted in with cement and sand. About a year ago I
saw several hundred yards of such newly-laid track in
George-st. West “stand up and look at itself” during a
wet spell, owing to the blocks, having absorbed water.
Sydney tracks are continually under repair.
Perth (W.A.), on the other hand, has very bad over-
head work, but really splendid track-construction, with
921 b. rails in the city and 851 b. in the suburbs. The cost
of maintenance is practically nil, owing to the solidity of
the work. Of course, the amount of traffic in the two
cities cannot be compared, but the fact that 921 b. rails
are considered necessary for a small town like Perth
emphasises the weakness of the Sydney work.
Auckland (M.L.), again, where the construction, as in
Perth, is in American hands, has 921 b. rails throughout,
and the under-track work is particularly heavy and
good. By the way, it may be mentioned here that one of
the finest pieces of “special work” (i.e., crossings and
curves) is to be seen in Auckland, where two tracks which
intersect at right angles have curves connecting them in
all four directions. I know of no other similar piece of
work in Australasia. The overhead construction is also
very fine, being in marked contrast to Perth in that
respect. Light and very handsome iron centre-poles are
used throughout, while the “ feeder” cables (cables which
feed the current to the trolly wires at intervals) are
carried underground through the heart of the city, thus
rendering the work less unsightly than usual.
Adelaide and Dunedin have electric systems in course
of design, and as the engineering is in American hands,
it is safe to predict that rails heavier than those in Sydney
will be used. Reason of Sydney’s singularity in this
respect is hard to understand when the substantial nature
of the rest of the construction is taken into consideration.

“Araxes”: Why does not the Labor member
east off the mantle of ultra-respectability . with
which he has enshrouded himself, and poke his
nose into the question of wholesale leasing of
public bores in N.S.W. ? No sooner are these
finished, as a rule, than they are leased to wool-
scourers at a nominal rent for a long term. Appli-
cants for work at these scours are offered sweating
wages and conditions. In the N. W., in the vicinity
of the Boomi Creek, selectors’ sheep are being fed
on scrub, and are dying for want of water, while
not a mile away bore-water running in drains is
blocked, simply because Govt, will not pay the
proprietor of the bore (leased from Govt.) for the
water to be run there ! Govt, is already paying
the proprietor for water from its own bore for the
use of the carriers’ horses !

The recent Trades Congress in England shows
that the workers have at last awakened to the
futility of meeting periodically merely to pass
“resolutions” which the hereditary Tories in
power treat with steady contempt. Thanks to the
goad of the adverse finding by the Law Lords in
the Taff Yale matter, the Trades Unions are now
well forward with plans for contesting about 60
seats at next general election. Money and candi-
dates are both forthcoming, and it is confidently
expected that 30 or more seats will be won. Some
of the election cries are to be —“The appropria-
tion of a slice of the £26,000,000 of poor rates to a
fund for old age pensions,” “free and secular
education,” “better housing for the working
classes,” “clearing away tlie anomalies of the
fiscal system,” “a more equitable system of land-
taxation. ’ ’ Many English papers—even those hostile
—show by. their comments that they consider a
success for Labor assured. And the big new
“ Labor party,” that is not yet elected, is already
receiving much advice about how to hold the
“balanc4 of power.”

In 1891, according to Coghlan’s statistics,
there were 47,958 hands employed in N.S.W. fac-
tories, and wages were £4,272,704, or £B9 Is. 10a.
each. In 1901 the employees numbered 66,135, and
the wages were £4,943,079, or £74 14s. lOd. each.
Is this great fall attributable to Reid s dry dog
policy, or what ?
Horace Tozer informs Queensland, in his
last annual report, that he carefully advertises his
State, and “makes an endeavor to draw a
picture of life in Queensland which shall be
correct and attractive” to possible emigrants.
Does Horace tell them about the ruined pas-
toralists, struggling farmers, decimated flocks and
herds, and hungry unemployed who just now
swarm in Queensland. If not —why not ? Are
immigrants to be brought out under false pre-
tences ?
From S.M. Herald (21/10/’O2)
The desire of the Johannesburg Chamber of Mines to
claim exemption from taxation for five years is perhaps
natural. But it was understood from the first that the
mining industry should share in the cost of the war. It
was the outlanders of Johannesburg largely who were
responsible for it.
Quite true, though the truth comes rather late in
the day. And what an awful liar the Herald
must have been all these last three years !
It has been proved that Napoleon was a
colossal forger. When he got his grip on Vienna he
obtained complete specifications of the Austrian
apparatus for making bank-notes. This he
duplicated in Paris. His next move was to bribe
his way to getting a similar duplication of the
Russian mechanism, which was not very elaborate.
Thirdly, he insinuated his agents somehow into
the Bank of England, and duplicated its appa-
ratus. He fabricated great numbers of Austrian,
Russian and English notes. His idea was to
destroy the note credit of those nations and force
them to pay in gold, while he annexed all the gold
he could. However, he does not seem to have
made any impression.
The Death of a Lake.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Dry/” said the old fisherman. “Ilived near
Lake George for 50 years, and know as much
about it as any man living. I never thought
it could go dry. It has a belt of dead timber all
round it; and when a scientific man happened
along, we Lake people always wanted to know
the cause of their decay, but could never get any
satisfaction. Some said it was one thing, and
some another. The nearest to the truth, I think,
was the fellow who said it was caused by the
water rotting the roots. Of course water will rot
anything. But these trees all died suddenly in a
week.
“In 1870, Mr. Russell, the astronomer, was up
there. He established an observatory to report
the rise and the fall of the water. His occupation
is gone now. Some people spoke about the tides
there ; but who ever heard of a tide on an inland
lake ? Anyway, I never quite knew what made
the water rise and fall. There seemed to me
something uncanny about it. And now, for the
blessed thing to go and dry up ! The lake basin
is 20 miles by 8, and used to be fed from the Bun-
gendore end, by the Deep and Turalla Creeks,
which rise in the Black Range, way up near Cap-
tain’s Flat. The creeks, in my time, were
narrow, and a couple of good showers started ’em
agoing. Since then, I believe, they have widened
considerably, and it takes a flood to put anything
you could see into them. That may account for
the lake going dry. You’d think the widening of
the creeks would be all the better for the lake;
and so it would be if there was any rain like we
used to have.
“I’ve seen Lake George up to Bungendore;
and into the back yard of the old Lake George
Inn. You could sail a boat out of the yard to
Collector, 26 miles away. Collector is at the
southern end of the lake, and it is bounded on the
west by a range of hills, over which is Gundaroo.
“There was a time before when the old lake
started to dry, and we all thought she was a
“goner,” but she recovered herself again. Then
she formed herself into two basins ; and I drove a
bullock team between them from Carey’s Gap, on
Gundaroo side, to Cooper’s Willeroo station. It
put me in mind of Moses and his crowd going
across the Red Sea. We had the average rainfall
that season too; and it has always appeared to
me that there was an upheaval of mud in the
centre, which caused the two basins to form. If
so, the mud fell back again, as, when the lake
righted herself, there was deeper water there
than anywhere else. I wouldn’t be a bit sur-
prised to hear that the old bath has been leaking,
and that the water has got away into the bowels
of the earth.
“ Fish there ! You’ve just about said it; and
I’ve been wondering all the time I’ve been talk-
ing what could have become of them. There were
tons of ’em. They were all Murray cod, and I
remember the first lot that was put there by Ter-
rence Aubrey Murray. You’ve heard of him, of
course. They were pretty tidy-sized fish, some of
them. I remember one weighing 123£1b. Poor
Lake George 1 ”

SYDNEY.
Concerning Loyalty.
A song of Loyalty—a song of Pride—a song of
the dues that are Manhood’s and Manhood s only !
A song of him who walks erect —a paean to the
neck that never bends, the stiff spine, the free
heart, and the fearless bosom. Where shall we
find them ? , ,
Shall we seek among the portly paunches of
eminent aldermen, the adipose plenitude of in-
fluential publicans, the verbose brag of bibulous
politicians, the frequent supplications of the
pious and puny ? .
Does a manly heart desire the pitiable adorn-
ment of a paltry bauble ? Does a teeming brain
beget a yearning for the tinsel of trivial titles ?
Does a stajwart soul yearn to kiss the fat fingers
of a crapulous princelet ?
If there be greatness in the simple hero —can
there be grandeur in the simple groveller ?
It seems to me the sin of sins—this venal vice
of mean abasement, —
This gilding wood to look like gold —this carving
of marble tombs to hold- but clay and poor cor-
ruption ; this pouring paeans to the proud who
scorn the lips that pour them; this is, I say, the
Sin of Sins !
There is need for a new creed—a new gospel—
a gospel ot pride, of stiff backs, of noble work and
noble self-reliance;
A creed of less kneeling, less genuflection, more
upright bearing towards Man, less creeping sub-
servience to the free God who loves the free glance
of his own freemen.
I see in many a deity merely the apotheosis of
a fat despot, crowned by cruelty, throned by fear,
surrounded by scourges, Sheolie brimstone and a
multitude of Paradisaical sugar-plums.
There is need, indeed, for a new attitude —
There is need, indeed, for the proclamation of a
true pride as a manly virtue.
And, above all, there is need that a vigorous
manhood should especially brand this venal vice
of mean abasement!
Q.
P.L.
The Illegitimate.
Mary Gilmore writes re illegitimacy:—
No amount of legislation will blot out the stigma of
illegitimacy ; the stigma is not in being illegitimate ; it
lies in having that kind of mother. All that the law can
do is to fix the question of inheritance—of legal standing.
The stigma can only be removed by a change of conven-
tion. The conventional is a people’s interpretation of a
sub-conscious knowledge. When the raison d’etre of the
illegitimacy convention is understood we will probably
know why the woman suffers and the man goes scot free
in these things. A' convention is good till we have evolved
past the stage that produced it, though it may live till a
newer convention kills it. But no matter how logically
absurd a convention may have become, no woman can
break; it without being either below the general standard,
or so much above it that she follows a higher law than
that of the general. If there be no higher law (I refer
solely to the moral) the higher type of woman invariably
conforms to the conventional. “Like mates with like,”
or as near like as circumstances allow. Hence, the
woman who falls below the conventional standard attracts
the man of like level. Thus the stigma of parentage is
doubled. The conventional requires that the infant be
legitimate, and for the well-being of the race it should be
so. The Spaniard, all over the world, is notoriously
illegitimate arid decadent. The life history ;of
the world is -punctuated by the death of nations
that have gone out in a whirl of illegitimacy, childless-
ness (as regards the family), and general immorality.
TnE Bulletin seems to incline to the opinion that the
illegitimate is the child of the strong. The illegitimate
is oply the child of the strong where the convention is
puritanical, for, in such a case, the weak fear to break
through for fear of consequences, and, consequently,
only the strong dare. In relation to the stigma as it
concerns the man and the woman, I think one may
safely say that the nearer the moral tone of a place
approaches that of the city, the less blume attaches to
the man. In real country places the rule is for the girl
to be pitied and the man to find it necessary to clear out.
But the stigma remains all the same. It is always there,
and will always be there while our idea of national
health remains that of the race from which we came
♦
“Burglar Bill” on the present jury-system :
The Briton’s boast of a fair trial by twelve of his peers
is mostly bosh. I’ve been there. On trial once myself
and noticed a friend amongst the jurymen. When he
was called I didn’t bother challenging him. He being a
friend, it might have hurt his feelings to think that 1 did
not consider him a fit and proper person to try my case.
So be formed one of the dozen. The Judge summed up
at 4.25 p.m. dead against me, and the jury retired. They
came back at 7 p.m. with a verdict against my two
fellow prisoners (as one of them admitted his guilt at
the lower court, his Honor did not bother asking
whether it was “Guilty” or “Not Guilty”), but
the foreman intimated that they could not agree re-
garding me. His Honor questioned the foreman as
to whether there were any points of law he could ex-
Elain that would enable them to come to a decision, and
eing answered in the negative, informed them that he
would wait till 10 p.m. to take their verdict. If they had
not agreed then he would lock them up for the night.
At 10.5 they returned, and my friend, who had previously
occupied a front seat, sneaked into a back one. So I
didn’t wSnt to hear the foreman speak to know it was all
up. I spoke to my friend, the juryman, after my release,
and he told me that at 7 p.m., when the jury came back
the first time, he believed me innocent, but he was
talked into a conoiction before 10. He and one other
were the only two who held out for “ Not Guily,”
and he told me that he put up with more insults
during that 5J hours than he had during the preceding 12
months. One.bully, who wanted to convict before leaving
the box (mind you, it wasn’t so glaring as that) told him
that he couldn’t see a hole through a ladder and each of
the others asserted that his stupidity would be the cause
of their being locked up for the night. How many hur-
ried verdicts are the result of that threat? My friend is
a mild, inoffensive man, so I forgave him. Now what I
want is to give, the inoffensive man a chance to hand
in his own opinion, not the opinion of the bully
who sits alongside him. Discussing the evidence
amongst themselves flattens out the weak-willed men,
who, though possessing more intelligence, allow themselves
to be dominated by bullies whose lungs are stronger than
their perceptive faculties. Give me four loud-mouthed
ruffians and I will get the verdict every time. To
remedy this : box each juryman up by himself, give him
a reasonable time to consider the evidence—say from one
hour'upwards (no man’s liberty should be forfeited under
an hour)—and then let him hand out his opinion, and let
a substantial majority decide. This will give the mild man
a chance to send in the result of his own careful deliber-
ation on the evidence placed before him ; it will also do
away with that infernal threat of “ lock up for the night.”
I know lots of men who were brought in guilty at 10 p.m.
[“ Burglar Bill ” somehow recalls the story
of the pathetic old Irish lady charged
with shooting a bailiff, or some such trifling
matter. ;'Shie was grey-headed and unde-
fended* ‘My poor woman,” said the Judge,
sympathetically, “ have you not got counsel
to assist you?” “No sorr,” she replied, tremu-
lously. “ But plaze your Honor I’ve got a frind
on the jury.” The jury system can’t be changed
because “ Burglar Bill’s ” “friend on the jury ” is
a somewhat weak-minded gentleman. “Burglar
Bill ” should choose his friends with more dis-
cretion. No man, however timorous, would surelv
abandon an honest opinion of innocence ; though
he might be argued out of a dishonest one. The
essence of the jury system is the consultation that
it allows of. Only point on which “ B.B.” gets
home is the locking-up threat. It certainly is
scandalous —the way in which some judges bdlly
juries on that point.—Ed. B.]


“ Colocynth, M.D.” on a delicate matter :
I’m afraid the cry for a White Australia will
be largely discounted by an important factor little
known to the lay public. I refer to the wide-
spread desire among Australian matrons to interrupt
normal gestation. So largely, indeed, are measures em-
ployed for this unlawful purpose that hardly one |in 20
married women can be considered quite innocent of this
charge. And most bare-facediy do t'hese women appeal
to doctors for assistance, and, when the iniquity of the
request is pointed out by the physician, they are un-
feignedly surprised, and often leave him in great dudgeon,
with the statement that they’ll apply to someone less
squeamish. The birth-rate of Australasia is far below
what it should be, and the reason is apparent to the
members of the medical profession who are unable to
prevent the growing evil. Any doctor of experience
will substantiate these statements.

In the very same issue Melb. Argus which
chronicles the wearisome platitudes of the chamber
of Derham, Blyth and co., as to the “vicious
minimum wage principle having reduced the out-
put of the factories, lessened employment, and
driven away capital ” (7/10/’O2), that stodgy
journal remarked in a sub-leader that “the Vic-
torian factories have been fuller of orders of late
than ever they were before.” It might have gone
further, and mentioned that Victoria’s factories
had grown faster under the Factories Act and
minimum wage law than they ever did before.
The growth of the Vic. factories during the years
when they were being ruined by socialistic legisla-
tion was a most remarkable circumstance.

CONSUMPTION.
TOO ILL TO LEAVE HIS BED.
A COMPLETE CURE.
Mr. W. G. Hearne.—Dear Sir, —I ain writing to tell you
about the wonderful cure your medicine has effected in
my case. About three years ago I began to cough. At
first the cough was not severe, but it gradually got worse,
and I became very weak and troubled with night sweats,
pain in my chest, and great quantities of phlegm. On
several occasions there was blood in the expectorated
matter. I had been treated by a doctor, who pronounced
my case to be Consumption, and various other treatments
had been tried, but without benefit. It was at this stage
that I heard of your Bronchitis Cure, and sent to you for
a course of the medicine. When it arrived I was too ill to
leave my bed, but I commenced taking it at once, and
gradually improved. lam glad to say that the tyvo lots
of medicine you sent have effected a complete cure, for
which accept my very bestthanks.—Yours gratefully,
J. BLAIR.
Westminster Bridge-road, S.E., London.

Your letter and Bronchitis Cure to hand quite safe.

have tried to get it made up here, and let my chemist

week he can make nothing out of it, he never saw any-
thing like it before, so there is only one thing for me to
do—send for more. I have never kept in bed one day
since I commenced to take it. I used to be in bed a fort-
night at a time always, and after that for months I was
as weak as I could possibly be, and was always taking
cod liver oil, so you will see at once it is quite worth
while sending for it such a long distance.
“ Something more I must tell you. Charlotte has been
very ill since I wrote you. Her cough was so bad she
never had a night’s rest, and was spitting up blood
very much. The doctor told her husband that there was
nothing more he could do for her. So on the Sunday I
sent her half a bottle of the Bronchitis Cure, and told
her to try it, and if she did not use it, not to waste it,
but send it back again. She had such confidence in her
doctor that I thought she would not try it. On the
Wednesday I sent over again, and she was much better,
the night’s rest was very good, and cough and bleeding
from the lungs better. She sent for another half-bottle,
and on the following Sunday sent over to say that she
was quite cured and did not require any more medicine.
So you see what great good it has done, and she wishes
to have some of my next supply.”

KUTNOW’S POWDER
Kutnow’s Powder assures a
perfect Digestion, and speedily
dispels Headache, Neuralgia,
Anaemia, Sleeplessness, Loss of
Appetite and Energy caused
by Imperfect Digestion and
Mal-Assimilation.
The work of removing the waste matters that result from
the digestive processes falls chiefly on the bowels, liver, and
kidneys. If these organs become disordered and inactive,
Constipation and Dyspepsia occur, and should they stop
working altogether for a few hours, death would result from
the accumulated poisons in the body. While these poisons
are not harmful when present in a normal quantity, they
propagate disease if allowed to accumulate in the system.
Perfect action of all these eliminative organs is therefore
necessary to keep the body in sound health. The gateways
of excretion must be kept free, and the filters must be clean.
This is now readily accomplished by using KUTNOW’S
POWDER, which cleanses and stimulates the Bowels, Liver
and Kidneys, and when deranged and sluggish restores them
to a strong, healthy, active condition. As the organs regain
their ability to perform their several functions, the blood
will become purer, the nerves stronger, the appetite keener,
sleep sounder, and the skin healthier. Headache, bilious-
ness, and drowsiness will vanish, and the entire body be
rendered stronger and more vigorous.
The use of “ liver pills ” in the treatment of liver dis-
orders may in most instances be compared to the whipping
of an exhausted horse when each application of the scourge
only tends to hasten entire collapse.
KUTNOW’S POWDER is an aperient in which the
drastic, lowering, and nauseous features of the ordinary
aperient medicine are entirely absent. It is composed of
the most curative, healing, and purifying constituents of the
European mineral-spring waters, scientifically blended with
other health-giving ingredients.
KUTNOW’S POWDER does for the Stomach, Liver,
and Kidneys what a course of physical exercise does for the
body ; that is to say, it puts it in the finest possible condi-
tion, and creates within the system a power of resistance
which practically renders the body proof against disease in
general and any epidemic in particular.

The Cuckoo.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Evison sat scowling at the magazine in his hand,
inwardly cursing the howling rain that kept him
at home. He glanced across the room at his wife
reclining on her couch, and his scowl deepened.
He resented her plainness, the lines made by the
illness that had made her home her prison. His
gloomy thoughts travelled back. What a young
ass he’d been ! At twenty-four one is very un-
willing to die, and he had been very grateful to
the woman who had dragged him, by her devoted
nursing, from the very shadow of the Hereafter,
and thought it was something deeper. Their first
years of poverty haunted him still. Her ceaseless
illness had robbed her of her shadowy prettiness,
and drained away every penny he made. Now he
was fairly rich and wanted a wife, handsome and
young, to take about, and to fill his house with
people of the right sort.
He threw the magazine down. “What rot
they put in these things! The rain’s stopping.
I’m going out, Lucy.”
A shadow crossed the woman’s tired face.
“ Can you wait a little while, Frank ? ” she said
timidly; “I want to ask you something.”
“ Not that adoption business again ’—all right.
What is it?”
“I want to ask my cousin, Elsie Markham, to
come and live with us. The Markhams live in
Otago, and are very poor. I have not seen her
since she was a girl; she was very pretty then.
It is rather lonely for me all day, and I know it is
dull for you in the evenings.”
“Ask her, by all means ; you might have done
so long ago. Can’t stay any longer. Damn it!
Where’s my coat ? Ah ! Good night! ”
She still loved him and would see nothing but
the handsome, passionate youngster who would
not take her hesitating ‘* No ” only five years ago.
She had wanted to adopt one of those poor surplus
mites whom “kind ladies” escort to their early
graves; take it and save it and love it, but he had
absolutely refused her; spoke of hereditary crimi-
nals; and the motherhood in her yearned hope-
lessly.
* * « *
“ This is Elsie, Frank.” Evison started, having
forgotten the expected arrival. He had looked for
a gawky, countryfied girl, and here was the woman
of his dreams; tail, beautifully proportioned, with
almost olive skin —the red just showing through—
and blue-black hair.
She smiled at his evident amazement. “ Please
don’t turn on me, I’m very docile.”
Evison did not fill his house with people of the
right sort; he couldn’t think of any. He rarely
went out at night now, and his boon-companions
chaffed him for his sudden relapse into domestic
virtue. He was madly in love with the girl. And
she, hitherto chained by poverty to a bare exist-
ence in a little country town, found it Paradise
to her starved nature. He took her to theatres,
dances, and drove her everywhere. ■ He bought
her the pretty things a woman loves, laughingly
claiming his privilege of cousinship. During the
day she waited on Mrs. Evison, read to her, and
hummed about the house like a beautiful bird.
“ Frank is too good to me, Lucy ; and I often
think I should not let him do so much for me.
But, oh ! it is heavenly after that dreadful place.
Will you keep me always ? ”
“My dear, I hope you will never want to leave
us; my illness has prevented me being much of a
companion to Frank, and now he is pleased, I am
happy. Besides, I could not part with you now
for my own selfish sake.”
It was a happy year to the girl.
• * *
“ 1 can’t understand what has come over you,
Elsie,” Mrs. Evison said, looking down at the
girl, who was sitting on a cushion, her head
against the couch ; “you are so quiet now, and
you’ve lost all your beautiful color.” She laid her
hand on the other’s head. The girl winced.
“Tell me, little girl ... I know. You’re in
love, and ”
“ Oh ! no ! no ! ” and Elsie burst into tears and
hurried from the room.
That night Evison was late. He wanted the
dark to speak in. He went to his wife’s room.
“ Elsie wants to go away.”
“Go away ! Oh, Frank ! what shall Ido with-
out her? The poor child is worrying about some-
thing. Does she want to go home ? ”
‘‘No ! she has some friends in Sydney. I think
we’d better let her go. It’s a confounded nuisance,
though, as I must go to Melbourne in a day or two
on a business I can’t dodge. I’ll wire your sister
to come and stay with you.”
* * * *
“Isn’t he a little darling, Frank? It was so
good of you to let me have him, dear. It is
wicked, I know ; but I feel almost grateful to li is
parents for dying. But I did miss Elsie so much.
Won’t she ever come back ? It seems ages since
she went, and she writes so seldom. I cannot
understand her.”
Evison had a letter in his pocket, and he felt
reckless. Life was not worth living without her,
and she had refused to let him come near her since
the baby’s birth, and would not accept a penny
from his hands. “I’d drown myself,” she had
said, “only it would be too pleasant. I’ll give
my boy to Lucy, and suffer for my sin.” Well,
let her come, and see if she was strong enough to
repulse him. “Write and ask her again, Lucy,”
he said.
* * * *
The baby, rosy and lusty from his birth, gurgled
aud kicked in the invalid’s arms. The street-door
opened and shut. “ Now you will see the pretty
lady, babbie. Bring Elsie here, Frank ! Oh ! my
dear, I .”
She looked up; a nameless fear froze her
lips, and she turned to her husband. The woman
—a tigerish light in her eyes—tore the baby from
Lucy’s arms: “ Give him to me ! He’s mine ! ”
S. A.
G. ELBURG.
The ancient superstition that the Scotch-
man is the most canny man on earth is altogether
wrong. The Welshman, when careful, can knock
spots off Sandy. Waihemo County Council
(M.L.), recently finding itself in possession of a
full-blown Chinese leper, built him a nice, com-
fortable hut, and the ancient, diseased and
dilapidated premises which he had previously
occupied were ordered by the Health authorities
to be burned. The Council was just about to
arrange for the burning, when a Taffy councillor
arose and said it would be a pity to burn the
shanty down, as it would serve the leper for fire-
wood—he hated waste; and it was only the
difference between burning it gradually and
having one sudden blaze. A lot of the other
councillors were Scotch, and this appeal from the
Welshman got them down. So the Health
Department’s orders were disregarded, and the
old hut is ,to be used to warm the leper in his new
domicile.
A Church Parade.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was Sunday morning, and the column was rest-
ing. This was as it should be, seeing it was the
Day of Rest. For the previous five days the
column had been on the move, the main-body,
escorted by tired and footsore Tommies, doing its
weary two miles an hour. The flanks and rear-
guard, composed of mounted men, mostly colonials,
also did two miles an hour, keeping their allotted
distances from the main body. But the advance-
guard, five miles on ahead, had done various dis-
tances within the hour, and not always in the same
direction. This was accounted for by the presence
of snipers. But now the column was resting. It
had arrived at the place appointed for it, and now
awaited the signal, which was to announce that
“ the cordon was complete ” and the time to strike
had come. It would later have to show cause, in
turn with other columns, why the enemy had
escaped.
_ The 70-odd heavy ox-waggons are drawn up in
line, and the black, yellow, brown and brindle
drivers are lying asleep in their shade, or
cooking at dung fires. A few officers’ tents
are pitched here and there. The men have
erected shelters from the burning sun by means of
rifles, bridles and blankets, with a cunning born
of much practice. The guns are distributed in
likely places with their ugly muzzles pointing
skylinewards. Across a spruit a half-mile or so
away the horses, oxen and mules are grazing, the
latter shepherded by natives and the former by a
mounted guard. On the surrounding skyline
black dots, which sometimes move, denote out-
posts. Occasional shots tell that they and the
snipers are pelting nickel-plated lead at each
other—mostly at a safe distance. This relieves
the tedium of outpost duty and does no one much
harm.
* * * *
In the Bushmen’s lines a “headin ’em ” school
is in full swing. Visitors from Maorilanders’ and
J.L.H. lines help to swell it. On the outskirts
hang a few Tommies, whose mouths are agape at
the sums lost and won at a throw, “ Any part of
afiver’eads!” yells the “boxer.” “Two, four.”
“ Another quid’eads.” “ Your set, Bill.” “Fair
go; let ’em fly ! ” The pennies float upward,
and every head round the ring is raised, as a
chicken raises its head when drinking, as the men
watch the coins in their flight. “Two—outs!”
says Bill, and flings the kip down. And so it goes
on. Presently, there is a row. “ I tell yer I give
yer four quid; this bloke seen me!” “ Yer only give
me three, yer lyin’ cow ! ” They leave the ring to
settle it. Most of the players are too busy to
watch the go. But the pair are soon surrounded
by hangers-on and Kaffir “orderlies.” Bets are
freely made as to whether it was three quid or
four. After a few rounds and much blasphemy it
is decided that it was four.
* •# * •
“Fall in for church-parade in half-an~hour ! ”
yells the orderly sergeant. “ Now then, lads,
put them pennies away and get yer rifles cleaned.
There’ll be an inspection before parade.” This
announcement is hailed with a variety of
curses from the gamblers aud recumbent
figures along the line. The extemporised
shelters are pulled down to an accompaniment of
lurid blasphemy. A corporal, coming along the
line in search of a fatigue party to dig a grave, is
rushed by volunteers anxious to avoid the hated
farce in store for them. He takes six names down
and moves off. “Now then, markers, fall-in.”
The usual “ stripe-hunters ” spring to their places,
ground arms, and stand at ease. The rest fall-in
m a leisurely manner, cursing under their breath.
Arms are inspected by abored-looking captain who
has just been awakened. The men are marched
off to another part of the camp, where
the other regiments are formed up. A hollow
square is made, into which the chaplain presently
steps. The General and his staff and the O.C’s.
of regiments salute. The chaplain is a harmless-
looking little man, who seems to be in earnest and
quite unconscious of the incongruous position he
occupies and the illogical conclusions he arrives
at. He gives out a hymn, “Fight thegood fight,”
and leads off himself. Whether or no the audi-
ence can “fight the good fight,” there is no doubt
it cannot sing about it with any success, and he
finishes a hopeless solo. The men are then given
permission to sit down while he commences his
discourse, taking the text, “Praise be to
God, Who giveth us the Victory.” He
is very much in earnest, and his audi
ence is not. Two men are envying his easy
billet and fat screw in an undertone. A long,
vicious-looking, alleged bushman is relating an
episode with a Kaffir lady, in which he was the
principal actor, to an appreciative audience The
service comes to a close at last with the singing of
“God Save the Queen.” The Tommies in the
front line, with their officers’ eyes upon them, yell
lustily with much contortion of face. Those in
the rear ranks grin and keep silent. Salutes arc
again exchanged, and the parade is dismissed.
G.R.H
In a certain Australian seaport they were
farewelling a kindred soul, to whom, in the course
of the evening, a watch was to be presented.
This duty was to be performed by a retired
“salt,” who, when the time came, arose, and,
awkwardly dangling the watch from his fingers,
started in : —“ I have been called upon —that is—
it is with great pleasure that I—that we—that
you—that, er—er. It is with great pleasure that
we have met here to er—er. Oh ! here — take the
watch, and be done with it ! ”
“Kodak”: Whilst waiting for a friend
one Sunday morning, a derelict, with a watery eye
and other symptoms of rum, wandered up and
told me his tragedy, in a husky voice : “Ruined,
I was—years ago—be a (condemned) Chow ! In
a pub. one night, up Goulburn way, chap, down
on ’is luck, offered t’ sell me fi’-bob ticket in
Tatt.’s fer shillin’. I took it, an’ sold it t’ a Chow
next day fer two bob ! That ticket—my ticket-
won that Chow fi’ hundred quid. Ruined me.
Got a tray ? ” I passed him the coin, and watched
him down the street towards the back of the
corner hotel. A capricious fate, at that moment,
happened to send a prosperous-looking old China-
man down that very street to that very spot where
stood that very wreck, with the memory of the
old Chow’s perfidy rankling in his unworthy
bosom. The ill-fated Asiatic went down at the
derelict’s first hit, and I and several others were
only just in time to save him from utter demoli-
tion. Subsequent police-court proceedings gained
the wreck six months’ gaol, but they also revealed
the astonishing fact that he had, after many
years, revenged himself on the author of his ruin.
He couldn’t remember his Chinaman’s face, and
didn’t know him from Adam. Out of 300,000,000
Mongols he plugged one at a venture, and plugged
the right one.

SO

To My Soul.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Be patient, Omy Soul; the prison bars
That check thy flight
Will break beneath the sun, or silent stars,
Some day or night.
Be still and wait; the Body seems to reign
In pride serene;
But darkly in its pathway crouches Pain,
With poniard keen. <
Grieve not when it is grieved, nor, when it errs—
’Tis naught to thee ;
Its sins and sorrows are but ministers
To set thee free.
Behold, it is a bondslave to the Earth
From which it springs ;
Its laugh is loudest in the Masque of Mirth—
It loves all things,
That make the world seem beautiful and gay,
But live not long—
The joy of springtime and the dawn of day,
Wine, Women, Song.
Red-tongued it rushes, like a hound unchained,
To hunt Desire;
But thou remainest still a proud, unstained
Spirit of fire.
It has no part in thee ; thou hast no mate
To share thy throne.
Thou art invincible, inviolate,
White and alone.
Dost thou not feel in rapt imaginings,
In dreams sublime,
The sovran sweep of thy immortal wings
Through Space and Time ?
The stars and suns whose magnitudes appal
Shall seem to thee
Like twinkling lights of some small port of call
Seen far at sea.
Be still and wait, O caged Immortal Bird !
Thou shalt be free ;
Not all in vain hast thou the voices heard
Of lives to be.
Be still and wait ! No Being that draws breath
Thy bounds can set;
Though God Himself forget thee, Faithful Death
Will not forget.
VICTOR J. DALEY.
Concerning Fires.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Very few people believe it possible to produce fire
by the friction of two sticks; yet fires were
burning in thousands of aboriginal camps when
the first white man landed in Australia. How
were they lighted ? The method, as described to
me by an old blackfellow, consisted in twirling a
hard pointed stick in a shallow hole in a parti-
cular wood, the hole being filled with dry powdered
bark. The stick was held upright between the
palms, and twirled rapidly by rubbing the palms
together. A gin knelt before the operator and
blew gently on the powdered stuff the moment it
began to smoke. It sometimes tired out a dozen
male operators before the fire came, but the one
gin waited to blow all the time.
A waggon wheel, turning on a dry axle, will set
it ablaze, and even weld the axle to the box. It
takes a tremendous heat to do that, and such heat
has been generated by friction. However, fire-
making by friction is a forgotten art, and had
probably been disused long ere the landing of
Captain Cook, for the blacks never travelled
without firesticks. Even to-day the fire-stick is
carried, swung gently in the hand to keep it
alight. Nor is the custom confined to the
aborigines. Many a hunter is seen on the .track
swinging his firestick from camp to camp. When
he sits down for a spell, he puts a few twigs on it
to keep it burning.
In the early days, when the blacks were bad,
diggers and others, striking across country to new
fields, left their fires at sundown, and went on for
two or three miles before camping for the night.
The blacks, attracted by the blaze, would often
gather round the abandoned fires, and throw in a
shower of spears.
The tramp makes at least two fires a day—one
at noon and one at sundown. The first—the
“ billy fire is a midget, to boil a drop of water
for tea. Even this is methodically built. He
places a stick the thickness of the forearm on the
ground, leans a few lighter pieces on it the width
of the billy apart; then between, and against the
back piece, he places a handful of dry leaves,
ferns, grass, or shredded bark, with twigs and
sticks on top to light it. The billy is planted
against it, and the fire fed with light wood until
the water boils. The “johnny-cake fire ” requires
a good armful of picked wood—iron-bark, box, or
gidgeafor preference—which is burning while the
dough is being worked up. The unburnt sticks
are then put aside, the coals levelled by stamping
them lightly, and the johnnies dropped on. A
blaze of good red coals is kept going at one side
where the johnnies, as they stiffen, are stood to
toast. The “ damper fire ” should burn all night,
so as to leave a heap of clean, hot ashes—not coals!
A few coals are raked over the top to keep the
heat in, but there must be enough ashes between
to keep them from burning the damper.
In a “working” camp a round excavation is
made for the oven to concentrate the heat and
shield it from the wind. Game is roasted in these
holes by travellers without an oven. A bird is
placed on a piece of doubled wire a few inches
above the coals, and a fire is made on a sheet of
tin laid over the top. Another way is to wrap
the dressed bird in a sheet of greased brown paper
and roast in the ashes. Cakes, eggs, chops
steaks, &c., can be satisfactorily cooked in this
camp oven.
At sundown, the bushman’s camp-fire is lit—the
most important of all, the fire that denotes home
For this he has a large back-log, and sees that
theres sufficient “big wood” on hand for the
night. In summer, he’ll be satisfied if the log
“keeps in,” and makes coals for morning. To
keep a fire in over-night, it is covered with
ashes. Glowing red coals will be found under
them in the morning, and it is only necessary to
throw on a few dry sticks to set the fire going.
Ihe best woods'for fire-purposes are box, coolabah,
grey gum, mulga, gidgea, forest oak, cypress pine,
ironbark and blackbutt. On a summer’s night*
mosquito fires are necessary adjuncts. They are
lit at intervals with cowdung, corkwood, green
bark and leaves.
In winter time the single fire is not a luxury.
Lying beside it you get half-roasted on one side,
while the other side is freezing. A fire at each
side is better. Those who are troubled with cold
feet light a third to keep them warm. Under such
circumstances it does not do to be fidgetty in one's
sleep. Many a one has been wakened by a blaze
a H 3 ?’ng his blankets, and I saw a man kick one
night till he got right down into his foot fire, and
but for a timely billy of cold tea would have been
incapacitated from walking for a week or two.
A man should know his “sleeping character”
before trusting himself among three fires. An ex-
horse-trainer, tramping out-back, was addicted to
steeplechase riding in his sleep. The nights were
cold, and he had to supplement his “feet-fire”
with side-warmers. At first he put two stakes at
each side of him to keep himself in ; but he
rode his dream-nags over these, and came a
cropper in the tire. So he had to be content with
one warmer, and to keep out of that he tethered
himself to a tree on the opposite side. He always
travelled alone. Any mate he picked up, as soon
as bed-time came, would pack and leave hur-
riedly, with the observation that, “A feller wot
ties himself up at night must be a bit cronk
m the upper storey.”
The blackfellow’s fire is the best. It is very
small, permitting him to lie close to it all night,
and to enjoy an even temperature. “White
pfeller big fool,” says Murri. “Him make um
big fire—can’t get close. By’n’bye fire go down
an white man catch um cold. No sense ’bout
dat.
Many travellers have a fatal habit of lighting
fires against the butt of a tree. No one can judge
how long a tree will take to burn down. A small
tree may burn for days, and a big one fall in a
few hours. I once saw a burning tree rain streams
of honey and melted wax. It was a small dead
ironUrk, in a projecting limb of which there was
a bv. o nest. As the fire roared up the hollow
1 1 link and burnt into the limb, the bees were
driven out, and tlte honey began to pour down.
We caught a lot in a billy, but it was so full of
melted wax, bee.-bread, and burnt bees as to be
unusable.
Another dangerous fire is the one built against
a hollow log. Apart from the danger of setting
the bush on fire, all manner of horrible things
come crawling out as the fire eats its way into the
hollows. A Barwon native, who dearly loved his
log-fire, used to surround himself with newspaper,
pinned to the ground. Snakes, Scorpions, and
centipedes make a great noise crawling over or
under dry paper; and, being a light sleeper, he
was always warned when danger approached. In
ant country he made a little trench round his nap-
ground, averring that no ant would cross it.
Bushmen are generally careful with fires; out
there are many whose carelessness has caused
enormous damage and loss of life. A few smoul-
dering embers are left by the track side, a wind
fans them up, carries a spark into the dry grass
and the result is a disastrous bush fire. The cul-
prit is frequently a new-chum, or an immigrant
from the Paroo country, where bush fires are
unknown. There is seldom any grass there to
carry a fire, and a sojourn in such a place makes a
man careless. Glass bottles, lying in dry grass,
with a hot sun shining upon them, have started
many a bushfire for which innocent swagmen have
been blamed. Note that bushfires are always
plentiful on very hot days.
, South Australians are the most careful people
in this respect—perhaps because the law is more
rigorous there than elsewhere. There, the wax
match is tabooed, and anybody seen carrying a
firestick would be chased as a lunatic, or arrested
by the nearest policeman for imperilling life and
property. Nearly everybody uses safety matches
—made in Germany—-and no one is allowed to
smoke an uncovered pipe in the august presence
of a wheatfield.
Anyone who has crossed the One-tree Plain, or
travelled on the Darling Downs, knows what
economical firing is. There he is fortunate if he
has the posts of a wire fence from which to break
splinters and little bits of bark. Failing this, he
boils Ins billy with tufts of grass and the bones of
a dea,d sheep. There is generally a strong wind
blowing; and the best way to light up is to face
the wind, holding the match low down and
striking it into the kindling material.
Lighting a fire in wet weather, when the ground
is soaked, and wood, leaves, and everything else is
sopping, is an art in itself. If there are any iron-
bark trees, blackbutts, or woollybutts about, the
task will be easy. Under the wet exterior there
are layers of dry, crumpled, highly-combustible
bark. Dry bits of bark may be found on one side
of gums and other trees; a few dry leaves and
twigs in hollow logs ; a bit of greased rag may be
procurable, and, as a last resource, take a bit of
th ® bagging with which your coat or vest is
padded. Splintered pine and mulga twigs are the
quickest fire-lighters m the bush.
A small fire in the tent, alongside the flap, is
cosy on a cold or wet night. A heap of short
sticks close by, a slush-lamp or a penny candle at
your head, two or three late papers to read, your
dog coiled against the coals, your pipe in good
going order—and not much is wanted to complete
your happiness.
Lighting a fire with the last match is a serious
task. No matter how careful you are. something
is sure to go wrong. There is bound to be a strong
breeze blowing, without intermissions; or else a
whiff springs up just as you strike. Then it may
°«T y a and s . moulder away, or the head will fly
All creation seems to be against that one
match. When you have a box full you can strike
them as off-bandedly as you like, even in a strong
wind, and you have to blow hard in addition to
put the match out. But what a little puff puts
the last one out! It snuffs out for no reason
whatever. You have your leaves, grass, bark, and
twigs all nicely and carefully nested ; you get
down on your knees, hold the bottom of the box
close to the nest, grip the match close to the head
with thumb and fingers, and strike gently off the
box into the finest grass—and 10 to 1 you’ll bump
it against something and put it out.
You can then sit down and meditate on the
dullness and dreariness that broods over every-
thing, and realise what a comforting friend and
companion is the camp-fire.
EDWARD S. SORENSON.

Mrs. Marian Pascoe, of Christ-
church, New Zealand, sends a photo-
graph of herself and little boy and
says:
“I had indigestion very badly.
My food rested heavy on my stom-
ach. I lost my appetite and rapidly
grew weak. My blood became im-
pure and I had almost constant
headache. I was so nervous I hardly
knew what to do. A friend sug-
gested that I try Ayer’s Sarsapa-
rilla, which I did, and only a few
bottles made me strong and hearty
again. My little boy began to .com-
plain, said he was all tired out,
could not play, nor do anything.
He lost his appetite, and grew weak
and languid. I tried the Sarsapa-
rilla on him also, and he was soon
perfectly well.”
“The World’s Greatest Family Medicine.”
When you lose your appetite, when your food distresses you, and when
your nerves are weak, you may know that severe disease is not far away.
